Mishneh Torah, Sacrificial Procedure



Chapter 1

All of the sacrifices of living animals comes from five species alone: a) cattle, b) sheep, c) goats, d) turtle doves, and e) small doves.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:2 with regard to the differences between these species. In this chapter, the Rambam outlines the different types of animal sacrifices and which animals are offered for each one.</i>
All of the sacrifices - whether those brought by the community or by individuals - are of four types: a) burnt-offerings, b) sin-offerings, c) guilt-offerings, and d) peace-offerings.
There are also three other types of individual sacrifices: a) the Paschal sacrifice, b) the sacrifice of the firstborn,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice of the firstborn of one's cattle or flocks.</i> and d) the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the tithing of one's cattle or flocks.</i> sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah, in the introduction to the order of <i>Kodshim</i>, the Rambam speaks of "peace-offerings and [offerings] that resemble peace-offerings." He later explains that the term "[offerings] that resemble peace-offerings" refers to the three types of sacrifices mentioned here, because they resemble peace-offerings." He elaborates on that theme in his commentary to <i>Zevachim</i> 5:5, explaining that the procedure in which these three sacrifices are offered resembles that of the peace-offerings with only minor differences. For that reason and because they are offerings which a person is required to bring only on specific and infrequent occasions, he does not consider them as a separate category of sacrifices.</i>
All of the communal offerings are burnt-offerings or sin-offerings. There are no communal peace-offerings except the two sheep offered with the bread that is waved on Shavuot.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the two loaves offered that day. The Rambam is borrowing the wording of Leviticus 23:17.</i> They are called "the sacrifices of communal peace offerings." The community never offers a guilt offering, nor a fowl.
The communal offerings are the two <i>temidim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Literally "continuous offerings."</i> offered every day,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 1:1-3.</i> the additional offerings of the Sabbaths,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 4:9.</i> <i>Rashei Chodashim</i>, and the festivals,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i>, chs. 7-10.</i> and the sin-offering of a goat brought on Yom Kippur.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 10:2.</i> Similarly, if [the High] Court<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sanhedrin</i> of 71 judges who would hold their sessions in the Chamber of Hewn Stone.</i> inadvertently gave an erroneous ruling with regard to the worship of false deities, every tribe is required to bring a bull and a goat. The bull is brought as a burnt-offering and the goat as a sin-offering. These goats are called "the goats of false deities." If they inadvertently gave an erroneous ruling with regard to other mitzvot, they bring a bull as a sin-offering. It is called "the bull associated with the overlooking of a matter by the community."<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these sacrifices are described in <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 12:1.</i>
The sacrifices [brought by] individuals include: a) the firstborn offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bechorot</i>, ch. 1.</i><br>b) the tithe offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bechorot</i>, ch. 6.</i><br>c) the Paschal offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i>, ch. 1.</i><br>d) the <i>chagigah</i> offerings, i.e., the peace offerings [brought in association with the pilgrimage festivals],<br>e) the pilgrimage offering which is a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these sacrifices are described in <i>Hilchot Chagigah</i>, ch. 1.</i><br>f) the sacrifice brought by a convert, that involves a burnt-offering from a domesticated animal, two small doves or two turtle doves; both of them are burnt-offerings, or two domesticated animals, one as a burnt-offering and one as a peace-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i>, ch. 1.</i><br>g) one who vows<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">A person vows to bring either a burnt-offering or a peace-offering.</i> or pledges<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A person pledges to bring a particular animal as a burnt-offering or a peace-offering.</i> a burnt-offering or a peace offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 14 of these halachot (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i>) which describes these sacrifices.</i><br>h) peace-offerings that are accompanied by bread; they are called thanksgiving offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 5, and onward which describe this sacrifice.</i><br>i) the sacrifices of a nazirite,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">One who took a vow not to drink wine.</i> which are a burnt-offering, a sin-offering, and a peace-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i>, ch. 8.</i><br>j) the sacrifices of a <i>metzora,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">A person afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> a skin ailment, similar, but not identical, to leprosy.</i> which are a sin-offering, and a guilt-offering, and a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kaparah</i>, ch. 4.</i><br>k) the sacrifices of a <i>zav</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A male who has emissions from his sexual organ similar to those resulting from a gonorrheal infection.</i> a <i>zavah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who experiences vaginal bleeding outside her menstrual cycle.</i> and a women after childbirth; they are a sin-offering and a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kaparah</i> 1:3.</i><br>l) the sacrifice brought by a person who inadvertently violated a negative commandment punishable by <i>karet</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Premature death at the hand of heaven and the soul being cut off in the World to Come.</i> it is a sin-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i>, ch. 1.</i><br>m) if a person was unsure of whether he transgressed or not, that transgressor brings a guilt-offering; it is called a conditional guilt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9 which describes both these types of sacrifices.</i><br>n) there are certain sins<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">When he engaged in relations with a maid-servant who was married, robbed, made mundane use of consecrated property. Similarly, a nazirite who became impure and a person afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> must bring this offering.</i> for which one brings a guilt-offering [to atone for their transgression]; this is called a definite guilt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9 which describes both these types of sacrifices.</i><br>o) similarly, the ram brought as a burnt-offering and the bull the High Priest brings from his own resources as a sin-offering on Yom Kippur,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i>, ch. 4, which describes the offering of these sacrifices.</i> are individual offerings; the bull is called "the bull of Yom Kippur."<br>All of these sacrifices are explicitly mentioned in the Torah and the laws governing each of them are explained in the appropriate places.
[The person bringing] any of the individual offerings is responsible for them and for their accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the animal he designated as a sacrifice dies or the meal or wine offering designated for the sacrifices was spoiled, he must bring another one in its place.</i> with the exception of an animal pledged as sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained in ch. 14, in that instance, the person is not vowing to bring a sacrifice, he designated a particular animal as a sacrifice. Hence if that animal is lost, he is not obligated to replace it.</i> [The community at large] is not responsible for sacrifices or their accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">We are speaking about sacrifices that are required to be brought at a specific time. If for some reason, the community was unable to bring them at that time, they are not required to bring them afterwards. As <i>Berachot</i> 26a states: "If the time passes, the sacrifice is nullified."</i> If a sacrifice was offered, they are responsible for its additional offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For these additional offerings may be brought even at a later date, as will be explained in Chapter 2, Halachah 12.</i> When an individual's sacrifice was required to be offered at a fixed time,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Like the Paschal or festive offerings.</i> it is like a communal offering and the person is not responsible for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, since the time passed, the sacrifice cannot be brought. In truth, the matter is not at all dependent on whether an individual's sacrifice or a communal offering is involved. The determining factor is whether there is a specific time associated with the sacrifice or not (Radbaz).</i>
All of the animals brought as burnt-offerings must only be male.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 22:18-19 speaks of bringing the three species mentioned as burnt offerings and specifies that they must be male.</i> It may be brought from sheep, goats, or cattle, whether large or small.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Provided they are eight days old, as explained in Halachah 11.</i> They may also be brought from turtle doves and small doves, whether male or female.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah does not make any specification with regard to fowl.</i>
A sin-offering may be brought from these five species, from both males and females, from small ones and large ones.
A guilt-offering may be brought only from male sheep. There are guilt-offerings that come from large members of this species<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Those brought for the sins mentioned in note 32.</i> and others which come from small members of this species.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The guilt offering brought by a nazirite.</i>
Peace offerings may be brought from sheep, goats, or cattle, from males and females, whether large or small. A fowl may not be brought as a peace offering.<br>[When describing an animal as] small, [the intent] is one between the eighth day<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Before eight days, it is forbidden to bring an animal as a sacrifice, as stated in <i>Hilchot Isssurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:4.</i> and a full year, from day to day. If the year was declared a leap year, [the extra month is included]. "Large" implies until three full years from day to day for cattle, for flocks,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Both sheep and goats.</i> until two full years from day to day. From this age onward, [the animal] is considered as "old" and it should not be brought as an offering.
Although all of the sacrifices are acceptable if they are brought from the eighth day onward, as an initial preference, we do not bring [an animal] as a sacrifice until it is 30 days old or older<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If it was brought earlier, it is acceptable after the fact (Radbaz).</i> with the exception of a firstborn offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since Exodus 22:29 explicitly states that a firstborn animal may be offered on its eighth day of life, our Sages did not desire to place any restrictions on the offering. It may be offered on the eighth day even as an initial preference.</i> a Paschal offering, and a tithe offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The tithe offering and the Paschal sacrifice are, like the firstborn offering, sacrifices of lesser sanctity and the laws governing the pouring of their blood on the altar are the same. Hence, our Sages considered them the same in this instance as well [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Parah</i> 1:4)].<br>The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> note that the mishnah cited also states that the same laws apply to offerings that were vowed or pledged and question why the Rambam does not mention them in this halachah. The Radbaz notes that the Rambam does not mention pledges and vows in his Commentary to the Mishnah and maintains that he possessed a different version of the mishnah.</i> If one desires to offer these sacrifices from the eighth day onward, he may.
Hours are counted with regard to consecrated animals,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, years are counted from day to day and not from hour to hour. For example, with regard to a child becoming Bar Mitzvah at age 13, if he was born at 3 PM on the sixteenth of Elul, he reaches Bar Mitzvah at nightfall on the sixteenth of Elul. He need not wait until 3 in the afternoon. With regard to sacrifices, however, <i>Zevachim</i> 18b uses Biblical exegesis to teach that we count from the hour of an animal's birth.</i> i.e., if their [lives] were an hour longer<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam describes in the following clause.</i> or an hour was subtracted from their [lives],<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to an animal like a ram that is not acceptable for sacrifice until it is a year and 30 days old. It is not acceptable until the hour of its birth passes on the thirty-first day.</i> they are unacceptable.<br>What is implied? When it is required that a sacrifice be less than a year old, if an hour was added to its year, it is invalidated. Even if it was merely a year old when it was slaughtered and additional time was added before its blood was sprinkled [on the altar], it is invalidated. It must be less than a year until the time the blood is sprinkled [on the altar]. Similar [laws] apply with regard to all the sacrifices.
Whenever the Torah uses the expressions, "a male sheep," "a female sheep," "sheep," the intent is [an animal] in its first year [of life]. "A ram" or "rams"<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The term, <i>ayil</i>, Hebrew for ram, is identified with strength. Implied is that the animal must reach a stage of maturity that endows it with strength and power.</i> implies males in their second year [of life]. When is an animal called a ram? When 31 days of its second year of life pass. On the thirtieth day, however, it is not acceptable, neither as a sheep, nor as a ram. [At this stage,] it is called a <i>pilgas</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is a Greek term. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Parah</i> 1:3), the Rambam explains that the term carries an allusion to the Hebrew phrase <i>peleg gas</i>, implying that it has passed its limits exceedingly. Similarly, this animal is in an intermediate state, having left one category, but not entered another.</i><br>Whenever the expression "a calf" is used, the intent is [an animal] in its first year [of life]. The term "bull" implies that the animal is in its second year of life. The term "a goat kid" implies that it is in its first year, "a goat," that it is in its second year. Throughout the second year, it is called a goat.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps the Rambam's intent is that there is no concept of a <i>pilgas</i> with regard to goats.</i>
All of the communal offerings are male. All<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on early printing and authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text has a slightly different version.</i> of the communal sin offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Brought on Rosh Chodesh or the festivals and those mentioned in the following halachot.</i> come from goats or from cattle, none are brought from sheep. All of the communal burnt offerings are from sheep or cattle; there are no burnt-offerings brought from goats.<br>All of the sin-offerings brought by individuals are female. They may be eaten by the priests and they do not come from cattle except three: a) the sin-offering of a <i>nasi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a king as stated in <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 15:6.</i> which is a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as opposed to ordinary sin-offerings which can be either a goat or a sheep, the prince's offering must be a goat and it must be male in contrast to those offerings which are female.</i> and is eaten, b) the sin-offering of the anointed priest which is a bull,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">I.e. the High Priest, but only one who was anointed. If he assumed his office through wearing the garments of the High Priest (as did the High Priests of the Second Temple), he is not required to bring this sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Shegagot, loc. cit.</i>).</i> which is burnt;<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and not eaten.</i> it is called "the bull that comes because of [the violation of] any mitzvah;" c) the bull brought by the High Priest on Yom Kippur;<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:1; 4:1.</i> it is a sin-offering that is burnt.
All of the communal sin-offerings are eaten with the exception of the goat offered on Yom Kippur whose partner is sent [to Azazel],<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:1; 4:1.</i> the goats brought [because of the violation<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">By people at large due to a erroneous ruling by the High Court.</i> of the prohibition against] idol worship,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 12:1.</i> and the bull [brought because of the violation of] a law forgotten [by the High Court].<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">By people at large due to a erroneous ruling by the High Court.</i> "The bull that comes because of [the violation of] any mitzvah"<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e. the High Priest, but only one who was anointed. If he assumed his office through wearing the garments of the High Priest (as did the High Priests of the Second Temple), he is not required to bring this sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Shegagot, loc. cit.</i>).</i> and the bull [brought because of] a law forgotten [by the High Court] are called "the bulls that are burnt." The goats brought [because of the violation of the prohibition against] idol worship are called "the goats that are burnt."<br>Thus there are five sin-offerings that are burnt: two are individual offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The two bulls brought by the High Priest mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> and three are communal offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The bulls and the goats brought because of transgressions performed due to a erroneous ruling by the High Court and the goat offered as a sin offered on Yom Kippur [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 12:5)]. Significantly, in his Commentary to <i>Parah</i> 8:3, the Rambam does not mention the goat offered on Yom Kippur among the goats that are burnt.</i>
All of these offerings are called <i>zevachim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that this appellation is found in <i>Zevachim</i> 47a. He questions the reason for the Rambam's inclusion of this point and explains that it can resolve a question that might arise if a person made a particular pledge.</i> All of the burnt-offerings, sin-offerings, guilt-offerings, and the two sheep brought as peace-offerings on Shavuos are called "sacrifices of the highest order of sanctity."<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachot 2-3.</i> Peace offerings brought by an individual, the firstborn offerings, the tithe offerings, and the Paschal offerings, are called "sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity."<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 4.</i>
The limbs and organs that are burnt on the altar from the sin-offerings that are eaten, from the guilt-offerings, and from the peace-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not mention the burnt offering, because then the entire animal is burnt.</i> are called <i>eimorim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (Introduction to the Order of <i>Kodshim</i>), the Rambam explains that this term is derived from the term <i>emar</i>, "spoke," i.e., these are the organs spoken about, i.e., specified by God to be burnt.</i> These are the <i>eimorim</i> of an ox or a goat: the fat that is on the inner organs,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The Biblical term <i>kerev</i> (Leviticus 3:3, <i>et al</i>) refers to the inner digestive organs. That verse speaks of "the fat that covers the inner organs" and "all the fat on the inner organs." The first term refers to a large membrane that covers all of these organs like a sack. The second term refers to the fat on the organs themselves.</i> included with that is the fat that is on the maw,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one of the animals four stomachs. Since the verse uses the term <i>kol</i>, "all," our Sages understood that additional fat was to be brought.</i> the two kidneys and the fat that is on them, together with the fat that is on the flanks, the lobe of the liver, and a small portion of the liver should also be taken with its lobe.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">These are also mentioned explicitly in Leviticus 3:10, <i>et al</i>.</i> If the sacrifice is from sheep, he should add to these the fat tail<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The tails of goat and cattle do not collect fat. Hence the term <i>alyah</i>, "fat tail" is not appropriate with regard to them and their tails are not offered (Radbaz).</i> in its entirety<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 3:9 speaks of <i>haalyah temimah</i>. In this context, <i>temimah</i> means "entire."</i> together with the vertebrae from the spine until the place of the kidneys, as [Levitcus 3:9] states: "he shall remove it opposite the kidneys." All of the <i>eimorim</i> are burnt on the outer altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The inner altar is used only for incense and the blood sprinkled on it on Yom Kippur. See <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 2:11.</i>
If a [sacrificial] animal was pregnant, even though the fetus had been carried for a full term, and even if it was discovered to be alive [after the mother was slaughtered],<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, its fat is forbidden to be eaten, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 7:3.</i> its fat should not be offered together with the fat of its mother. Instead, only the fat of the mother is offered. The fetus is considered as one of its limbs.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">We do not consider it as if two animals were offered as this sacrifice and the <i>eimorim</i> from each must be offered on the altar. Instead, only the mother is considered as the sacrifice and the fetus is considered as an appendage to it. If the sacrifice is a sin offering, it is given to the priests to partake of. If it is a peace offering, it is given to the owner. Females are not sacrificed as guilt offerings.</i>

Chapter 2

The wine and the fine flour that are brought together with an offering are called accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>nesachim</i> is used continually throughout the Torah, e.g., Numbers, ch. 15. Literally, it means "libations." We have not used that term, because it is not appropriate with regard to the meal offerings. In his introduction to the tractate of <i>Menachot</i>, the Rambam states that the Torah uses the term in a general sense without attention to its particular meaning.<br>Since every sacrifice is accompanied by such offerings, before delineating the details of the sacrifices, the Rambam describes these accompanying offerings.</i> The fine flour alone is called the accompanying meal-offering. The accompanying meal-offering need not be waved, brought [to the corner of the altar], nor [offered with] frankincense.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 12, Halachot 6-7, all of the meal offerings brought independently must be brought to the corner of the altar and frankincense must be offered with them. Some also must be waved.</i> It requires salt<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For Leviticus 2:13 states that salt must be brought on all sacrifices.</i> and it is burnt on the outer altar in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, there are other meal offerings which are fit to be eaten.</i><br>The wine is poured over the altar. It is not poured over the fire. Instead, the priest should lift up his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that <i>Sukkah</i> 48b gives that instruction with regard to the water libation brought on Sukkot. Nevertheless, that text questions why this instruction is mentioned here with regard to the wine libation, for seemingly, there would be no reason to do so.</i> and pour it on [the altar's] base<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad notes that <i>Sukkah</i> 48a,b speaks of two cups on the southwest corner of the altar, i.e., on the upper level around which the priests would walk. The wine and water libations would be poured into these cups and they would extend to the <i>shittin</i>. The Radbaz notes that the Rambam himself (<i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 6:5; see also Chapter 7, Halachah 11) speaks of bringing the wine to the top of the altar. Hence, he maintains that the Rambam agrees with the Ra'avad concerning this issue. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> differs and maintains that these cups were only used during Sukkot. Otherwise, the wine was poured on the altar's base and from there, it flowed into the <i>shittin</i>.</i> and from there, it descends to the <i>shittin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:11 which explains that these were two cavities in the southwest corner of the altar.</i>
Accompanying offerings are required only for an animal brought as a burnt-offering or as a peace-offering. Whether it was a communal sacrifice, an individual sacrifice, a sheep brought by a woman who gave birth, or the ram brought by the High Priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">These two offerings are singled out because they are individual burnt-offerings that are not dependent on an individual's pledge or vow. <i>Menachot</i> 91b cites an explicit verse that teaches that accompanying offerings are required for these sacrifices.</i> since it was a burnt-offering, it requires the accompanying offerings. Accompanying offerings are not brought for [sacrifices of] fowl,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Even those brought as burnt offerings.</i> guilt-offerings, sin-offerings, with the exception of the sin-offering and guilt-offering of a person who had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i>, for those accompanying offerings are explicitly mentioned in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 91a derives the need for such accompanying offerings from a juxtaposing Leviticus 14:10 and Numbers 15:5. In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that accompanying offerings are brought for these sacrifices, because they do not come because of a sin.</i>
What is the source that teaches that accompanying offerings should not be brought together with sin-offerings and guilt-offerings? [When speaking about the accompanying offerings, Numbers 15:3] states: "to utter a vow or pledge," [implying that these offerings are necessary only for sacrifices] brought because of a vow or pledge. [This] excludes a sin-offering, guilt-offering, firstborn offering, tithe offering, and Paschal sacrifice. Since [these offerings] are not brought because of a vow or a pledge, they do not require accompanying offerings.<br>Which is the source that teaches to include the festive peace-offerings and the pilgrimage burnt-offerings?<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Which are obligations and not dependent on a person's volition.</i> [The above verse] states: "Or on your festivals."
What is the measure of the accompanying offerings? The accompanying offering for a male or female sheep is an <i>isaron</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>isaron</i> is equivalent to the size of 43.2 eggs. In modern measure, the size of an egg is 57.6 cc according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 99.5 cc according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> of fine flour mixed with a quarter of a <i>hin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 7, a <i>hin</i> is equivalent to twelve <i>log</i>. Each <i>log</i> comprises four <i>revi'iot</i>. In modern measure, a <i>revi'it</i> is 86 cc according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and approximately 150 cc according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>. Thus a <i>hin</i> is 48 times this amount.</i> of oil and a quarter of a <i>hin</i> of wine as a wine libation. These are also [the accompanying offerings] for a goat whether small<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In its first year of life, as stated in Chapter 1, Halachah 11.</i> or large<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In its second year of life (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and whether male or female and for a ewe, even if she is large. The accompanying offerings of a ram, however, are two <i>esronim</i> mixed with a third of a <i>hin</i> of oil and a third of a <i>hin</i> of wine as a libation. The accompanying offerings of a cow or a calf, whether male or female, are three <i>esronim</i> mixed with a half of a <i>hin</i> of oil and a half of a <i>hin</i> of wine as a libation.
These measures are applicable for the accompanying offerings for burnt-offerings and peace-offerings for each animal offered,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if a person pledged to bring several sacrifices of a given type, he must bring the required accompanying offerings for each animal.</i> as [implied by <i>ibid.</i>:12]: "According to the number that you offer, so shall you do for [each] one according to their number." We may not increase these measures, nor may we decrease them. [Indeed,] if one increases or decreases [the measure by] even the slightest amount, one disqualifies [the accompanying offering]. The only exception is the sheep offered as a burnt offering on the day of the waving of the <i>omer</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:3 where this sacrifice is mentioned.</i> The accompanying offering for it is two <i>esronim</i> mixed with a third of a <i>hin</i> of oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that only a quarter of a <i>hin</i> of oil should be used. The Radbaz notes that <i>Menachot</i> 89b appears to support the Ra'avad's position. Although he explains that the Rambam's position could be justified, he admits that it is somewhat difficult. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> also suggests that a printing error crept into the text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i> Although the measure of flour was doubled,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, only one <i>isaron</i> of flour was brought for a burnt-offering of a sheep, as stated in the previous halachah.</i> the measure of wine was not and [only] a quarter of a <i>hin</i> is brought as a libation.
A person who had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> must bring three <i>esronim</i> of flour [as accompanying offerings] for the three sheep which he brings as a sin-offering, a guilt-offering, and a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:3. Bringing accompanying offerings for these sacrifices is an exception to the general rule, as stated in Halachah 2. The obligation to bring these three <i>esronim</i> is explicitly stated in Leviticus 14:10. The Oral Tradition (<i>Menachot</i> 91a) teaches that each one is designated for a different sacrifice.</i> According to the Oral Tradition, we learned that they come because of the sacrifices he brings,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This version is found in some of the authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and is also suggested by the <i>Or Sameach</i>. The standard published text follows a slightly different version.</i> an <i>isaron</i> for each sheep mixed with a quarter of a <i>hin</i> of oil. A <i>revi'it</i> of wine should be brought with each of the <i>esronim</i> as is the rule for the accompanying offering for all sheep. When one brings a <i>pilgas</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A ram that is between one year and one year and a month old (Chapter 1, Halachah 14).</i> he should bring with it the accompanying offerings for a ram, [nevertheless,] it is not considered as if he brought [the appropriate] sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., regardless of whether he pledged a ram or a male sheep, he is not considered to have fulfilled his obligation by bringing a <i>pilgas</i>, for it is too old for the latter type sacrifice and not old enough for the former.</i>
A <i>hin</i> comprises twelve <i>log</i>. We have already stated the measure of a <i>log</i> together with other measures in <i>Hilchot Eruvin</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Eruvin</i> 1:13.</i> An <i>isaron</i> is an <i>omer</i> which is the measure [of dough from which] <i>challah</i> must be separated. We have already explained this measure with regard to [the mitzvah of] <i>challah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i> 6:15.</i>
When the accompanying offerings or the meal offerings are measured, whether for an individual offering or a communal offering, they should not be measured in a measure of three <i>esronim</i> for a bull or two <i>esronim</i> for a ram. Instead, everything should be measured with the measure of one <i>isaron</i> that existed in the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 1:16 which describes the various measures that existed in the Temple.</i> Similarly, the oil for the accompanying offerings should be measured with the appropriate measure in the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As stated (<i>ibid.</i>:17), there were measures of a half a <i>hin</i>, a third of a <i>hin</i>, and a quarter of a <i>hin</i> in the Temple.</i> The oil for a meal offering brought by an individual is measured in the measure of a <i>log</i> that existed in the Temple;<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a person should not measure out the flour in a private measure he has outside the Temple. Instead, the measurement should be made with the Temple's measure (Radbaz).</i> the number of <i>lugim</i> [of oil] is determined by the number of <i>esronim</i> [of flour].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 7 and Chapter 13, Halachah 5.</i>
The overflow of the measures of flour are considered to be ordinary flour,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are not consecrated.</i> because the outer side of the <i>isaron</i> measure is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Flour, wine, and oil that come in contact with sacred utensils become consecrated. Nevertheless, only the inside of the dry measures were consecrated. The outside remained unconsecrated. Hence, the fact that the flour came in contact with it does not change its status. See <i>Hilchot Klei HaMikdash</i> 1:19.</i> The overflow of the wine and oil, by contrast, is consecrated, because it flows down the back of the utensil and both the inside and the outside of the liquid measures were anointed.<br>Why were the overflows consecrated even though the person measuring only intended to sanctify what was within the utensil?<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:20, an object placed in a sacred utensil does not become consecrated unless the person placing it there did so intentionally. Hence, even though the overflows came in contact with a sacred utensil, seemingly, there was no intent for them to become consecrated.</i> So that it would not be said that [substances] are used for ordinary purposes after having been in a sacred utensil.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a safeguard instituted by our Sages lest a mistaken impression be created.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that even though this rationale is advanced by <i>Menachot</i> 90a in support of a minority opinion, it would still be accepted by the majority.</i>
What would they do with the overflows? If there was another sacrifice, they would sacrifice them with it. [In that instance,] if they remained overnight,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there was another offering and it was thought they would be offered with it, but for some reason they were not and remained overnight.</i> they became disqualified accordingly.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For once they were placed in a sacred utensil, remaining overnight would disqualify them, as stated in Halachah 12.</i> If not, they were [redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to redeem them, because when consecrating them, there was the intent that they would be redeemed in such an eventuality (Radbaz).</i> and the proceeds] used to provide "dessert for the altar."<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:4), where he explains that the term <i>kayitz</i> refers to the time of the fig and grape harvest. These fruits are served as dessert, after a person has eaten his major meal. Similarly, these offerings do not represent the fundamental "food" of the altar, but instead, are offered only when the altar is free.</i>
What is implied? Burnt offerings are purchased [with the proceeds of] their [sale]. The meat is offered to God and the hides are given to the priests.
The flour mixed with oil<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The flour and the oil are, however, absolute requirements. One cannot be offered without the other (Radbaz).</i> of the accompanying offerings are not indispensable requirements preventing [the offering of] the wine libation, nor is the wine libation an indispensable requirement preventing their offering, nor are the accompanying offerings an indispensable requirement preventing the offering of the sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the person is required to bring both of these elements of the accompanying offering. Nevertheless, the offering of one is not dependent on the other as the Rambam continues to explain.</i> Instead, a person may bring his sacrifice one day and his accompanying offerings after ten days. [This applies to] both individual offerings and communal sacrifices, provided the accompanying offerings were not consecrated in a sacred utensil. If, however, they were consecrated in a sacred utensil, they are disqualified if they remain overnight.
The accompanying offerings may only be brought from ordinary produce. They may not be brought from <i>terumah</i>, the second tithes, or the first fruits.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">All of these have a certain dimension of holiness and are not considered as ordinary property. It is forbidden to use them for any purpose other than partaking of them in the ordinary manner. Hence they may not be used for these offerings.</i> Even for the thanksgiving offering whose bread may be brought from the [second] tithe, its accompanying offerings should only be brought from ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The bread from the thanksgiving offering may be eaten by an ordinary person. Hence, it is permitted for it to be brought from the second tithes. The accompanying offerings, as stated above, are offered on the altar entirely. Hence, they may not be brought from the second tithes (Radbaz).</i>
All of the measures of the accompanying offerings mentioned in the Book of Ezekiel,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">These differ greatly for the measures usually employed.</i> the listing of the sacrifices, and the order of service written there are all inauguration offerings and will not be practiced in generations to follow.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that since we are speaking about a directive for a specific time and not an ongoing practice, as a prophet, Ezekiel had the right to speak of offerings not prescribed by the Torah. This does not constitute a violation of the prohibition to add to the Torah's commandments. See <i>Hilchot Yesodei HaTorah</i>, ch. 9.</i> Instead, the prophet commanded and outlined how the inauguration offerings will be brought at the time of the dedication of the altar at the time of the coming of the King Mashiach when the Third Temple will be built.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">All authorities agree that Ezekiel's prophecy referred to special sacrifices and was not to be followed continuously. Rashi (<i>Menachot</i> 45b) interprets it as referring to the Second Temple, while the Rambam understands it as applying to the era of Mashiach.</i>
Just as the princes<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The leaders of the tribes as related in Numbers, ch. 7.</i> offered sacrifices at the dedication of the altar [of the Sanctuary in the desert], bringing offerings that were not brought in coming generations, and they brought them on the Sabbath, so too, a prince will bring a dedication offering on the Sabbath in the Ultimate Future, as stated explicitly there.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Ezekiel 46:4.</i> Similarly, the sacrifices which the people who returned [to Zion] from the [Babylonian] captivity in the days of Ezra,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Ezra 8:35.</i> were inaugural offerings and will not be practiced in generations to come. The practices to be followed in generations to come are the words of the Torah that we have explained as they were copied from Moses our teacher.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., from the Torah scrolls which he wrote.</i> We may not add to them<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 313) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 454) count the prohibition against adding to the Torah as one of its 613 mitzvot.</i> or subtract from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 314) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 455) count the prohibition against adding to the Torah as one of its 613 mitzvot.<br>The above is not merely a point of law, but a fundamental issue of Jewish faith. As the Rambam states in the ninth of his Thirteen Principles of Faith (Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Sanhedrin</i>, ch. 10) declaring:<br>The ninth principle is that the Torah of Moses will never be nullified.... There can be no additions to it, nor any deletions from it - neither in its text nor in its explanation. And thus we are commanded: "Do not add to it and do not detract it from it."<br>(Our translation is taken from the original manuscript versions of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah. The standard published text varies slightly.)</i>

Chapter 3

When two people desired to bring a peace-offering or a burnt-offering in partnership,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 9.</i> they may whether it was pledged or vowed.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 6, with regard to the distinction between these types of offerings.</i> Even a fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an entity of seemingly little value. One might think that bringing such an offering in partnership is not becoming to the altar. Even so, if one's intent is desirable, the offering is accepted (Radbaz, based on <i>Menachot</i> 110a).</i> may be brought in partnership.
Men, women, and, servants may bring all of these types of sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In this chapter, the Rambam outlines the types of individuals who may bring a sacrifice and the rite of <i>semichah</i> because that is an obligation on the person bringing the sacrifice.</i> From gentiles, by contrast, we accept only burnt offerings, as [derived from Leviticus 22:28]: "From the hand of an alien, you shall not offer the food of your God [from all of these]."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:1) notes that the prooftext states that it is forbidden to accept an offering from a gentile from a blemished animal. One can infer that if the animal is unblemished, the offering may be accepted.<br>The verse uses the term <i>lechem</i>, "food." That term is understood as referring only to a burnt-offering, as Numbers 28:2 states: "My food for my fires."And the burnt-offering is the only type of offering, consumed entirely by the fire of the altar (Radbaz).</i> Even a burnt offering of fowl may be accepted from a gentile,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Although the prooftext speaks only of animals, our Sages understood that the leniency applies to fowl as well (Radbaz).</i> even if he worships false deities.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 4, burnt offerings are not accepted from a Jew who worships false deities. Nevertheless, such restrictions are not placed upon gentiles.</i><br>We do not, however, accept peace-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">To offer them as peace-offerings. They are, however, brought as burnt-offerings, as stated in the following halachah.</i> meal-offerings, sin-offerings, or guilt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For the concept of atonement applies only with regard to the Jews' relationship with God.</i> from a gentile. Similarly, burnt-offerings that do not come as vows or pledges are not accepted from gentiles, e.g., a burnt-offering from a women who gave birth or the like or other burnt-offerings that do not come as vows or pledges.
When a gentile brings peace-offerings, they should be sacrificed as burnt-offerings, for [the intention of] a gentile's heart is [for the sake] of heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Menachot</i> 73b) interprets this as meaning that the gentile desires that his sacrifices be offered entirely to God and not have mortals partake of them.</i> If he gives them to a Jew with the intent that a Jew receive atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz notes that peace-offerings are not intended to bring atonement and explains that this is referring to an instance where a Jew vowed to bring a peace offering and the gentile offered to bring it for him. One might think that since the gentile is bringing them they would be offered as burnt-offerings. Hence it is necessary to explain that they are peace offerings.</i> the Jew may partake of them like the peace-offerings of the Jewish people. Similarly, if he gives them to a priest, the priest may partake of them.
When a Jew is an apostate who worships false deities or who desecrates the Sabbath in public,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> where the Rambam explains that the public desecration of the Sabbath is equivalent to idol worship, because they are both cornerstones of the Jewish faith. "Public" refers to a matter known about by ten people.<br>The Radbaz adds that we do not accept the sacrifices of a Jew who has abandoned Judaism and accepted a faith like Islam which does not involve idol worship. Such a person is included in the category (<i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 3:9) of an apostate with regard to the entire Torah.</i> we do not accept any sacrifices from him at all. Even a burnt-offering that is accepted from a gentile is not accepted from this apostate. [This is derived from Leviticus 1:2 which] states: "A man from you who will sacrifice." According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chulin</i> 5a-b.</i> we learned: "From you," i.e., not all of you, excluding an apostate.<br>If, however, one was a heretic with regard to other transgressions, any sacrifice [he brings] is accepted so that he will repent. If, however, he was a heretic with regard to a transgression and it is public knowledge that he commits it and he has become accustomed to doing so, a sacrifice [that he brings atoning for] that transgression - whether committed to anger [God]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he had two cuts of meat before him of equal quality, one kosher and one non-kosher and he ate the non-kosher one solely for the intent of angering God (<i>Gittin</i> 47a).</i> or out of desire<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, in <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 10:3, a distinction is not made with regard to the motivations for the transgression. There are other instances - see <i>Hilchot Teshuvah, loc. cit.</i>, <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim</i> 8;14, <i>Hilchot Gezeilah ViAveidah</i> 11:2, and <i>Hilchot Rotzeach</i> 4:10 - where the Rambam does make such a distinction.</i> -is not accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he frequently violates this transgression, we assume that he is not sincere in his desire for atonement for it, because a request for atonement must be accompanied by sincere regret. Sacrifices brought for other transgressions are, however, accepted from him. See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 3:7.</i> What is implied? If a person was accustomed to eat fat - whether committed to anger [God] or out of desire - and then he inadvertently partook of fat and brought a sin-offering [for this transgression], it is not accepted.
Gentiles do not bring accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">A meal offering and a wine libation.</i> for the burnt-offerings they bring, as [can be inferred from Numbers 15:13]: "Every native among you shall do this."<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This verse concludes the passage commanding the offering of the accompanying offers, implying that it is only a native - i.e., a member of the Jewish people - who is required to bring them.</i> The accompanying offerings for their sacrifices are, however, brought from communal funds,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Temple treasury.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i>:12] states: "So shall you do for each one according to their number."<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Implying that the sacrifices themselves require that the accompanying offerings be brought.</i> [These burnt offerings] do not require <i>semichah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Leaning on the animal with all one's strength, as the Rambam proceeds to explain in the concluding halachot of the chapter.</i> for <i>semichah</i> is performed only by a Jew, and by a male and not a female.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The prooftext for the obligation to perform <i>semichah</i> speaks of "the children of Israel," and uses the male form. <i>Menachot</i> 93b understands this to exclude gentiles and <i>Chagigah</i> 16b understands it as excluding women. See also Halachah 8.</i>
All of the offerings from domesticated animals that an individual brings<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This also includes offerings brought in partnership. The exclusion is only of communal offerings. See Halachah 10.</i>- whether those in which he is obligated or those promised through a vow - [require] <i>semichah</i> while they are alive with the exception of the firstborn offering, the tithe offering, and the Paschal sacrifice. [This is derived from Leviticus 3:2]: "And he shall lean his hand on the head of his offering." According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra</i> to the verse; <i>Menachot</i> 92b.</i> we learned that this refers to all the sacrifices with the exception of the firstborn offering, the tithe offering, and the Paschal sacrifice.
[A sacrifice of] a fowl does not require <i>semichah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Sifra</i> derives this concept from the exegesis of Leviticus 1:4: "And he will lean his hand on the head of the burnt-offering." "The" implies that there are some burnt-offerings to which this does not apply.</i><br>We already explained in <i>Hilchot Shekalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shekalim</i> 2:3; 3:14.</i> that the law is that all the money concerning which it was decided that it should be given to the chest for freewill offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Money that was found between the chest of the freewill offerings and the chest of the <i>shekalim</i> that was closer to the chest of the freewill offerings.</i> should be used to purchase burnt-offerings. The person to which this money belonged does not perform <i>semichah</i> on that animal, nor does he bring the accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money was placed in the chest for the freewill offering, it is no longer considered as his personal property, but as the property of the community. Hence, he is not the owner of the sacrifices and may not perform <i>semichah</i> on them. For that same reason, the community brings the accompanying offerings.</i> Instead, the accompanying offerings are brought from communal funds.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A meal offering and a wine libation.</i> Even if he was a priest, the service [of offering the sacrifice] and its hide belong to the men of the watch.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The priests designated to serve in the Temple that week. Even though the person whose money was used is a priest and he has the right to offer sacrifices that he brings (<i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 4:7), in this instance, he cannot demand the right to offer the sacrifice.</i>
All people may perform <i>semichah</i> with the exception of a deafmute, a mentally and/or emotionally unstable individual, a minor,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">These three individuals are not considered as responsible for their actions and are free of the responsibility for all mitzvot.</i> a servant, a woman,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5.</i> a blind man, and a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Gentiles are not obligated in any of the mitzvot. Hence the obligation of <i>semichah</i> does not apply to them.</i> An agent does not perform <i>semichah</i>, for <i>semichah</i> is performed only by the owners, as [implied by the prooftext]: "And he shall lean his hand." "His hand" and not the hand of his wife, his servant, or his agent.
When five individuals bring a sacrifice [in partnership], they all perform <i>semichah</i>, one after the other. They should not perform <i>semichah</i> at the same time. <sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this concept is derived from the fact that the prooftext uses the term "his hand" in the singular.</i><br>When a person died and left a sacrifice of a burnt offering or a peace offering, his heir should have it offered, perform <i>semichah</i> on it, and bring its accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For he is acting in the place of the original owner.</i>
<i>Semichah</i> is not performed with regard to communal offerings except with regard to two sacrifices: the goat sent to Azazel<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">During the Yom Kippur services. The High Priest performs <i>semichah</i> on it.</i> and the bull brought because of a law being forgotten.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In both these instances, there is an explicit verse (Leviticus 16:21; 4:15) requiring <i>semichah</i> for the sacrifice.</i> Three members of the <i>Sanhedrin</i> perform <i>semichah</i> on it. This is a law conveyed by Moses our teacher that <i>semichah</i> is not performed on communal offerings other than these two.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., were it not for that tradition, we might have derived the need for <i>semichah</i> for communal offerings from these two instances using Biblical exegesis [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 9:7)].</i>
<i>Semichah</i> is performed only in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 32b explains that just as the slaughter of the animal must be performed "before God," in the Temple Courtyard, so too, <i>semichah</i> must be performed "before God," in that same place.</i> If one performed <i>semichah</i> outside the Temple Courtyard, he should perform it again inside. If the one bringing the sacrifice was standing outside [the Temple Courtyard] and he extended his hands into [the Courtyard] and performed <i>semichah</i>, his <i>semichah</i> is valid,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This is indeed what is done when the atonement process of the people bringing the sacrifice has not been completed and they are not allowed to enter the Temple Courtyard until the sacrifice is offered.</i> provided he performed <i>semichah</i> with all his strength.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Halachah 13.</i><br>Only a person who is ritually pure may perform <i>semichah</i>. If a person who is ritually impure performed <i>semichah</i>, the <i>semichah</i> [is acceptable].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">After the fact.</i>
The animal should be slaughtered in the place where <i>semichah</i> is performed.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 93b derives this concept from the subsequent law: that slaughter must be performed directly after <i>semichah</i>. It thus follows that one must slaughter the animal in the same place where <i>semichah</i> was performed, for otherwise, this is not considered as directly afterwards.</i> The animal must be slaughtered directly after <i>semichah</i>. If one slaughtered it in a different place or waited [before slaughtering it], the slaughter is acceptable.<br><i>Semichah</i> is an incremental aspect of the mitzvah. Accordingly, if one did not perform <i>semichah</i>, the sacrifice [still] brings atonement; [the <i>semichah</i>] is not an indispensable requirement. Nevertheless, it is considered as if the sacrifice did not bring atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot Yesheinim, Yoma</i> 5a, explains that the intent is that although the person is not obligated to bring another sacrifice, in G‑d's eyes, his atonement is lacking.</i>
The person performing <i>semichah</i> must do so with all his power, [placing] both hands<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This is derived from Leviticus 16:21 which states such a requirement with regard to the goat sent to Azazel.</i> on the head of the animal, as [Leviticus 1:4] states: "on the head of the burnt-offering." [Implied is his hands must be placed on the head] and not on the [animal's] neck or the side of its face,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Its cheeks (Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 93b).</i> and that there should not be any intervening substance between his hands and the animal.
How is <i>semichah</i> performed? If the sacrifice was one of the offerings of the most sacred order, he should have the animal stand in the northern portion of the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices of the most sacred order must be slaughtered in this portion of the Temple Courtyard, as stated in Chapter 5, Halachot 2-3. See also <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:16.</i> facing the west. The person performing <i>semichah</i> stands to the east with his face to the west. He should place both his hands between its two horns and recite [the appropriate] confession, for a sin-offering, the sin which warrants a sin-offering and for a guilt-offering, the sin which warrants a guilt offering. For a burnt offering, he confesses the sin of [negating the observance of] a positive commandment or of a negative commandment that can be corrected by the observance of] a positive commandment.
How does he confess? He says: "I sinned, I transgressed, I committed iniquity, and I did this-and-this,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 2:5 which states that a person seeking to repent must mention the particular sins that he violated.</i> and I have repented before You and this is my atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This is necessary, for without <i>teshuvah</i>, a sacrifice will not bring the person atonement (<i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 3:10).</i><br>If he is bringing a peace-offering, he should perform <i>semichah</i> with all of his strength anywhere he desires within the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated in Chapter 5, Halachot 2-3, sacrifices of lesser sanctity (of which the peace offering is one) may be slaughtered anywhere in the Temple Courtyard.</i> where [the animal] will be slaughtered. It appears to me that one does not confess on a peace-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For a peace-offering is not offered to atone for a sin.</i> Instead, he says words of praise.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As an example, the <i>Or Sameach</i> cites Psalm 100. The popular translation of II Chronicles 30:22 speaks of the people reciting confessions on their peace-offerings. Rashi and <i>Metzudot</i>, however, render the verb as meaning "offer thanks."</i>

Chapter 4

All of the sacrifices may be offered only during the day,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In this chapter, the Rambam lists the time limits associated with bringing the sacrifices and describes the intent with which the sacrifices must be offered.</i> as [can be inferred from Leviticus 7:38]: "On the day when He commanded the children of Israel to offer their sacrifices." [Implied is] during the day and not at night. Therefore sacrifices are slaughtered only during the day and their blood is sprinkled [on the altar] on the day of the slaughter, as [can be inferred from <i>ibid.</i>:16]: "On the day he offers his sacrifice." Implied is that on the day the sacrifice is slaughtered, it should be offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., its blood should be sprinkled on the altar.</i> When the sun sets [on that day], the blood is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As is the entire sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 1:37).</i>
As long as the elements that cause a sacrifice to be permitted were offered<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the blood of the sacrifice was sprinkled on the altar (Radbaz).</i> during the day, [the other elements of] the sacrifice may be offered on the altar throughout the night.<br>What is implied? When the blood of sacrifices was sprinkled during the day, their <i>eimorim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The fats and inner organs of the animal that are offered on the altar.</i> may be offered on the fire of the altar at night until dawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The appearance of the first glimmerings of the light of the sun on the horizon more than an hour before sunrise.</i> Similarly, the limbs of the burnt-offerings may be offered on the fire of the altar until dawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As Leviticus 6:2 speaks of "the burnt-offering... on the altar, the entire night until the morning."</i> In order to distance [a person] from inadvertent transgression,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., "lest time become pressing and they would not be offered until the following day" [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 1:3)]. Rashi (at the beginning of his commentary to <i>Berachot</i>) and other authorities differ and maintain that such a safeguard was not instituted.</i> our Sages declared that the <i>eimorim</i> and the limbs of the burnt-offerings should only be offered on the fire of the altar until midnight.
Even though the <i>eimorim</i> and the limbs [of the sacrifices] may be offered on the fire of the altar at night, they may not be willingly delayed. Instead, an attempt should be made to offer everything during the day, for it is desirable that a mitzvah be performed at its designated time. [The importance of this can be seen from the fact that] the offering of the <i>eimorim</i> and the limbs [of the sacrifices] on the fire of the altar supersedes the Sabbath prohibitions on that day. We do not delay this until Saturday night.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">When there would be no prohibition involved in offering them.</i>
Any element of a sacrifice that may be offered only during the day, e.g., the handful [of flour from the meal-offering], the frankincense [from the meal-offering], the incense offering, and the meal-offerings that are burnt [on the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For example, the meal-offerings brought by the priests. See Chapter 12.</i> may be offered [on the altar] at sunset.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Provided certain conditions are met, these may be offered on the altar even after the afternoon daily sacrifice has been offered. See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 1:6 and notes.</i> They then continue to be consumed [by the altar's fire] the entire night like the limbs of the burnt offerings.
The libations that are offered with sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As accompanying offerings. See Chapter 2, Halachah 1.</i> are only offered during the day,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">They need not be offered on the day the sacrifice is offered, but they must be offered during the day.</i> as [Numbers 29:39] states: "And for their libations and for their peace-offerings." An association is established between the peace-offerings and the libations. Just as the peace-offerings [are offered only] during the day, so too, the libations must be offered during the day. Nevertheless, the libations that are offered independently may be sanctified at night and offered at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since they were not associated with a particular sacrifice, there are no limits as to when they may be offered.</i> Therefore if it happens that a person obtains libations that are brought independently, he should<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the gloss of the Radbaz who maintains that it is preferable for him to offer them at night, for the libations should be consecrated and offered at the earliest possible opportunity. Hence, if it is possible for him to do so by day, he should do so by day. If that option becomes available at night, he should not wait for the next day.</i> sanctify them at night and offer them at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This is acceptable even as an initial preference.</i> Dawn disqualifies [these libations], as it disqualifies the limbs of the burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one might think that since they were sanctified at night, they would not be disqualified until the end of the next day. Hence, it is necessary to state that this is not so.</i>
The entire day is acceptable for <i>semichah</i>, slaughter, severing a fowl's head,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 6.</i> offering objects on the altar's fire, bringing [meal] offerings to the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 6.</i> sprinkling [blood on the altar], waving [sacrificial offerings],<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See, for example, Chapter 9, Halachah 7.</i> taking a handful of the meal offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachot 12-13.</i> and bringing the <i>Musaf</i> offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The word "day" is mentioned in either the verse commanding the ritual acts mentioned or in a verse associated with them. Our Sages inferred that these ritual acts could only be performed during the day (see <i>Megilah</i> 20b).</i> The entire night is acceptable to offer the <i>eimorim</i> and the limbs on the altar's fire.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural Law. See Halachot 2-3 above.</i><br>This is the general principle: When the mitzvah is to perform a particular act during the day, it is acceptable throughout the day. If the mitzvah is to perform the act at night, it is acceptable throughout the night. Nevertheless, the eager hasten to perform the mitzvot.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one should endeavor to perform them at the earliest possible opportunity.</i>
As an initial preference, all of the sacrificial animals may be slaughtered only with a knife that is a sacred utensil. Nevertheless, if one slaughtered it with any article [fit] to slaughter an ordinary animal - even the edge of a bulrush<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 1:14 and notes.</i> - it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that the rationale is that slaughter is not considered part of the sacrificial service, as evidenced by the fact that it may be performed by a non-priest.</i>
Whenever one received less than the amount of blood need to sprinkle it on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The laws governing the sprinkling of the blood are described in the following chapter.</i> from any of the sacrifices, the blood does not become consecrated. [As an initial preference,] one should have the intent of receiving all the [animal's] blood.<br>What should be done? One should hold the windpipe and the gullet<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam refers to these with the term <i>siman</i>, "signs," for they are the organs which, when slit in the desired manner, indicate that ritual slaughter is acceptable.</i> in his hand, and together with the jugular vein, place them inside a basin<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is quoting <i>Zevachim</i> 25a-b. As indicated by the conclusion of the halachah, it appears that the intent is not that the animal's neck should be held inside the sacred basin - for then, it would be difficult to perform the slaughter - but rather, above it (see Radbaz).</i> and slit both of them - or their majority<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 1:9, the Rambam states: "Superior slaughter involves cutting both [the windpipe and the gullet], whether for an animal or a fowl, and a slaughterer should have this intent. [After the fact,] if one cut the majority of one of them for a fowl and the majority of both of them for an animal or a beast, the slaughter is acceptable."</i> - so that all of the blood will be received in the basin. [After the slaughter,] the knife should be lifted up, so that the blood does not flow down along it, but rather from the neck. He should wipe off the blood that is on the knife on the [outer] edge of the basin.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">So that the blood will not flow into the basin (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
The space above the basin is considered as if it is inside the basin.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Our division of the halachot is based on the authentic manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard published text has a slightly different version.</i> If one was receiving the blood [of a sacrificial animal] and the bottom of the basin opened before the blood reached the open space of the bottomless basin, the blood is not consecrated. [The rationale is that when the blood enters] space in which it will ultimately not come to rest, it is not considered as if it came to rest.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The question is whether the blood is considered as if it came to rest in the basin and then spilled out (in which instance, it would be acceptable to be gathered from the floor) or whether it spilled directly on the floor and was hence, disqualified (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 25b). The Rambam is stating that since the blood is not going to come to rest in the bottomless basin, it is not considered as if it was spilled out and is therefore not consecrated.<br>Note the Radbaz who maintains that even if the utensil has a hole in it, if the blood is able to be collected, it is acceptable.</i>
With regard to all of the sacrifices, the person performing the service<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">His intent is significant and not the intent of the person who donated the sacrifice [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 4:6); <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 14:1].</i> must have the intent of offering the proper type of sacrifice for the sake of the person bringing it at the time of slaughter, at the time the blood is received, at the time it is brought to the altar, and at the time that it is sprinkeled on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Although there are many activities connected with a sacrifice being offered, these four activities are considered the ones that are significant with regard to whether or not the sacrifice is disqualified.</i> as [indicated by Leviticus 7:15]: "And the meat of his thanksgiving, peace-offering." [Implied is that] the slaughter together with the [three other] services must be for the sake of his peace-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The process of Biblical exegesis through which these concepts are derived is explained in <i>Zevachim</i> 4a-b.</i> Similar concepts apply with regard to other sacrifices.<br>If one slaughtered [an animal] and performed [these] other services without any intent, without thinking at all,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, they were sacrificed with a different intent, the sacrifice is acceptable, but the person is not considered to have fulfilled his obligation (<i>Zevachim</i> 2a).</i> this is acceptable for a burnt-offering and a peace-offering and the owner is considered to have fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's words, it appears that this is not true with regard to a sin-offering or a guilt-offering or the Paschal sacrifice. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> note that from <i>Zevachim</i> 46b, it would appear that even those sacrifices are acceptable as long as one does not have a wrong intent. It is only when one has a wrong intent that they are disqualified. The Radbaz maintains that even though those sacrifices are not disqualified, the person bringing them is not considered to have fulfilled his obligation. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> does not accept this explanation and leaves his question of the Rambam's ruling unresolved.</i>
At the time of the slaughter of [an animal designated as] a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries discuss whether these concepts apply with regard to the peace offerings as well.
