From the Rambam's words, it would appear that the general intent applies to the peace-offerings, some of the particulars the Rambam mentions obviously do not.</i> [the person offering it] must have six things in mind, [that the animal be slaughtered]: for the sake of the [desired] sacrifice,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he must know the type of offering for which he is slaughtering an animal (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 46b).</i> for the sake of the person bringing it,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and not for someone else (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> that it be sacrificed unto God, blessed be He, that it be consumed by fire,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Rather than roasted for human consumption (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> that its consumption by fire be for the sake of generating a [pleasing] fragrance,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">That they be burnt on the altar and not elsewhere and then brought to the altar (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and that the fragrance be pleasing before God. If he slaughtered [the animal] without any intent, it is acceptable, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous halachah.</i><br>One who slaughters [an animal as] a sin-offering or a guilt-offering should have the intent that [the sacrifice atone] for the sin which he committed.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 15:6.</i>

Chapter 5

We have already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:6.<br>In this chapter, the Rambam explains the place where sacrifices are offered and the manner in which the blood and fats from the sacrifices is presented to God.</i> that the slaughter of consecrated animals is acceptable even when performed by non-priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, a non-priest may slaughter a sacrificial animal as an initial preference (Radbaz).</i> From the receiving of the blood and onward, the mitzvah is that of the priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 1:5 states: "The sons of Aaron will offer it." Implied is that all the acts involved in offering the animal should be performed by the priests (<i>Zevachim</i> 32a).</i> The blood of all the sacrifices must be received in a sacred utensil<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 97b notes that Exodus 24:5-6 speaks of the blood of peace-offerings being received in sacred vessels. Now if this is a requirement for peace-offerings which are sacrifices of lesser sanctity, it certainly holds true for sacrifices of the most sacred order.</i> held by a priest's hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the receptacle may not be resting on the ground (Radbaz).</i> Nevertheless, the place where they are slaughtered and their blood is received is not the same in all instances.
What is implied? Sacrifices of the most sacred order may be slaughtered and their blood may be received<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as implied by Chapter 4, Halachah 8, one must receive the blood immediately after slaughter, this is seemingly obvious. It could be explained that the Rambam is implying that even after the fact, receiving the blood elsewhere disqualifies the sacrifice. Moreover, even if he received only part of the blood outside the northern portion of the Temple Courtyard, the sacrifice is disqualified (Radbaz).</i> in the any portion of [the area designated as] north of the altar whose boundaries we defined in the beginning of this book.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:16 states:<br>[The area] from the northern wall of the Temple Courtyard until the wall of the Altar, was sixty and a half [cubits] wide. The length of this area, from the wall of the Entrance Hall until the eastern wall of the Courtyard, was 76 [cubits].<br>The rectangle [described above] is called "the northern portion." The sacrifices of the most sacred order were slaughtered there.<br>The 60 and a half cubit width can be broken down as follows:<br>the space between the pillars and the northern wall, 8 cubits,<br>the area of the pillars 12.5 cubits,<br>the area of the tables, 8 cubits,<br>the area of the rings, 24 cubits,<br>the space between the rings and the Altar, 8 cubits. It was also called <i>beit hamitbachayim</i>, the butchering area, because here the large majority of sacrifices were slaughtered and prepared to be offered on the altar.</i> Sacrifices of lesser sanctity may be slaughtered and their blood received in every place in the Temple Courtyard.
What is the source that teaches the sacrifices of the most holy order may be slaughtered only in the northern portion of the Temple Courtyard. With regard to a burnt-offering, [Leviticus 1:11] states: "And it shall be slaughtered on the northern flank of the altar." And with regard to the sin-offering, [<i>ibid.</i> 6:18] states: "In the place where the burnt-offering is slaughtered, you shall slaughter the sin-offering." And just as a sin-offering is called a sacrifice of the most sacred order,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse cited.</i> so too, the burnt-offering is called, a sacrifice of the most sacred order.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 5:1), he explains that although there is no specific verse referring to a burnt-offering as a sacrifice of the most sacred order, since an equivalence is established between it and a sin-offering and a guilt-offering, we can assume that it is included in that category.</i> With regard to the guilt-offering, [<i>ibid.</i> 7:2] states: "In the place where the burnt-offering is slaughtered, you shall slaughter the guilt-offering." And an association was established between communal peace-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the sheep offered on Shavuot. Different laws apply to individual peace-offerings, as stated below.</i> and sin-offerings, as [<i>ibid.</i> 23:19] states: "You shall offer one he-goat as a sin-offering and two sheep a year old as a peace-offering." Therefore, they are considered sacrifices of the most sacred order like a sin-offering and are slaughtered in the place where it is slaughtered.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that based on Numbers 10:10, <i>Zevachim</i> 55a establishes an association between communal peace-offerings and burnt-offerings and asks why the Rambam prefers the association with the sin-offerings. Seemingly, deriving the concept from the burnt-offering would be more direct. In resolution, he explains that since the Torah states that the sin-offering should be slaughtered in the same place as the burnt-offering, it is as if it is explicitly stated that the sin-offering should be slaughtered in the north. Hence, the derivation is not indirect.</i> In the place where [these sacrificial animals] are slaughtered, their blood should be received.
What is the source that teaches that sacrifices of lesser sanctity may be slaughtered in any place in the Temple Courtyard, even behind the Temple building?<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 54b explains that there was a small opening at the rear of the Holy of Holies. Since the area behind the Temple building could see the Holy of Holies, it was considered as if it saw "the entrance to the Tent of Meeting."</i> With regard to the peace-offerings, [Leviticus] 3:2] states: "And you shall slaughter it at the opening to the Tent of Meeting," this indicates that all directions are acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the verse cited, <i>Zevachim</i> 55a states that there is another verse (Leviticus 3:8  which mentions the slaughtering a sacrifice "before the Tent of Meeting." It explains that both verses are necessary, for from the verse cited by the Rambam, one would not know that it is permissible to slaughter an animal in the side portions of the Temple Courtyard. Indeed, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that a printing error crept into the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and the Rambam indeed desired to cite Leviticus 3:8.</i> for a particular direction was not specified. The same applies to other sacrifices of lesser sanctity. If they were slaughtered in the Temple building itself, they are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For if seeing the entrance to the Temple Building makes a sacrifice acceptable, certainly, being inside the Temple building itself does (<i>Zevachim</i> 63a).</i> If, however, they slaughtered them on the roof of the Temple building,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, this applies with regard to the roofs of the chambers in the Temple Courtyard (Radbaz). For the roofs of the structures in the Temple complex were not consecrated (<i>Pesachim</i> 85b).</i> they are not acceptable, for the roofs are not fit for ritual slaughter at all. It must be performed on the earth of the Temple Courtyard.
Peace offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tosafot, Yoma</i> 29a, maintains that this also applies to any other sacrificial animals that must be slaughtered. This view is not, however, accepted by all authorities (see the commentaries to <i>Zevachim</i> 61a).</i> that were slaughtered before the gates to the Temple building were opened are unacceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This law also applies if the gates to the Temple were closed during the day, but that generally did not take place (Radbaz).</i> as [indicated by the prooftext,] "at the opening to the Tent of Meeting," i.e., when it is open. Even if the doors were closed, [but not locked,] they are considered as locked. However, the curtain that is over [the entrance]<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 7:17.</i> does not disqualify [the offering].
The manner in which the blood of a burnt-offering, a guilt-offering, and a peace offering, whether communal or individual, is cast on the altar is the same for all three types of sacrifices at all times.<br>What does [the priest] do? The priest takes the blood which is in a receptacle and casts it from the receptacle<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., unlike the sin-offering mentioned in Halachah 9, he need not sprinkle it with his hand.</i> twice, [once] on each of two of the corners of the altar on a diagonal, on the bottom half of the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Beneath the scarlet band that was tied around the middle of the Altar. See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:9. If he cast the blood above that band, the sacrifice is disqualified.</i> [The corners were] the northeast and the southwest.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The priest would cast the blood on the northeast corner and then the southwest in that order (<i>Tamid</i> 4:1), so that when walking, he would be circling the altar to the right (<i>Yoma</i> 15b). These corners were chosen because there was no base on the altar's northwest corner (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:10).</i> When casting the blood on the corners, he should have the intent that the blood will surround the corners like a <i>gamma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A Greek letter whose shape resembles an upside-down "L."</i> In this way, the blood from these two castings will be found on the four walls of the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Hence our Sages (<i>Zevachim</i> 53b, <i>et al</i>) uses the expression: "Two presentations of blood that constitute four."</i> For with regard to the burnt-offering and the peace-offering, [the Torah] uses<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 1:11, 3:2.</i> the term "surrounding."<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">And in the manner described above, the blood will be cast on all four sides of the altar.</i> This also applies to the guilt offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 7:2 which describes the guilt offering also uses the term "surrounding." Nevertheless, its wording is slightly different.</i> The remainder of the blood is poured on the foundation on the southern side of the altar.
The sin offerings from which [the priests] partake require four presentations [of blood], [one] on each of the four corners of the outer altar, on its upper half,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If the blood is placed below the midway point of the altar, the sacrifice is disqualified.</i> for concerning it, [Leviticus 4:25] states: "On the corners of the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Implying that it must be placed on all four corners.</i><br>What should [the priest] do? When the priest takes the blood in the receptacle, he carries it to the altar. He dips the index finger of his right hand<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For all service is performed with the right hand. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 5:18.</i> into the blood. He then places his thumb [on one side of the altar] and his middle finger [on the other side] and applies<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The verb used by the Rambam (and his source, <i>Zevachim</i> 53a) refers to the wording employed by Leviticus 6:19, which means "performs the service of the sin-offering."</i> [the blood] on the point of the corner of the altar, descending until he finishes [bestowing] all the blood on his finger. He does this on each corner. If he placed the blood near the corner, within a cubit on either side, he achieves atonement [for the owner of the sacrifice].<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after the fact, the sacrifice is acceptable.</i>
He must dip his finger [in the blood] before applying it to each corner. When he finishes applying it to the corner, he should clean his finger on the [outer] edge of the container.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">So that it does not become mixed with the blood remaining in the container (<i>Zevachim</i> 93b).</i> He then dips it in a second time, for the blood on his finger is unacceptable to place on another corner.
None of the other sacrifices require the application of the blood on the altar with one's finger aside from the sin-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6.</i> for with regard to it [Leviticus 4:6] states: "And he shall dip his finger into the blood." There must be enough blood so that he can dip his finger into it. He should not collect the blood with his finger.
From [which] corner should he begin? He should ascend on the ramp and turn to his right, walking on the surrounding ledge. He should apply the blood to the southeast corner first and then to the second corner that is close to it, the northeast corner.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For he is circling to the right, as required (<i>Zevachim</i> 62b).</i> Afterwards, he should proceed to the third corner, [the one] next to it, the northwest corner, and afterwards, to the fourth corner next to it, which is the southwest. On the base of the altar at the corner which he completed the applications of the blood, he pours out the remaining blood,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If he fails to do this, the sacrifice is not disqualified (see Chapter 19, Halachah 4).</i> as [Leviticus 4:17] states: "And all of the [remaining] blood he should pour on the base of the altar of the burnt-offerings."<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests amending the prooftext to Leviticus 30:34 for that verse refers to the sin-offerings brought on the outer altar, while the verse cited by the Rambam refers to the sin-offerings whose blood is sprinkled within the Temple itself.</i> This refers to the base on the southern side [of the altar].
The blood of all of the sin-offerings that are burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachah 16, for a definition of this term.</i> is taken in the Temple building, and it is sprinkled there as described in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah for an actual description.</i> The remainder of the blood should be poured on the western base of the outer altar, [the one] that he encounters first when he leaves the Temple building.
How is their blood sprinkled and how many sprinklings are made? The blood of both the bull<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The sin-offering of the High Priest and his priestly brethren.</i> and the goat<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The goat whose pair was sent to Azazel.</i> offered on Yom Kippur require eight sprinklings between the staves [of the ark] and eight sprinklings on the curtain [before the Holy of Holies]. He then mixes together the blood of the bull and that of the goat and sprinkles it four times, one on each of the four corners of the golden altar in the Temple building and seven sprinklings on the center of this altar, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 4:2.</i> [Even] if he did not carefully direct the sprinklings<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The sprinklings had to be directed, one upward and seven downward, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i>. If the High Priest was not careful about directing the sprinklings in this manner, they are still acceptable.<br>This is the interpretation offered by Rav Yosef Corcus and also suggested as an alternative by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, also offers another interpretation: that the Hebrew should be translated as "he did not have the intent," i.e., he performed the act without the intent of it being a mitzvah. This can be connected to a question of a much larger scope: Must a person who performs a mitzvah have the intention of doing so or not? From <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 32b, it would appear that such intention is necessary. However, other Talmudic sources imply that there is no need for such intention. Whenever a person performs the deed of a mitzvah, he fulfills his obligation, regardless of his intention.<br>On the surface, the Rambam's own decisions regarding this question appear paradoxical. <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 6:3 states: "A person who eats <i>matzah</i> without the intention [of fulfilling the mitzvah]... fulfills his obligation." By contrast, <i>Hilchot Shofar</i> 2:4 states that a person who hears the sounding of the <i>shofar</i> from one who blows it casually, without the intent of performing a mitzvah, does not fulfill his obligation. There is extensive debate about this issue among the commentaries. The general consensus is that one must have the intent of performing a mitzvah. The situation involving <i>matzah</i> is unique for various reasons, each authority explaining the rationale for the exception in his own way. See the discussion of the rulings cited for more details.</i> performed in the innermost [chamber], they are acceptable.
The blood of the bulls that are burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">These include a bull brought by a High Priest as a sin-offering and the bull brought as atonement for a law forgotten by the High Court (Chapter 1, Halachot 15-16).</i> and the goats that are burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As atonement for the prohibition against idol worship (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> should both be cast [in the following manner]. They should be sprinkled seven times on the curtain that separates between the Sanctuary and the Holy of Holies and four times on the four corners of the golden altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This is stated explicitly with regard to the sin-offering brought by the High Priest in Leviticus 4:6-7 and the bull brought because of the error of the High Court (<i>ibid.</i>:17). The sprinkling of the blood of the goats is derived from the laws regarding these offerings as stated in Halachah 16.</i>
With regard to the blood placed on the golden altar: When he enters [the Sanctuary],<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the sprinkling of the blood on Yom Kippur, this refers to the entry of the High Priest from the Holy of Holies. With regard to the other sacrifices, it refers to the entry into the Sanctuary from the Entrance Hall. The commentaries see a difficulty between the Rambam's words here and his statements in <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 4:2.</i> he stands between the altar and the <i>Menorah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Menorah</i> was on the southern side of the Sanctuary (to the left of the priest as he faces the Holy of Holies).</i> with the altar in front of him. He should [sprinkle the blood] on the outer side of the horns of the altar. He should begin with the northeast corner and [proceed] to the northwest and then to the southwest and then to the southeast.
With regard to the bull brought by the anointed priest<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest.</i> for the violation of any of the mitzvot, the anointed priest himself receives its blood and sprinkles it in the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As related by Leviticus 4:6, he sprinkles the blood toward the curtain separating the Sanctuary from the Holy of Holies.</i> If an ordinary priest received this blood and sprinkled it, it is acceptable.
The goats [offered in atonement for] idol worship are called the goats which are burnt. The Torah does not explicitly state how their blood was sprinkled [on the altar]. Nevertheless, since they are communal sin offerings, the laws applying to them should be the same in their particulars as those applying to the bull brought because of a forgotten law, for it is also a communal sin-offering. [This applies to] the sprinkling of the blood, burning [the sacrifices], and the impurity they impart to the one who has them burnt.
The blood of each [of the following], the firstborn offering, the tithe offering, and the Paschal sacrifice is required to be presented to the altar through one pouring at its base, in any direction one desires on the three corners of the altar that have a base. For, as we explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:10.</i> the southeastern corner did not have a base.<br>What is the source that teaches that all that is required is one present? For with regard to the first born sacrifice, [Numbers 18:17] states: "You shall cast its blood on the altar." According to the Oral Tradition, we learned that this also applies to the tithe offering and the Paschal sacrifice. Their blood is presented once like that of the firstborn offering.
The <i>eimorim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The fats and the inner organs that are offered on the altar.</i> of all the sacrifices are the first [elements of the sacrifice] offered on the pyre of the altar after the blood is cast [on the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This order is explicitly stated in the Torah's description of the manner in which several sacrifices are offered, e.g., Numbers 18:17: "Their blood shall the cast upon the altar and their fats they shall offer on the altar's pyre."</i> All of the sacrificial [animals] are skinned and then the <i>eimorim</i> are removed. They are not skinned until the blood is cast. [This applies] with the exception of the sin-offerings, for they are not skinned at all,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 2.</i> as [Leviticus 16:27] states: "You shall burn with fire their hide and their flesh." Thus, [the order of sacrifice is] first the blood is cast [on the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For otherwise there would be an interruption between receiving the blood and casting it upon the altar (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i>103b). This, however, is not an absolute requirement. If the animal is skinned first, the sacrifice is not disqualified (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:9).</i> Then the sacrificial [animals] are skinned and then their [bellies] are ripped open, the <i>eimorim</i> are removed and offered [on the pyre of the altar].
All of the hides of the sacrifices of the most sacred order - whether communal offerings or individual offerings - are given to the priests, as [Leviticus 7:8] states: "The hide of the burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">And from the burnt-offering, we derive that this law applies to other sacrifices of the most sacred order (<i>Zevachim</i> 103a).</i> he offered [shall belong to that priest]." The hides of sacrifices of lesser sanctity, by contrast, are given to the owners. Whenever the flesh of a burnt-offering did not merit to be offered on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the sacrifice was disqualified before its blood was cast on the altar in which instance, it was never required that its flesh would be offered on the altar.</i> the priests do not acquire its hide, as [implied by the above verse which] states: "the burnt-offering of a man." [We can infer that it refers only to] a burnt-offering that was acceptable for a man.
In all instances that a disqualifying factor was caused in one of the sacrifices of the most sacred order before they were skinned, their hides are not granted to the priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, they are burnt together with the meat of the sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:9).</i> [If they are disqualified] after they are skinned, their hides are granted to the priests. All of the hides should be divided among the members of the priestly watch from Friday to Friday.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tosefta, Zevachim</i>, ch. 11, states that originally, the hides were divided daily among the members of the clan who served in the Temple that day. Nevertheless, under these circumstances, strong-armed priests would seize the hides from others. Hence, they organized a larger division where seizing the hides in that manner would not be possible.</i>
When a person consecrates a burnt-offering to the Temple treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is offered on the altar, but it is not considered as his personal sacrifice.</i> or one consecrates his property [to the Temple treasury] and among [his possessions] were male animals concerning which the law is that they should be sacrificed as burnt-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam LeAm</i> notes that there is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:7), one opinion states - as the Rambam states here - that the male animals should be offered as burnt-offerings. Rabbi Eliezer maintains that the males should be sold to others to be offered as burnt-offerings. Significantly, when referring to this law in <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> and in his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam follows Rabbi Eliezer's position.</i> the hides are not given to the priests, for [the prooftext] states: "the burnt-offering of a man." This excludes the burnt-offering of the Temple treasury. Instead, the hides should be sold and the money given to the Temple treasury.<br>The hide of a burnt-offering brought by] a man or a woman, a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that the standard published texts for <i>Zevachim</i> 103a speak about a convert rather than a gentile. He favors the Rambam's version of the text, because seemingly there is no reason to separate between a convert and another Jew in this regard.</i> or a servant, is given to the priests. The term "man" was used only to exclude the Temple treasury.

Chapter 6

It is a positive commandment to offer the burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 63) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 115) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. This refers both to the burnt-offerings brought by individuals and those brought by the Jewish people as a whole.</i> according to its statutes as they are written in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Having described the general principles that apply with regard to all the sacrifices, the Rambam begins to focus on each one individually, describing its laws in a particular chapter or set of halachot. Here the Rambam focuses on the manner in which the sacrifices are brought. In later sets of halachot, he speaks of the obligations of individuals and of the Jewish people as a whole to bring these sacrifices.</i> How is the burnt-offering brought? One slaughters [the animal] and casts its blood in the manner described,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachot 1-3, with regard to the slaughter of the animal and Halachah 6 of that chapter with regard to casting its blood on the altar.</i> skins it, cuts it into pieces and offers all the pieces on the pyre of the altar, as [Leviticus 1:9] states: "The priest shall offer the entire [sacrifice] on the pyre of the altar."
The wool on the heads of the sheep, the hair of the beards of the goats, and the bones, sinews, horns, and hoofs of the [sacrificial animals]<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">All of these are not fundamental parts of the animal's body. Hence while they are connected to the body, they are considered as part of it and must be offered on the altar's pyre. The implication is that ordinarily, they would not be separated and the animal would be offered on the altar while they were attached. Nevertheless, if they had been separated and they had descended from the altar, they are considered as distinct and there is no longer any obligation to offer them.</i> should be offered on the pyre of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, they flew off the pyre, but remained on the altar, they should be placed back on the pyre (<i>Meilah</i> 9b).</i> if they are still connected [naturally<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This addition is made on the basis of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 9:5).</i> to the animal's body]. If they have been separated, they should not be brought up [to the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, if they were brought up to the altar as separate entities, they should be taken down again, rather than offered (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:16). Nevertheless, if they were separated on the altar itself before they were offered, they should not be taken down, but instead should be offered on its pyre (Radbaz, Rav Yosef Corcus).</i> [This is derived from Deuteronomy 12:27 which mentions]: "Your burnt offerings, the flesh and the blood."<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The prooftext is defining the meat and the blood as the fundamental elements of a burnt offering. The implication is that it is necessary for them to be consumed by the altar's fire and hence, they must be returned to the altar if they flew off. There is, by contrast, no fundamental necessity for the secondary elements of the animal's body to be consumed by fire. Hence there is no obligation to return them to the altar if they flew off.</i> All parts of the sacrificial animal which flew off<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 9:6), the Rambam explains that often we see that when entities that contain moisture are placed on a fire, the moisture will vaporize. At times, the process will be powerful enough to lift up that entity and propel it upward.</i> the altar should not be returned to it. Similarly, a coal which flew off the altar should not be returned to it.
[The following rules apply when] the limbs [of a sacrificial animal] flew off the altar. If they still have substance, even if they flew off after midnight,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">When at times a distinction is made, as the Rambam explains in the second portion of the halachah.</i> they should be returned to the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">To be consumed by its fire, as the mitzvah requires.</i> [for Leviticus 6:2] speaks of [the burnt-offering remaining] "on its fire, on the altar, the entire night." If they do not have substance, even if they flew off before midnight, they should not be returned.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For the mitzvah of having them consumed by fire has been completed.</i> If the meat became charred and dried up upon them like wood<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they did not become like ash.</i> and they flew off before midnight, they should be returned. After midnight, they should not be returned.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 86a notes that the prooftext cited speaks of the burnt offering being on the altar's fire "the entire night, until the morning." From the redundancy, our Sages derive that within the night, a distinction should be made. See also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
After one cuts up the limbs of the burnt-offering, all of the different portions should be brought to the altar's ramp<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 10-18 which describes the manner in which the limbs are brought to the altar.</i> and salted there.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For salt must be applied to all offerings (see <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:11-12).</i> Afterwards, all of the limbs should be brought up to the top of the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Different priests would perform this service, i.e., one priest would bring a limb to the ramp and salt it and another one would bring it to the top of the altar (Radbaz, <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 4:6,8).</i> The <i>gid hanesheh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The Rabbis identified the <i>gid hanesheh</i> as the sciatic nerve, the large main nerve running down the back of an animal's hind leg. This nerve must be separated before the meat of an animal is eaten or offered on the altar. See <i>Hilchot Ma'achaolot Assurot</i> 8:1 for more details.</i> should be removed at the top of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">It should not be removed beforehand, because the limb will not look attractive as it is being carried to the altar (<i>Chulin</i> 90b).</i> and tossed on the pile of ash in the midst of the altar. All of the limbs should be cast on the altar, as [implied by the prooftext cited above], "the flesh and the blood." Just as the blood is cast [on the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">As described in Chapter 5, Halachah 6.</i> so too, all of the flesh must be cast [on the pyre].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, the altar's ramp was separated slightly from the altar itself to insure that the limbs were thrown on the fire rather than merely placed there (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:13).</i> After they were tossed [on the altar, the priest] should come and arrange them on the pyre, as [Leviticus 1:12] states: "And the priest shall arrange them." If limbs [of sacrificial animals] were roasted and then brought up to the altar, they are not considered as a pleasing fragrance.
How is the burnt-offering cut up? One would not break the animal's leg. Instead, a hole was made in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As obvious from the continuation of the halachah, the hole is not made in the animal's foot, but on its thigh above its knee. Thus it will remain hanging after its legs were cut off.</i> It was hung from [the hole]<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">There were pillars in the Temple Courtyard with hooks to serve this purpose (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:13).</i> and skinned. If [the burnt-offering] was an ox, it should be skinned without being hung.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps because of its weight, hanging it up would present a difficulty.</i><br>The person<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:6, the Rambam states that the skinning of the animal and its division into portions need not be performed by a priest.</i> continues skinning, until he gets to the breast, When he gets to the breast, he should cut off the head<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If he would skin the breast before cutting off the head, the skin would hang from the neck and it would be difficult to cut off the head precisely (<i>Tifferet Yisrael, Tamid</i> 4:2). Alternatively, since the head is offered first, it is appropriate that it be cut off first (Radbaz).</i> and give it to a priest. He then cuts off the feet<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The portion below the knees.</i> and gives them to others. He then completes skinning the animal. He rips open the heart and takes out its blood. He then proceeds to the forelegs and cuts them off.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the portion from the knee until the shoulder joint.</i> He proceeds to the right hindleg and gives it to [the priest] who merited [to carry] the head. The two testicles should be taken with it.<br>Afterwards, he rips open the animal's [belly] until he reveals the intestines. He takes the fat<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fat that is on the digestive organs.</i> and places it on the head, to cover the place of slaughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">So that when the head is carried to the altar, the opening where it was severed will not be seen. This is a gesture of respect for the Divine Presence.</i> He takes the inner organs and gives them to another [priest].
[The latter] goes and washes them with water, as [Leviticus 1:13] states: "The inner organs and the feet should be washed with water." [This excludes] wine, a mixture of wine and water, and other liquids. All types of water are, [however,] acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even water that is collected is acceptable (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>To what extent should he wash them? The stomach should be washed in the Washing Chamber<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This chamber is described in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:17. The stomach was washed there, because it would not be respectful to wash out its filth in the Temple Courtyard.</i> to whatever degree necessary. The intestines should be washed at least three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are narrow, it is difficult to clean them thoroughly. At least three washings are necessary. If more are required to clean them, they should be washed more [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Tamid</i> 4:2)].</i> They should be washed on the marble table located between the pillars.
He then takes the knife and separates the lung from the liver and [also] the lobe of the liver from the liver.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For it is sacrificed with the tail as stated in Halachot 9 and 13.</i> He should not move the liver from its place. He hollows out the chest and gives it to another [priest]. He then proceeds upward to the right flank and cuts if off and descends to the backbone. He does not touch the backbone<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For the backbone is offered with the left flank.</i> until he reaches the two soft ribs. He cuts it off and gives it to another [priest] while the liver is attached to it.
He then reaches the neck and leaves it with two ribs on either side. He cuts it off and gives it to another priest with the windpipe, the heart, and the lungs attached to it. He proceeds to the left flank and leaves two soft ribs above and two soft ribs below and, as he had left in the other one. Thus on the two sides, he leaves four ribs on either side. He then cuts it off and gives it to another person together with the backbone<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that the backbone is left intact so that the vertebrae will not separate into individual units.</i> with the spleen attached to it.
He then comes to the tail and severs it and gives it to another [priest] together with the fat-tail, the lobe of the liver, and the two kidneys with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The ribs that were left are also taken with it (Radbaz).</i> He should take the left foot and give it to another [priest]. This order should be followed when skinning and cutting up a burnt-offering of an animal. These are the portions referred to by the Torah [in Leviticus 1:6]: "You shall cut it into its portions."<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus states that there is no Scriptural source for the division of the animal in this manner. Instead, this was simply the most practical and logical way of dividing it.</i>
How many priests bring the limbs to the ramp? If the burnt-offering is from sheep, six priests should carry it.
The first [priest] carries the head and the [right] foot.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In this way, a portion of the animal with many bones (the head) and much meat (the leg) will have been offered. The head is also given priority, because it has been mentioned explicitly in the Torah (Radbaz).</i> [He carries] the head in his right hand<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For the offering of the head is most important.</i> with its nose facing his forearm and its horns between his fingers. The place where [the animal] was slaughtered is held upward with the fat upon it. The right foot is in his left hand<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since carrying the limbs to the altar is not part of the atonement process, there is no difficulty in it being performed with one's left hand (Rabbenu Asher).</i> with the side from which the skin was removed<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the outer side. Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Tamid</i> 4:4).</i> facing outward.
The second [priest carries] the two forelegs, the right one in his right hand and the left one in his left hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">These limbs are the largest and hence, receive priority.</i> The side from which the skin was removed should face outward.
The third [priest carries] the tail and the hindleg, the tail in his right hand with the fat-tail that hangs between his fingers. Together with it should be the lobe of the liver and the two kidneys. The left hindleg should be in his left hand with the side from which the skin was removed facing outward.
The fourth [priest carries] the breast and the neck, the breast in his right hand and the neck in his left hand with the ribs between his fingers.
The fifth [priest carries] the two flanks, the right flank in his right hand and the left flank in his left hand. The side from which the skin was removed should face outward.
The sixth [priest carries] the intestines in a bowl.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>op. cit.</i>). Others interpret the term <i>bezech</i> differently.</i> The feet should be placed above them.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">To cover them, for their appearance is not appealing (Radbaz).</i> [This applies] if the burnt-offering was a sheep or a goat. If, however, it was a ram, two priests should bring the intestines.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since a ram is much larger than a sheep, it would be difficult for one priest to carry the intestines alone. The Radbaz explains that the intent is that the intestines are cut in half and part given to one priest and part to another. The <i>Tifferet Yisrael</i> (<i>Yoma</i> 2:6) differs and maintains that two priests carry them together.</i><br>Similarly, [if it was a sheep,] the flour of the accompanying offering should be brought by one priest and the wine by another. If it was a ram, the flour should be brought by two priests<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Here too, because it is a larger amount, it would be difficult for one priest to carry it. Here too, the Radbaz explains that the intent is that each priest carries a container with half of the meal offering, while the <i>Tifferet Yisrael</i> maintains that it is placed in a large container and that container is carried by two priests together.</i> and the wine, by two priests.
Thus eight [priests] bring [a burnt offering of] a sheep or a goat to the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is speaking about any burnt-offering. Therefore he does not mention the <i>chavitin</i> offering which is brought together with the daily burnt-offering.</i> That of a ram is brought by eleven [and that of an ox is brought by twenty-four].
There are 24 [priests] who bring [a burnt offering of] an ox to the altar. The first brings the head. The second and the third bring the right foot.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For the foot of an ox is large and requires two priests to bring it. Similar concepts apply with regard to the other limbs.</i> The fourth and the fifth bring the tail. The sixth and the seventh bring the left foot. The eighth brings the breast. The neck is brought by three [priests], the ninth, tenth, and eleventh. The two forelegs are brought by two priests, the two flanks, by two, and the intestines, the flour, and the wine in sets of three.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the Radbaz explains that the portions would be divided, with each priest receiving a separate portion. This, he maintained, is a greater expression of honor and respect than to have the priests carry the burdens together.</i> Thus there are 24.
Why was a large limb of an ox not divided into portions? Because it is written: "You shall cut it into its portions." [Implied is that] its portions should not be cut into portions.<br>When are the above [number of priests] required [to bring the limbs to the altar]?<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed additions are made on the basis of the gloss of the Radbaz. He explains that the animal may not be cut into smaller portions, as implied by the first clause of this halachah. (Indeed, the prooftext cited refers to an individual burnt-offering.)</i> For a communal burnt-offering. If it was desired that the portions of an individual burnt-offering be brought to the altar with less than this [number of priests] or more, it is possible.
How was the burnt-offering of a fowl brought? [The priest] would ascend upon the ramp<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Holding the fowl.</i> He would turn [right] to the surrounding ledge and approach the southeast corner.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 65a states that this corner is chosen, because it is close to the ash pile where the skin and the intestines would be cast.</i> There he would sever the head [of the fowl] at the nape of its neck, severing [the head]<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> (gloss to Chapter 7, Halachah 6) quotes authorities who maintain that he need not sever the head entirely from the body entirely. What must be severed are the gullet and the windpipe organs. Just as ritual slaughter requires that they be severed, so too, they must be severed in the rite of <i>melikah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> (to that halachah) however, understand the Rambam's words simply. The head must be separated from the body entirely.</i> entirely. If he does not sever it entirely, [the sacrifice] is invalid. He then squeezes out the blood of the head and the blood of the body on the wall of the altar above the [scarlet] band in the middle of the altar.<br>If he squeezed out the blood of the head, but did not squeeze out the blood of the body, [the sacrifice] is invalid. If he squeezed out the blood of the body, but did not squeeze out the blood of the head, [the sacrifice] is valid.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale for both these rulings is that the majority of the blood is lodged in the body.</i>
He should take the head and bring the opening where it was severed close to the altar, dip it into salt,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For all offerings must be salted.</i> and cast it on the pyre. He then focuses his attention on the body. He removes the crop,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">One of the stomaches of the fowl.</i> the skin that is upon it with its feathers,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the Rambam's Commentary of the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 6:5). The Radbaz interprets the term <i>notzah</i> as feces rather than as feathers and explains that this enables us to understand why these organs are not burnt together with the rest of the fowl. The intestines of an animal are washed out before being offered. Hence they are fit to be offered on the altar. Those of the fowl are not.</i> and the intestines that are removed with it<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Leviticus 1:16.</i> and casts them on the ash pile.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The ash pile on the floor of the Temple Courtyard, between the ramp and the altar, near the southeastern corner of the altar. See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 2:12. The term ash-pile has several meanings; see Chapter 7, Halachah 4.</i>
[Leviticus 1:17 states:] "He shall split it with its wings" - [this is performed] by hand,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from Judges 14:6 (<i>Zevachim</i> 65b).</i> not with a knife. He need not separate [one part of the body from the other], as [that verse] states: "He [need] not separate it." If he separates it, it is valid. He then spreads salt upon it and casts it upon the altar.<br>If he did not remove the craw, the feathers, or the intestines or spread salt upon it, it is valid. As long as the change that one makes comes after squeezing out the blood, [the sacrifice] is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Because the primary element of the sacrifice was completed (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i>
How is <i>melikah</i> performed? [The priest] cuts and descends with his nail at the nape of the neck.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 8, which describes the manner in which the priest would hold the fowl. As stated there, this was one of the difficult tasks performed in the Sanctuary.</i> If he desires to move his nail back and forth,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As one does when performing ritual slaughter (<i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 2:7).</i> he may. If he wishes to chop and descend with his nail,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Such a motion is unacceptable for ritual slaughter (<i>ibid.</i> 3:7).</i> he may. If the organs necessary for ritual slaughter slip from their place,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This would disqualify an animal for ritual slaughter (<i>ibid.</i> 3:14).</i> he need not be concerned.<br>He must cut through the spine, i.e., the neckbone, without cutting through the majority of the meat surrounding it, for if he would cut through the majority of the meat, [the sacrificial animal] would be considered as dead<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that would make the animal. <i>trefe</i>.</i> before he reached the organs necessary for ritual slaughter. With regard to a burnt offering, as he descends he must cut both the organs<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Cutting the majority of them is, however, acceptable. They need not be severed entirely.</i> necessary for ritual slaughter.<br>When one performs <i>melikah</i> with a knife or does so from the side, it is not considered as <i>melika</i>. Instead, it is like strangling or decapitating the fowl. The entire [width of] the nape of the neck is acceptable for <i>melikah</i>.

Chapter 7

It is a positive commandment to offer the sin-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 64) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 138) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. Even though there are several different types of sin-offerings, since they are all offered in the same manner, they are considered only as one mitzvah (Radbaz).</i> according to its statutes as they are written in the Torah. How are the sin-offerings which are eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 1, Halachot 15-16, the Rambam mentions which sin-offerings are eaten and which are burnt.</i> brought? One slaughters [the animal] and sprinkles its blood in the manner described,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachot 1-3, with regard to the slaughter of the animal and Halachot 7-10 of that chapter with regard to casting its blood on the altar.</i> skins it, and separates the <i>eimorim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The fats and inner organs offered on the altar. See Leviticus 4:8-10.</i> He salts them and casts them on the pyre. If he desires to place the <i>eimorim</i> in a container while they are being carried to the altar, he may. The remainder of the meat is eaten by male priests in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 6:19 speaks of the priests partaking of the sin-offerings in the Courtyard of the Tent of Meeting. See Chapter 10.</i>
How are the sin-offerings which are burnt brought? One slaughters [the animal] and sprinkles its blood in the manner described. Afterwards, one rips open [its belly] and removes the <i>eimorim</i>. He places them into a container, salts them, and casts them on the pyre. The remainder [of the animal] should be taken outside the city<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 2:12 speaks of these sacrifices being burnt outside the camp. See also <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 3:7.</i> and cut up there as the burnt offering is cut up<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Halachot 5-9.</i> with its hide.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">They are not skinned before being burnt, as related in Chapter 5, Halachah 18.</i> [The pieces] are burnt there in the ash pile.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4.</i>
There are three places [where sacrifices] are burnt: The first is in the midst of the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">It was to the east of the ramp ascending to the altar (<i>Sifra, Vayikra</i> 9:3).</i> [The following are] burnt there: sacrifices [of the highest order of sanctity] that have been disqualified,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Zevachim</i> 104b) explains that since these sacrifices are eaten in the Temple Courtyard, they should also be burnt there.</i> the <i>eimorim</i> of sacrifices of lesser sanctity which were disqualified,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since these <i>eimorim</i> should have been burnt on the altar, they are burnt in the Temple Courtyard if they became impure (Radbaz). The sacrifices of lesser sanctity themselves should be burnt in Jerusalem (the place where they are eaten). We can assume that they were burnt on the Temple Mount, at the <i>birah</i> (<i>ibid.,</i> gloss to Halachah 4).</i> the bulls and goats that are burnt if they are disqualified, whether before their blood was sprinkled or after their blood was sprinkled,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Although they had reached a stage where they were to be taken out of the Temple Courtyard, since in fact they had not been removed from the Temple Courtyard before they were disqualified, they should be burnt there (Radbaz).</i> e.g., they became impure, they were taken outside the Temple Courtyard before the time they were supposed to be taken out arrived, or their meat or their <i>eimorim</i> remained overnight [without being burnt].<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See more details in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:3.</i>
The second place is on the Temple Mount. It is called the <i>birah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>birah</i> means "tower" or "large building." At times, it is used to refer to the Temple complex as a whole.</i> There we burn sin-offerings that are to be burnt if they were disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Because of impurity or because they remained overnight. These factors can still disqualify these sacrifices even though they have been taken out of the Temple Courtyard (Radbaz).</i> after they departed from the Temple Courtyard.<br>The third place is outside Jerusalem. It is called the ash-pile. There we burn the sin-offerings that are burnt when they are burnt according to their commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when they have not been disqualified. The Biblical commandment is to burn them "outside the camp." In later times, that meant "outside Jerusalem" as stated in Halachah 2. The term ash-pile has several meanings; see Chapter 6, Halachah 21.</i>
It is acceptable for the burning of all of [the sacrifices] that must be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., those sacrifices which must be burnt outside Jerusalem. Those that must be burnt in the Temple Courtyard must be burnt with wood fit to be used for the altar (<i>Sifra</i>).</i> to be performed by a non-priest<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This indicates that burning them is not a fundamental element of the sacrificial service (Radbaz).</i> and at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">There are some exceptions to this as stated in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:5.</i> Any type of wood, even straw and stubble, is acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the wood need not meet the criteria for wood required for the altar.</i> for the burning of all of the sacrifices that must be burnt outside the Temple Courtyard, as [Leviticus 4:12] states: "On wood with fire," any type of fire. Why is wood mentioned? To exclude lime or hot ash.
How is a sin-offering from fowl brought? <i>Melikah</i> should be performed on the southwest corner [of the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Zevachim</i> 63a,b, it is apparent that, after the fact, a sin-offering is acceptable if <i>melikah</i> is performed at any place in the Temple Courtyard. (See also Halachah 9.) This corner is mentioned as an initial preference, because the blood must be sprinkled there (Radbaz).</i> as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus states that the reference is to the order of <i>melikah</i> described at the conclusion of ch. 6. <i>Melikah</i> at the southwestern corner of the altar is not mentioned elsewhere.</i> He should descend with his nail until he cuts the organs [required for ritual slaughter]<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The windpipe and the gullet.</i> or [at least] the majority of one of them. He should not separate the head from the body.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the requirement for the burnt-offering mentioned in Chapter 6, Halachah 20.</i> If he does, he disqualifies [the sacrifice] and is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 112) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 124) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.<br>The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> quotes authorities who understand this as meaning that one may not separate both the gullet and the windpipe entirely. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, maintains that the Rambam's words should be explained simply: the head may not be severed from the body. If it is attached, even if these two organs are severed, the prohibition is not violated.</i> as [Leviticus 5:8] states: "He shall nip off its head at the nape [of the neck], but should not separate it."<br>He then sprinkles its blood on the wall of the altar, below its midpoint.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the requirement for the burnt-offering mentioned in Chapter 6, Halachah 20.</i> The remainder of the blood should be squeezed out on the altar's base,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Hence when performing <i>melikah</i>, the priest does not ascend on the ramp, but rather stands on the ground (Radbaz).</i> as stated [<i>ibid.</i>: 9]: "And the remainder of the blood, he shall squeeze out on the altar's base." One can conclude from this that when one presents the blood on the wall [of the altar], [the fact that] the remainder [of the blood] is squeezed out toward the base [indicates that] "the wall" refers to the lower [portion] of the wall.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is explaining why the obligation is to present the blood of a sin-offering from fowl on the lower half of the altar in contrast to the blood of a sin-offering from an animal which is presented on the upper half.</i>
Squeezing out the blood of a fowl brought as a sin-offering is an absolute requirement.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Although there is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in the Talmud and there are passages which appear to follow the other view, the Rambam's approach represents the consensus, as indicated by <i>Me'ilah</i> 9b (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> All the altar receives from this offering is its blood. The remainder is eaten by males of the priestly family like the meat of an animal brought as a sin-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10.</i>
How should the fowl brought as a sin-offering be held at the time of <i>melikah</i>? He should hold its two feet between two of his fingers<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The pinky and the finger next to it on his left hand.</i> and its two wings between his other two fingers,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The index finger and the middle finger.</i> extending its neck over [the thumb for]<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed additions are made on the basis of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 6:4).</i> a width of two fingers and then snip off its head.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">With his right hand.</i><br>This was one of the difficult tasks performed in the Temple. If one deviated and held the fowl in any other manner, it is acceptable.
Every part of the altar is acceptable for <i>melikah</i>, provided he sprinkles its blood below the midpoint of the altar. If he sprinkles it anywhere [on the altar], it is acceptable provided he presents [at least] a small amount of the blood of the soul<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 6:3, this term refers to the blood that flows out when the fowl is slaughtered.</i> below [the midpoint of the altar].
The upper half of southwest corner of the altar would serve three purposes and the lower half would serve three purposes. The lower half was used for the <i>melikah</i> of a fowl brought as a sin-offering, approaching the altar with the meal-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 6; Chapter 13, Halachah 12.</i> and pouring the remainder of the blood of the burnt-offerings, the sin-offerings that are eaten, the guilt-offerings, and the peace-offerings upon its [base].<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachot 6, 10.</i><br>The three purposes for which the upper portion was used are: the water libation on Sukkos,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 10:6.</i> the wine libation of the accompanying offering [of Sukkot],<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in contrast to the wine libations offered throughout the year which are offered on the lower portion of the altar. See Chapter 2, Halachah 1, and notes.</i> and the burnt offerings of fowl if there are many of them. If the southeast corner<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Which was the preferential place for them to be offered, as stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 20.</i> does not have the capacity for all of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if many fowl were being offered and it was difficult to approach that corner of the altar.</i> [the priests would] turn to the southwest corner and perform <i>melikah</i> there.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For this is close to the place where portions of the sacrifices are discarded.</i>
All of those who ascend the altar on the right [side of the ramp],<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they should turn to the southeastern corner. The rationale is that, at the outset, one should always turn to the right.</i> circle it, and descend on the left [side] except for one who ascends for one of the latter three purposes mentioned above which are performed on the upper portion<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Those who perform the tasks on the lower portion of this corner do not ascend the ramp at all, but instead, approach the altar from the ground.</i> of this corner. [Those involved in these services] ascend on the left side, turn to the left, to that corner, perform their task, and retrace their steps.<br>Why do they turn to the left? So that they will encounter the southwest corner first. For if they would turn to the right and circle the entire altar until they reached the southwest corner, the water or the wine might become smoky<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Wine that became smoky is unacceptable for a libation (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 6:9). We can assume that the same law applies with regard to water.</i> or perhaps the fowl would die because of the altar's smoke.
Therefore when someone who performs a water or wine libation circles the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">There is no necessity - or even preference - that the priest circle the altar. The matter is solely dependent on his choice. The Mishnah (<i>Tamid</i> 6:6) that is the source for this teaching speaks about a High Priest, but as the Rambam states in his Commentary to the Mishnah, the concept applies to any priest. If he desires, he may circle the altar before performing this service.</i> he should not have anything in his hands. He begins circling from the southeast corner, [proceeding] to the northeast and then to the northwest and the southwest. He should not be holding anything. When he reaches the southwest corner, the water or the wine is placed in his hand and he performs the libation. If he does not [desire to] circle [the altar], he may ascend [the ramp], turn to the left, perform his service, and descend.

Chapter 8

There is a stringency that applies with regard to an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">But not a fowl (Halachah 2).</i> brought as a sin-offering that does not apply [even] to other sacrifices of the most sacred order. If blood from an animal brought as a sin-offering will spew from the container in which the blood was received<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, it spewed forth from the animal at the time of slaughter, spluttered from the blood sprinkled to the altar, or spilled to the floor and was collected from there, these laws do not apply (Halachah 6).</i> onto a garment before [the blood] was sprinkled [on the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, once the blood was sprinkled this stringency does not apply (Halachah 7).</i> that garment is obligated to by washed with water<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> in the Temple Courtyard, as [Leviticus 6:20] states: "If its blood is spewed on a garment, that which it has been spewed upon must be washed in a holy place."
A garment made from wool or linen,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of the term <i>beged</i> (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 93b).</i> a soft hide,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5.</i> or a garment from goat's hair<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is taken from the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keilim</i> 27:1).</i> is required to be washed. A firm hide, however, is considered as wood<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4.</i> and one should scrape the blood from it.<br>[The above applies to] the blood of sin-offerings that are eaten or those which are burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachot 15-16.</i> but not to sin-offerings from fowl, as implied by [<i>ibid.</i>:18]: "the sin-offering will be slaughtered," i.e., the Torah is speaking about [an offering] that is slaughtered and not one that is killed by <i>melikah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>melikah</i> is equivalent to ritual slaughter in certain contexts, since the verse specifies "slaughter," it is excluded in this instance (Radbaz).</i>
When a sin-offering was disqualified, its blood need not be washed [from garments]. This applies whether there was a time when it could have been acceptable or there was never a time when it could have been acceptable.<br>What is meant by [an offering] that had a time when it could have been acceptable? One which was left overnight, that became impure, or that was taken outside of the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in all these instances, the ritual slaughter and the sprinkling were performed in an appropriate manner and thus the meat could have been consumed in an acceptable manner.</i> What is meant by [an offering] that never had a time when it could have been acceptable? One that was disqualified because of [the manner in which] it was slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 15:1 which mentions several ways in which ritual slaughter can be disqualified.</i> or the manner in which its blood was sprinkled.
Only the place where the blood [was absorbed] must be washed.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">And not the entire garment (<i>Zevachim</i> 93b).</i> [The above applies provided the garment or] utensil<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this requirement applies not only to garments, but also other objects made from these materials.</i> is susceptible to contract ritual impurity and is fit to be washed. If, however, the blood spews on a wooden utensil or a metal utensil, it need not be washed because it is not fit to be washed. Instead, one should merely scrape the blood off.
If [the blood] spewed onto the skin of a fish, it is not necessary to wash it, for [the skin of a fish] is not susceptible to ritual impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 1:3-4; 10:1. <i>Zevachim</i> 93b states that only an article that is susceptible to ritual impurity must be washed.</i> If it was spewed onto the hide of an animal that was not skinned, it need not be washed. If, however, [the hide] was skinned [from the animal], it must be washed. Even though it is not susceptible to ritual impurity in its present state,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not considered as a <i>k'li</i>, a useful article, in its present state.</i> it will be susceptible to ritual impurity after it has been treated.
If blood spewed from [the animal's] neck onto a garment, it sputtered from the corner of the altar [to a garment],<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since it has already been sprinkled on the altar, these laws no longer apply to it (<i>Zevachim</i> 92b).</i> or the blood spilled to the floor [of the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Without first being received in a sacred utensil. In such an instance, it is not fit to be sprinkled on the altar (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 1:25). If, however, it was first received in a sacred utensil and then spilled, it is fit to be sprinkled on the altar. Hence, if it spewed onto a garment, the garment must be washed.</i> it was gathered and then it spewed on to a garment, there is no requirement that [the garment] be washed, as [the prooftext] states: "If its blood is spewed...."<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The same Hebrew root <i>haza'ah</i> is used both for the sprinkling of the blood on the altar and the spewing of the blood on a garment, leading to the inference stated by the Rambam.</i> [Implied is that the requirement] was stated only with regard to blood that was received in a sacred utensil and is fit to be sprinkled [on the altar], [because it is] of sufficient measure [to be sprinkled].<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the gloss of the Radbaz.</i>
If the four presentations of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed in Chapter 5, Halachah 7.</i> were made and then some of the remainder of the blood spewed from the container onto a garment, it need not be washed even though the remainder of the blood was not yet poured out on the base [of the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Because pouring out the remainder of the blood is not an essential element of the sacrifice.</i> Similar principles apply with regard to the sin-offerings that are burnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., once the priest has completed the sprinkling of their blood that is required as explained in Chapter 5, Halachot 12-18, the remaining blood does not require that it be washed.</i>
[If the blood of a sin-offering] sputtered from [a priest's] finger after he performed its sprinkling with his hand, [the garment onto which it sputtered] need not be washed, because the remainder of the blood on his finger is not acceptable for sprinkling.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from Chapter 5, Halachah 8.</i>
If [the blood] spewed from one garment to another, the second garment need not be washed.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 92b compares this to the situation described in Halachah 6 when blood was spilled onto the floor and then sputtered onto a garment.</i> If [blood] spewed on an impure garment, it need not be washed.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For it is considered as if the blood became impure beforehand and thus would have been unacceptable for sprinkling. <i>Zevachim</i> 93a associates this situation with the question whether water set aside to be used for the sprinkling of the ashes of the red heifer that became impure can be purified or not. Based on that discussion, Rav Yosef Corcus suggests that there is a printing error in the text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and the ruling is that the garment must be washed. This conclusion is supported by the Rambam's ruling in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 1:36 which states that blood from consecrated animals can never contract ritual impurity.</i><br>If blood from a sin-offering sputtered onto a garment and then ordinary blood<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., blood from an animal that was not consecrated. As evident from the following clause, seemingly, the same law would apply with regard to blood from a burnt offering. Indeed, <i>Zevachim</i> 98a-b states this explicitly.</i> sputtered onto the blood from the sin-offering, it must be washed.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For the blood from the sin-offering is absorbed into it and is not washed away by the other blood.</i> If, however, ordinary blood - or even blood from a burnt-offering - sputtered onto a garment and then blood from a sin-offering sputtered on to it, it is not required to be washed, because [the blood from the sin-offering] is not absorbed in [the garment].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For it had already absorbed the other blood.</i>
When the place [stained by] the blood is washed, it should be washed very thoroughly with water until no trace [of the blood] remains. All of the seven detergents<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The saliva of a person who has not eaten, beans that have been chewed, urine that has become sour, marsh mallow, natron, glasswort, and soapwort. [The names of these detergents were taken from Rav Kappach's translation of the Arabic terms used in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Niddah</i> 9:6).]</i> that are used [to determine whether] a stain is blood or not<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the stain is removed when these seven detergents are applied to it, we conclude that it was blood.
