If it is not removed, we assume that it is paint or another type of dye.</i> should be used to [wash away] the blood of a sin-offering with the exception of urine, for urine should not be brought into the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not respectful to bring such a substance into a place where the Divine Presence is overtly revealed.<br>The commentaries have noted that <i>Zevachim</i> 95a (the source for this halachah) mentions that urine was brought into the Temple, except that it was first mixed with the saliva so that it was not taken in as an independent entity.</i>
An earthernware vessel in which a sin-offering that is to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As opposed to those which are burnt.</i> was cooked must be broken<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that the flavor of the meat of the sin-offering can never be totally purged from an earthenware vessel.</i> in the Temple Courtyard. A metal vessel in which [a sin-offering] was cooked must be cleansed<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 11:8), the Rambam defines this term as meaning "thoroughly washing a utensil until everything attached to it is removed."</i> and rinsed in water<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">There the Rambam interprets this as meaning "pouring water over the utensil without scraping the filth off by hand." The Radbaz elaborates on the difference between the Rambam's approach (who appears to require only the thorough cleaning of the vessels) and that of Rashi who interprets <i>Zevachim</i> 95b as requiring that these utensils must be purged in the same manner as one purges non-kosher food from a vessel into which it was absorbed.<br>According to this conception of the Rambam's approach, the problem is not that the fat absorbed in the utensil becomes <i>notar</i>, "sacrificial food that remained overnight," and must be destroyed. Instead, the question involves merely the thorough cleaning of the utensil. This interpretation is borne out by the continuation of the Rambam's statements in his Commentary to the Mishnah where he speaks about the issue of <i>notar</i> with regard to the food attached to the utensil, but does not mention the food absorbed within it.</i> in the Temple Courtyard, as [Leviticus 6:21] states: "An earthenware vessel in which it is cooked shall be broken."<br>Although this verse does not state "in a holy place,"<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">As does the preceding verse which mentions washing the blood from a garment.</i> the same laws that apply to washing [a garment] apply. Just as the washing must be performed in a sacred place, so too, the breaking of an earthenware vessel and the cleansing and the rinsing of a metal utensil must be performed in a holy place. [These laws apply equally to] a utensil in which [the sin-offering] was cooked and one into which it was poured while it was boiling.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the stew including the meat of the sin-offering was boiling while it was poured into the vessel, it is considered as if it was cooked there.</i>
"Cleansing" is performed with hot water and "rinsing" with cold water.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This represents the Rambam's version of <i>Zevachim, loc. cit.</i> There are, however, other versions of that Mishnah.</i> The prooftext mentions "water," [excluding] wine, wine mixed with water, or other liquids. The thorough cleansing and rinsing [of the vessel] should resemble the thorough cleansing and rinsing of a cup.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam writes that the intent of this comparison is to imply that the container must be washed until there is no trace of the previous substance, as one would a cup from which he would drink.</i><br>A spit and a grill [used to cook the meat of a sin-offering] must be purged<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">To remove the fat absorbed in the container (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> in water that is heated by fire and then washed [in cold water].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In the above source, the Rambam explains that this purging follows the same process in which non-kosher food is purged from cooking utensils. Significantly, however, when he mentions the process of purging non-kosher cooking utensils (<i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 17:3-4), he does not mention the need to rinse them in cold water although he does mention that requirement in <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 5:23.</i>
When does the above apply? When one cooked [sacrificial meat] in these utensils after their blood was sprinkled as required by law. If, however, he cooked in [these utensils] before the sprinkling [of the blood]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This would disqualify the sacrifice.</i> or he cooked meat from sin-offerings that were to be burnt in such utensils,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 11.</i> it is not necessary that they be washed thoroughly and rinsed.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz questions this ruling, because seemingly, the fat absorbed in the utensil, becomes <i>notar</i>, "sacrificial food that remained overnight," and it must be destroyed. He offers three possible resolutions:<br>a) the Rambam is speaking about an instance when the utensil was used on the same day. Hence, the problem does not arise.<br>b) the issue does not concern <i>notar</i> at all, as explained in the notes to Halachah 12.<br>c) since the absorbed fat is more than a day old when it becomes <i>notar</i>, its flavor is impaired (<i>notain taam lifgam</i>). Hence, since the prohibition against the absorbed food is merely a Rabbinic safeguard, our Sages did not apply it in this instance, because the situation involves the Temple service.</i><br>If one cooked [the meat of a sin-offering] in [only] a portion of a utensil, the entire utensil must be washed thoroughly and rinsed.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz uses this law as a further support for his idea that the fat need not be purged from the utensil. For the concept that cooked food which is absorbed in part of a utensil is considered as if it were absorbed in the entire utensil is an established principle.</i>
There is an unresolved doubt [concerning the ruling] when meat [from a sacrificial offering] was roasted in the space of an earthenware oven:<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Similar laws apply with regard to sacrifices cooked in a metal oven, except that the question involves the requirement to be thoroughly washed and rinsed.</i> Must [the oven] be destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Halachah 11.</i> since [the meat] was cooked inside of it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For the Torah mentions the necessity of washing out or destroying the utensil after sacrificial meat was cooked in it. It does not state that for the requirement to apply, the flavor of the meat must be absorbed in the utensil.</i> or need it not be destroyed, since it did not touch it?<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the above question is discussed by <i>Zevachim</i> 95b and is left unresolved by our Sages.</i> [The above] does not apply only with regard to a sin-offering. Instead, all utensils that were used for [meat] from sacrificial offerings with hot water,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., sacrificial meat was either cooked in it or poured in it while warm [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 11:8)].</i> whether sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity or sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, are required to be washed thoroughly and rinsed after<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the glosses of the Ra'avad and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.</i> the time for eating from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam quotes <i>Zevachim, loc. cit.</i>, explaining that this concept is derived as follows. Leviticus 6:21-22 states: "All of the priestly males shall partake of it" and directly afterwards states: "and it shall be washed thoroughly and rinsed in water," implying that the two activities should be performed in direct sequence.</i> Similarly, a spit and a grill should be purged after eating [from the sacrifice which they were used to cook].<br>They should not be left until one desires to eat from them a second time.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., beyond the time when it is permitted to eat from them.</i> Instead, when the time for eating from them is completed, one should purge the grill and the spit and wash thoroughly and rinse the utensil whether it be a metal utensil or an earthenware utensil.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, stating that there is no point in washing an earthenware utensil thoroughly. Even if one does so, the taste of the sacrificial offering will remain absorbed within it. The Rambam's understanding is that the Torah only required that an earthenware utensil be broken when it was used for cooking a sin-offering, if it was used for cooking other sacrifices, there is no obligation. This difference of opinion relates to a question of greater scope: the difference of opinion mentioned above whether it is necessary to purge the utensils from the food absorbed in them or not.</i> [There is] one exception: [the meat from] a sin-offering. An earthenware utensil [in which it was cooked] must be broken. [Nevertheless,] one may cook [in a utensil] and do so a second and third time immediately, whether using a metal utensil or an earthenware utensil. [The requirement to] wash it thoroughly and rinse it [applies only] at the conclusion of the time permitted to partake [from these sacrificial foods].
Utensils made from animal turds,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keilim</i> 10:1), the Rambam mentions that there are some who interpret the Hebrew term as referring to utensils made from marble. He, however, favors the translation given above.</i> stone, or earth<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to utensils made from earth that were not fired in a kiln and hence, are not governed by the laws applying to earthenware utensils.</i> are not required to be thoroughly washed and rinsed even [when the meat of] a sin-offering was cooked in them. All that is necessary is that they be cleansed.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain that since the Torah does not make any specifications with regard to such utensils (as it does with regard to earthenware and metal utensils), there is no obligation with regard to them. He does not mention wood utensils, because wood utensils do not resemble earthenware and metal.</i><br>With regard to a sin-offering, [Leviticus 6:20] states: "Anything that will touch its meat will become sanctified,"<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 12, which states that at the outset, a sin-offering should not be eaten together with other sacrifices.</i> i.e., it will be of the same status. If it has been disqualified, anything that touches it is also disqualified. If it is kosher, anything that touches it should be eaten according to the laws that apply to it, with the same degree of holiness.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it can only be eaten in the Temple Courtyard by males of the priestly family on the day the sacrifice was offered and on the following night.</i>
When does the above apply? When [the flavor of the meat of a sacrificial offering] was absorbed by it. If, however, it merely touched it, but its flavor was not absorbed, it does not cause it to become sanctified.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 97a, <i>et al</i>, derives this concept from the fact that the term the prooftext uses for "its meat," <i>bibisarah</i>, literally means "in its meat." Implied is the flavor must be absorbed into the meat of the other food.</i> The above applies both to a sin-offering and any other sacrificial offering, whether sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity or sacrifices of a severe degree of sanctity, as [implied by Leviticus 7:37]: "This is the law for the burnt-offering, the meal-offering...."<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 98b interprets this verse as teaching that there is a fundamental commonality to all the sacrificial offerings.</i>
If the meat [of a sacrificial offering] touched a cake<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A soft, spongy wafer [see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:4)].</i> and [its flavor] was absorbed in a portion of it, [the cake] does not become sanctified in its entirety. Instead, one should cut off the portion in which it was absorbed.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">And only that portion becomes sanctified.</i>
[The following laws apply to] a utensil in which sacrificial food and ordinary food were cooked together or sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity were cooked together with sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity. If there is a sufficient amount [of the sacrificial food] to impart its flavor,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Unless there is a priest who can distinguish whether the taste was imparted or not, we assume that if the food in the more lenient category is 60 times the amount of the food in the more severe category, the taste of the more severe type of food has not been imparted. Otherwise, we must be stringent (<i>Zevachim</i> 97a).</i> the entire mixture must be eaten according to the laws governing the food of the most severe category. The utensil must be thoroughly washed and rinsed according to the laws governing the food of the most severe category.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, if an earthenware vessel was used to cook meat from a sin-offering, according to the Rambam, it must be broken. According to Rashi and others, this applies whenever sacrificial offerings are cooked in an earthenware utensil (Radbaz).</i> If it did not impart its flavor to them, the foods of the more lenient category need not be eaten according to the laws governing the food of the most severe category and they are not governed by their laws at all. The utensil [in which the mixture was cooked], however, must be thoroughly washed and rinsed.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when meat from sacrifices of the most severe degree of sanctity is cooked together with a larger quantity of meat from sacrifices of lesser sanctity, the utensils need not be purged at the time the commandment to partake of the sacrifices of the most severe degree of sanctity is concluded, because that meat is considered insignificant, due to the majority of the other meat. Nevertheless, when the time to partake of the sacrifices of the lesser degree of holiness is concluded, the utensils must be thoroughly washed and rinsed (<i>ibid.</i>). When sacrificial meat is cooked together with ordinary meat, even if its taste is not recognizable, we require that the utensils be thoroughly washed and rinsed, because of the stringencies involving sacrificial food (Radbaz).</i>
When the blood of a sin-offering sputtered on a garment and then that garment was taken out of the Temple Courtyard, it should be returned to the Temple Courtyard and washed there.<br>What should be done if [the garment] became impure outside the Temple Courtyard?<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">And thus it would not be permitted to return it to the Temple Courtyard in its present state [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 11:6)].</i> It should be torn<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to both ordinary garments and priestly garments with the exception of the cloak. Although it is forbidden to tear priestly garments with a destructive intent (<i>Hilchot K'lei Hamikdash</i> 9:3), tearing it for this purpose is not considered as tearing it with a destructive intent.</i> so that it will become pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Kelim</i> 23:11, when the majority of an impure garment is torn, the remnants are considered as ritually pure.</i> He should then bring it into [the Temple Courtyard] and wash it there. He must leave intact a portion of the garment the size of a handkerchief, for [the relevant verse]<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 6:20.</i> speaks of a "garment," i.e., a garment must be washed.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Anything smaller than a handkerchief could not be justifiably called a garment.</i> Even though [the remnants of the garment] are impure due to Rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">And thus one might think that they should not be brought into the Temple Courtyard.</i> because of the remnant the size of a handkerchief, since the majority of it is torn, it is ritually pure according to Scriptural Law and it is permitted to bring it into the Sanctuary to wash out the blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., our Sages did not enforce their decree in this instance, because doing so would lead to the nullification of a Scriptural obligation.</i>
What should be done when blood from a sin-offering sputtered on [the High Priest's] cloak,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Concerning which there is an explicit prohibition not to tear it (<i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash, loc. cit.</i>). Hence the advice given in the previous halachah is not relevant.</i> it was taken out [of the Temple Courtyard], and became impure? [The difficulty is that] one who tears it is liable for lashes, as we explained. He should bring it into [the Temple Courtyard] less than three fingerbreadths at a time<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">A portion of cloth less than three fingerbreadths wide is not considered as a garment. Hence, the laws of ritual impurity do not apply to it. Although in this instance, the cloak is intact and hence, is larger than this measure, since there is no alternative, this provision is allowed.</i> and wash it in [the Temple Courtyard]. After all the blood has been washed off it little by little, it should be immersed [in a <i>mikveh</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">To purify it.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard].
When an earthen-ware utensil in which a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz maintains that this law applies to the meat of a sin-offering, but not the meat of other offerings.</i> was cooked was taken out of the [Temple] Courtyard, it should be brought back in and broken there.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 94b explains that this concept is derived from the laws governing the washing of a garment mentioned in Halachah 19.</i> If it became impure outside the Temple Courtyard, it should be perforated to the extent that a small root [could protrude through it] so that it will be ritually pure,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 95a explains that "a utensil" must be able to contain liquids and if has a hole, it is no longer fit for such a purpose. Thus once the utensil has been broken, it is ritually pure. (This represents the Scriptural Law. With regard to Rabbinic Law, see <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 19:2.)</i> and then bring it back inside [the Temple Courtyard] and break it there. If it is broken with a larger hole, it should not be broken in [the Temple Courtyard], because only utensils are broken there.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">And if it is broken to a greater extent, it is not considered as a utensil at all and therefore should not be brought into the Temple Courtyard.<br>Why was a utensil broken to the extent that a root could project through it allowed to be brought into the Temple Courtyard? Since our Sages considered it a utensil in certain contexts, they allowed it to be considered a utensil so that the obligation to break utensils in the Temple Courtyard could be fulfilled.</i><br>Similarly, when a metal utensil in which [sin-offerings] were cooked was taken out of the [Temple] Courtyard, it should be brought back in and thoroughly washed and rinsed there. If it became impure when it was taken out, its [bottom] should be opened<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">With a large hole (see <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 11:1-2).</i> until it becomes pure<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Because it is no longer fit to be served as a utensil.</i> and then it should be brought back inside [the Temple Courtyard] [The metal] should then be flattened so that the opening becomes closed as is the form of utensils.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 12:1 states that when a metal utensil that had regained ritual purity, because it had been opened, becomes closed again, it reverts to being ritually impure. This, however, is a Rabbinic safeguard and our Sages did not uphold their decree in this instance so that the practice of purging the utensils could be fulfilled.</i> Afterwards, it should be thoroughly washed and rinsed in the Temple Courtyard, as [Leviticus 6:21] states: "If [it was cooked] in a copper utensil, [it should be thoroughly washed and rinsed with water]." [Implied is that] only "utensils" are thoroughly washed in the Temple Courtyard.

Chapter 9

It is a positive commandment to offer the guilt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 65) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 140) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> according to its statutes as they are written in the Torah. How are the guilt-offerings brought? Both the definite guilt offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i>, Chapter 9, for a description of the transgressions for which these sacrifices are brought.</i> and the conditional guilt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i>, Chapter 8, for a description of the situations which warrant bringing these sacrifices.</i> should be slaughtered and their blood should be sprinkled on the altar, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2, with regard to slaughter, and Halachah 6 of that chapter with regard to sprinkling the blood on the altar.</i> They are skinned,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 18.</i> the portions offered on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The fats, the lobe of liver, and the kidneys described in Leviticus 7:3-4.</i> are removed, salted,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For every element of a sacrifice offered on the altar must be salted (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:11).</i> and tossed on [the altar's] pyre. If one desires to carry [these portions] to the altar in a [sacred] utensil, he may. The remainder of the meat is eaten by males of the priestly family according to [the laws that govern the consumption of] sin-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For Leviticus 7:7 establishes an equation between the guilt-offering and the sin-offering. See also Chapter 10, Halachah 3.</i>
There is a difference with regard to [the laws governing] the acceptance of the blood of the guilt offering brought by a person who had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A skin affliction similar to, but not identical to leprosy, that afflicts people, their clothes, and their homes because of spiritual inadequacies, in particular speaking <i>lashon hara</i>, uncomplimentary gossip (the conclusion of <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Tzara'at</i>).</i> as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 4:2.</i> Nevertheless, all of its other procedures, the sprinkling of its blood on the altar, and its consumption are analogous to that required for other guilt-offerings in all regards.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This sacrifice is given as an example of one of the Thirteen Principles of Biblical Exegesis taught by Rabbi Yishmael (<i>Sifra</i> 1:4), It describes an entity (the guilt offering brought by a person afflicted by <i>tzara'at</i>) that was once included in a general category (all guilt offerings), but was singled out with regard to a new stipulation (that its blood be received in a different manner). Hence the laws that apply to that general category apply to it only because there is an explicit verse (Leviticus 14:13  that returns it to the general category.</i>
It is a positive commandment to offer all the peace-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 66) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 141) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as commanded. There are four types: one is the communal peace-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The two lambs offered on Shavuot (Leviticus 23:19 .</i> and the [other] three are individual peace offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that there is a difference between peace-offerings and other sacrifices. Most of the peace-offerings are brought voluntarily, while most of the other offerings are brought due to an obligation.</i>
What is the procedure for bringing the communal peace offerings? [The sacrificial lambs] should be slaughtered and their blood should be sprinkled on the altar, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2, with regard to slaughter, and Halachah 6 of that chapter with regard to sprinkling the blood on the altar.</i> They are skinned,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 18.</i> the portions offered on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The fats, the lobe of liver, and the kidneys described in Leviticus 7:3-4.</i> are removed, salted,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For every element of a sacrifice offered on the altar must be salted (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:11).</i> and brought to be consumed by [the altar's] pyre. The remainder [of the meat] is eaten by males of the priestly family according to [the laws that govern the consumption of] a sin-offering and a guilt-offering, for these [sacrifices] are also sacrifices of the most sacred order, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Halachah 17; Chapter 5, Halachah 3.</i>
There are three types of individual peace-offerings:<br>a) a peace-offering that is brought without bread, e.g., the festive peace-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Brought when making the festive pilgrimages (<i>Hilchot Chagigah</i> 1:1).</i> or the peace-offering of celebration,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Additional peace-offerings brought at the time of the festive pilgrimages (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> they are called peace-offerings;<br>b) peace-offerings brought with bread because of a vow or a pledge;<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The term "vow" refers to a promise to bring a sacrifice. The term "pledge" refers to a promise to bring a particular animal as a sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 1:2).</i> these are called thanksgiving offerings and the bread is called the bread of the thanksgiving offering;<br>c) the peace-offering brought by a nazirite on the day he completes his nazirite vow; this offering is accompanied by bread and is called the nazirite's ram.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nezirut</i> 8:1.</i>
What is the procedure for bringing these three [types of offerings]? [The sacrificial animals] should be slaughtered and their blood should be sprinkled on the altar, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 2, with regard to slaughter, and Halachah 6 of that chapter with regard to sprinkling the blood on the altar.</i> They are skinned<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 18.</i> and the portions offered on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The fats, the lobe of liver, and the kidneys described in Leviticus 7:3-4.</i> are removed. Afterwards, the meat is cut up and the breast and the right thigh are set aside.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Ultimately, they will be given to the priests as stated in Halachah 12.</i> The portions to be offered together with the breast and the thigh are placed on the hands of the owners. A priest places his hands below the hands of the owner and performs <i>tenufah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the following halachah.</i> with all these items "before God," to the east [of the Altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 61a. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 5:6), the Rambam writes: "With regard to the guilt-offering brought by a person afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i>..., it is written (Leviticus 14:12 : 'And <i>tenufah</i> should be performed with them before God.' A tradition has been received interpreting 'before God' as meaning 'in the east.'<br>Rashi (<i>loc. cit.</i>) interprets this to mean that even the area to the east of the altar is considered as "before God," but the area to the west certainly warrants that description. The Rambam does not accept this understanding. His interpretation is questioned by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others, for the Holy of Holies was to the west of the altar. Seemingly, the closer one comes to it, the more one is "before God."</i> Whenever there is a requirement for <i>tenufah</i>, it is performed to the east [of the Altar].
How is <i>tenufah</i> performed? [The items] are taken [to each of the directions]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed addition is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 5:5). This is also the perspective of Rashi (<i>Menachot</i> 62a) based on our Sages' statements that this action is intended to prevent destructive winds.</i> and returned, lifted up and brought low.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This is intended to prevent harmful dews (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> If the sacrifice was a thanksgiving offering, one should be taken from each [of the four] groups of ten breads that are brought with it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 17-22 for a description of the breads which would accompany the thanksgiving offering.</i> and placed together with the breast, the thigh, and the portions offered on the altar. <i>Tenufah</i> should be performed with all of these items upon the owner's hands, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous halachah.</i>
How are they placed on the owner's hands? The fats are placed on the owner's hands with the breast and the thigh above them. The two kidneys and the lobe of the liver are placed above them and if [the offering includes] bread, it is placed above them<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In this way, the bread will not be spoiled through excessive contact with the meat (Radbaz). In his gloss (based on <i>Menachot</i> 61b), he cites exegetical reasons why the other items are placed in the order mentioned.</i> and <i>tenufah</i> is performed with all these items.
If the sacrifice was a nazirite's ram, [the priest] should remove the portions to be offered on the altar, set aside the breast and the thigh, and cook the remainder of the ram in the Women's Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">More particularly, as mentioned in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:8, there was a chamber in the southeast portion of the Women's Courtyard set aside for this purpose.</i> The priest takes the cooked foreleg from the ram<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As commanded by Numbers 6:19. The Radbaz explains that it would appear that the foreleg would be placed above the other portions of the sacrifice, but below the bread.</i> and one from each [of the two] groups of ten breads that are brought with it,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 23.</i> together with the breast, the thigh, and the portions offered on the altar and places everything on the nazirite's hands. The priest places his hands under the owner's hands and moves all [items] as we described.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous two halachot.</i>
What is meant by the breast? The portion [of the animal's body] that faces the ground that extends from the neck until the belly. Two ribs on either side should be cut off [and given to the priest] together with it. What is meant by the foreleg? The portion from the upper-joint until the ankle joint; two limbs, one connected with the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">An animal's foreleg is comprised of three bones. According to the Rambam, the lower two are given to the priest. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Chulin</i> 10:4). Others maintain the upper two should be given to the priest.</i> The foreleg that is mentioned refers to the right foreleg. The corresponding portion in the rear leg is the thigh that is referred to universally.
After <i>tenufah</i> is performed with [these portions], they are offered on the pyre of the altar except for the breast and the thigh that are eaten by the priests,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 4.</i> as [Leviticus 7:34] states: "but the breast with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed and the thigh that was lifted up...." The remainder of the peace-offerings are consumed by the owner.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">And any - both male and female - with whom he desires to share the meat.</i> The priests do not acquire the breast and the thigh until after the portions to be offered on the altar were placed on its pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 59b derives this law from the order in which the concepts are stated in the Torah. Similarly, the owner may not partake of his portion until that time (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 18:7).</i>
Similarly, the bread with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed from the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram and the cooked foreleg are eaten by the priests. The remainder of the bread and the remainder of the meat are eaten by the owner. The bread with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed together with the breast and the thigh is referred to<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:21; <i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i> 1:15.</i> as "the elevated portion from the thanksgiving offering." The cooked foreleg together with the breast and the thigh and the bread with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed are referred to as "the elevated portion from the nazirite's ram."
There is an unresolved doubt whether the bread from the elevated portion of the thanksgiving offering<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">There is, however, no such doubt with regard to the elevated portion of the nazirite's ram (Radbaz).</i> is categorized as <i>terumah</i> or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For Leviticus 7:14 uses the word <i>terumah</i> when describing this offering. Nevertheless, since we find exclusions with regard to penalties associated with <i>terumah</i> in other verses, it is possible that they do not apply with regard to these breads. Accordingly, <i>Menachot</i> 77b leaves the matter unresolved.</i> Therefore one is not liable for death,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 6:6.</i> nor to repay an additional fifth, [as one is when partaking of] <i>terumah</i>. Nor is a mixture of it subject to the laws of <i>dimua</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 13:1-2, in such an instance, one hundred times the amount of <i>terumah</i> is required before the mixture is permitted.</i> as is a mixture of <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> note that if these breads become mixed with ordinary breads, seemingly, there is a question whether a Scriptural prohibition applies and one should rule stringently. They explain that as long as there is a majority of permitted substances, the Scriptural prohibition is considered as nullified and the prohibition is only Rabbinic in origin (see <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:13). This also applies with regard to a mixture of <i>terumah</i> (<i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 14:7). Accordingly, in this instance, since it is possible that the laws of <i>terumah</i> are not applied to these breads, we do not impose the Rabbinic prohibition.</i>
If the person bringing the thanksgiving offering was a priest, the remainder of the bread may be eaten by the owner like a thanksgiving offering brought by an Israelite. For the bread that accompanies a thanksgiving offering or a nazrite's ram is not called a meal offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As will be stated (Chapter 12, Halachah 9), in contrast to the meal offerings brought by Israelites, meal offerings brought by the priests are consumed entirely by the altar's pyre. Hence, the Rambam felt it necessary to clarify that these breads are not in that category.</i>
When two people bring a peace-offering in partnership, one should perform <i>tenufah</i> with the other's permission.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the two partners would perform <i>tenufah</i> each one holding part of the items, neither would be performing the rite as required. If one put his hand below the hand of the other one, there would be an interposition between that person's hand and the sacrificial items. And it is also impossible for them to perform <i>tenufah</i>, one after the other, because the Torah speaks about <i>tenufah</i>, using the singular, and not <i>tenufot</i>, using the plural (<i>Menachot</i> 94a).</i> Even if there are 100 [partners], one should perform <i>tenufah</i> for the sake of all of them. This does not apply with regard to <i>semichah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">That rite must be performed by each of the partners individually (Chapter 3, Halachah 9).</i>
When a woman is the one bringing a sacrifice, she does not perform <i>tenufah</i> with it. The priest must perform that rite,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz suggests that if she is married, her husband should perform this rite on her behalf.</i> for the sacrifice requires that <i>tenufah</i> be performed with it and a woman is unacceptable to perform that rite. A woman never performs <i>tenufah</i> except in two instances: a <i>sotah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A woman suspected of adultery who is required to bring an offering as part of her process of atonement.</i> and a female nazirite, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 3:15; <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 8:4.</i> <i>Tenufah</i> should always be performed before [the elements of the sacrifice] are brought close to the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 61a derives this concept from Numbers 5:25 which speaks of <i>tenufah</i> being performed with a <i>sotah's</i> offering and then of it being brought to the altar.</i>
What is meant by the bread that is brought together with the thanksgiving offering? One should take 20 <i>isaronim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>isaron</i> is defined as a measure equivalent to the volume of 43 and 1/5 eggs.</i> of fine flour. He should make ten <i>isaronim</i> leavened and ten unleavened. The ten that are made leaven should be made into ten loaves.
How are they made leavened? He should bring enough yeast to cause the dough to leaven and place it in the measure of an <i>isaron</i>. He then fills the measure. Even though ultimately, [the measure] will be lacking or excessive because of the yeast,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the space intended for the flour will be taken by the yeast.</i> for at times [the yeast] will be thick and hard<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">And thus take up only a small amount of space.</i> and at times it will be soft [and inflated],<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">And thus take up a lot of space.</i> we are concerned only with its measure at the present time. Hence he should measure full <i>isaronim</i>.<br>From the ten <i>isaronim</i> for the unleavened bread, he should make 30 loaves of the same size, ten of each [of the following three] types: ten loaves baked in an oven, ten loaves of flat-cakes, and ten loaves of fried cakes.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 9:3), the Rambam writes that he has found no definition for the term <i>murbechet</i>, but that appears to him that it implies that a large quantity of oil is used in the preparation of the cakes.</i>
How are the fried cakes prepared? [The cakes] should be scalded with boiling water.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:6; <i>Menachot</i> 9:3), where he describes how these fried cakes are made.</i> Then they should be baked slightly and then fried in oil in a roasting pot or the like, like doughnuts and fried. A large amount of oil is used for them. This is the deep frying process mentioned in all places.
How much oil is used to prepare these 30 loaves?<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Those loaves which are leavened do not require oil [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 7:4)].</i> Half a <i>log</i> of oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A half a <i>log</i> is 172 cc. according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and 300 cc. according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> This measure is a halachah communicated to Moses from Sinai. A fourth is used for the fried doughballs, an eighth for the loaves [baked in the oven], and an eighth for the wafers.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz maintains that the measure of half a <i>log</i> was communicated to Moses, but the breakdown of how this measure should be used was not. Hence, after the fact, the sacrifice is not disqualified if there was some deviation.</i>
With regard to the loaves [that are baked]: their flour should saturate in the eighth [of a <i>log</i>]. Afterwards, they should be kneaded and baked. The oil should be poured over the wafers after they have been baked.<br>The priest takes four loaves from the entire [mixture], one of each type, as [Leviticus 7:14] states: "One from each [type], a sacrifice."
When one made [only] four loaves for the bread for the thanksgiving offering, he has fulfilled his obligation. [The Torah] mentions 40 only as [the optimum way of fulfilling] the mitzvah. [This applies] provided he separates a <i>challah</i> from each of the types of sacrifices while they are still dough.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Although the person desires to bake only four loaves, he must also separate one loaf of each type for the priest. This separation must be done beforehand, as the Rambam precedes to explain. Thus one tenth of each type of dough must be separated and prepared for the priest.</i> For a piece of bread may not be separated [as a sacrificial portion].<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it would not be deferential to give the priest a piece of each of the four doughs as his sacrificial portion.</i> [This is implied by the prooftext:] "One from each [type], a sacrifice," that the priest should not receive a portion.
What is meant by the bread that is brought together with the nazirite's ram? He should take six and two thirds <i>isaronim</i> of flour and make 20 equal sized loaves from them. They must all be unleavened bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The nazirite is bringing two thirds of the unleavened bread brought in connection with a thanksgiving offering. Hence, he uses two thirds the amount of flour.</i> Ten should be wafers with oil poured over them and ten loaves whose flour was saturated in oil. The entire amount should be baked in an oven. A fourth [of a <i>log</i>] of oil is used for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a similar amount of oil is used as is used for the corresponding loaves of the thanksgiving offering.</i> This measure is a halachah communicated to Moses at Sinai. The priest takes two of the loaves, one from each type.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Numbers 6:19.</i>
Each of the two types of bread [brought by] a Nazirite and the four types of bread brought for the thanksgiving offering is an absolute necessity.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if any of the types of bread are lacking, the sacrifice is unacceptable and nothing should be brought at all.</i> The bread for both these offerings is prepared outside the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In his gloss to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 3:7), <i>Tosafot Yom Tov</i> writes that these breads were baked in <i>Beit Pagi</i>, a small settlement, outside, but close to the Temple Mount.</i>
How are the firstborn, tithe, and Paschal sacrifices offered? After their blood is poured on the altar as we explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 5, Halachah 17.</i> they are skinned, the portions offered on the altar are removed,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 5, Halachah 18.</i> salted, and placed on [the altar's] pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 1:14; <i>Hilchot Bechorot</i> 1:2; 6:4.</i> The remainder of the meat of the firstborn offering is eaten by the priests. The remainder of the meat of the tithe sacrifice is eaten by the owner. The remainder of the meat of the Paschal sacrifice is eaten by those enumerated upon it according to its laws, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot [Korban] Pesach.</i>

Chapter 10

It is a positive commandment for the sin offerings and the guilt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 89) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 102) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> to be eaten,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This mitzvah is incumbent on the priests; it is not a dimension of the obligation to bring a given sacrifice.</i> as [Exodus 29:33] states: "And they shall eat [the sacrifices] which convey atonement."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The choice of this prooftext and the concept it teaches sheds light on an issue debated by the commentaries: Is it a positive mitzvah to partake of the meat of the sacrifices of lesser sanctity? In his <i>Sefer HaMitzvot, loc. cit.</i>, the Rambam explains that since eating the other sacrifices does not bring about atonement, it should not be considered as an independent mitzvah. Instead, it is an extension of this mitzvah. In this instance, however, since the atonement of the person bringing the sacrifice depends on the priests' consumption of the sacrifice, it is considered as an independent mitzvah. There is extensive discussion on this issue by other Rabbis.</i> The priests eat the sacrifices and the owners receive atonement. This also applies to other sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., the communal peace offerings.</i> that are eaten by the priests; partaking of them is a mitzvah.
Similarly, partaking of the portion that remains from the meal offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after the handful has been taken out to be offered on the altar. See Chapter 12, Halachah 9.</i> is a positive commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 88) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 134) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. This mitzvah encompasses partaking of all the different types of meal offerings (Radbaz).</i> as [Leviticus 6:9] states: "What remains of it shall be eaten by Aaron and his sons."
The sin-offering, the guilt-offering, and the remnants of the meal-offering may be eaten only by males<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Even minors; see Halachah 17.</i> of the priestly family in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 5, there is a prohibition against partaking of these sacrifices outside the Temple Courtyard.</i> If they were eaten in the Temple building, their consumption is [acceptable], as [indicated by Numbers 18:9-10]: "For all of their meal-offerings, for all of their sin-offerings, and for all of their guilt-offerings.... In the most holy place,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This includes the Temple building.</i> you shall partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 63a asks: From that verse, it would appear that it is desirable (not merely acceptable after the fact) to partake of the offerings inside the Temple building. Our Sages then cite Leviticus 6:9 which indicates that the sacrifices should be eaten in the Temple Courtyard. They explain that it is preferable to consider the Temple Courtyard the primary place where the sacrifices are eaten, for the Temple building is the resting place for the Divine Presence and it is not befitting for that to be the primary place for eating, an activity that is essentially a human need.</i> All males shall partake of it." Similarly, the communal peace-offerings are similar to the sin-offerings and the guilt-offerings, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 9, Halachah 4.</i>
The breast and the thigh of the peace-offerings are eaten by both males and females of the priestly family, for with regard to these [Numbers 18:11] states: "I have given it to you, to your sons and your daughters." This also applies to the elevated portions of the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The breads and the portions of meat given to the priest from those offerings; see Chapter 9, Halachah 12.</i> [<i>ibid.</i>:19] states: "All of the elevated portions of the consecrated [offerings] that the children of Israel shall raise up have I given to you, your sons, and your daughters."
The same applies with regard to the firstborn offering, for concerning it [<i>ibid.</i>:18] states: "Their meat shall be yours, like the breast with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed and the right thigh."<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are comparable to the peace-offering.</i><br>All of these<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the portions from the sacrifices of lesser sanctity, including the breast and the thigh of the peace offering and the firstborn offering.</i> [portions of sacrificial meat] that may be eaten by the women of the priestly family may be eaten by the priests' servants and their wives, like <i>terumah</i>. All [these sacrifices]<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Not only the portions given to the priests, but also those given to the Israelites.</i> may be eaten throughout the city [of Jerusalem],<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 11, Halachah 6, which delineates the prohibition against eating such sacrificial meat outside of Jerusalem.</i> as [Leviticus 10:14] states: "And the breast with which <i>tenufah</i> was performed and the thigh that was lifted up shall you eat in a pure place." It was not specified that they [be eaten] "in a holy place," which would mean "the Temple Courtyard," but "in a pure place." This refers to the camp of the Israelites.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As related in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 7:11, while the Jews were journeying through the desert, their encampment was considered as divided into three camps: the Camp of the Divine Presence, the Camp of the Levites, and the Camp of the Israelites.</i> The parallel with regard to future generations is the city of Jerusalem.<br>The same laws apply to the tithe offering and the Paschal sacrifice, for they are sacrifices of lesser sanctity like the peace-offerings. The windows and the thickness of the wall is considered as being within [the city].<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:9. See also parallel rulings in <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 2:16; <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 9:1.<br>The Radbaz clarifies that this is referring to instances where the windows are open to the city. If they are open only to the area outside the wall, he maintains that they should be considered as outside the city.</i>
The peace-offerings may be eaten on the day they were slaughtered, throughout the [following] night, and throughout the following day until sunset, as [Leviticus 7:16-18] states: "On the day when his sacrifice was offered, it shall be eaten and on its morrow... If one would partake of the peace-offering on the third day...."<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The verse continues "he shall bear his sin," implying that there is a prohibition in partaking of the sacrifice at that time.</i> Thus one can derive that they may be eaten for two days and one night. [This applies both] to the portion of the priests and the portions of the owners. This also applies to the firstborn and tithe offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">But not the Paschal sacrifice, as stated in <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 8:1, 15.</i> for they are also sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, like the peace-offerings.
The thanksgiving-offering, by contrast, even though it is one of the sacrifices of lesser sanctity, may be eaten only on the day of its sacrifice and the [following] night, as [<i>ibid.</i>:15] states: "It shall be eaten on the day it was offered. Do not leave anything from it until the morning." This also applies to the nazirite's ram and the bread which accompanies [these offerings], both to the portion of the priests and the portion of the owner. This also applies to the sin-offering, the guilt-offering, the communal peace-offerings, and the remainder of the meal-offerings. All of these are eaten for a day and [the following] night, as it is written: "It shall be eaten on the day it was offered."<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Rambam understands the verse as referring to all sacrifices unless it is specifically stated otherwise.</i> This appears to include all of the sacrifices with the exception of the peace-offerings concerning which the Torah explicitly [stated otherwise] and the firstborn and tithe offerings which resemble the peace-offerings.
According to Scriptural Law, all of these [sacrifices] that may be eaten on the day [of their sacrifice] and the [following] night may be eaten until dawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The first appearance of the sun's rays - but not the sun itself - on the eastern hemisphere. According to different halachic views, it is between 72 minutes and two hours before sunrise.</i> In order to separate a person from sin, our Sages said that they may only be eaten until midnight.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if a person would be given the entire night, he might become lax and delay the consumption of the meat and come to partake of the meat after dawn. Giving him a more restricted time serves as a safeguard to prevent the violation of this limit. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Berachot</i> 1:1).</i>
All of the sacrifices - both those of the highest degree of sanctity and those of a lesser degree - may be eaten only by those who are ritually pure<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, as stated in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 18:13, partaking of sacrificial meat in a state of ritual impurity violates a Scriptural prohibition. See also <i>Hilchot Chagigah</i>4.</i> and who are circumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 19:4, sec. 69, the prohibition against partaking of sacrificial meat while uncircumcised is an extension of the prohibition against partaking of <i>terumah</i> while uncircumcised. See <i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 7:10; <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 9:7.</i> Even if the duration of the person's impurity has concluded,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The literal meaning of the words used by the Rambam is "his sun has set." For in most instances, it is at sunset when the duration of the term of a person's ritual impurity ends.</i> but he has not brought the [necessary] atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i>, ch. 1, which explains that a <i>zav</i>, a <i>zavah</i>, a person who had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> and a woman who gave birth or miscarried must immerse themselves in a <i>mikveh</i> (or in a stream) to regain ritual purity. Nevertheless, they may not partake of sacrifices until they bring the offerings required of them. See also <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 18:14.</i> he may not partake of sacrificial foods. A <i>tumtum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A person whose gender is masked by an extra piece of flesh.</i> may not partake of sacrificial foods, because there is an unresolved doubt whether he is uncircumcised.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if he is in fact male, circumcision will not have been performed upon him.</i> It appears to me that an <i>androgynus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">One with both a male and female sexual organ.</i> may partake of sacrifices of lesser sanctity.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For these sacrifices may be eaten by both males and females. Although there is a halachic uncertainty with regard to the definition of his gender (see <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 2:10), that should not prevent him from partaking of these sacrifices. He may not, however, partake of sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity, for these may only be eaten by males and he is not categorically defined as male.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> clarifies that this leniency applies only when the male organ of the <i>androgynus</i> has been circumcised.</i>
It is permitted to eat sacrificial meat together with any other food. Even the priests are permitted to eat their portions - both from the sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity and those of a lesser degree - together with any other food.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight difficulty with the Rambam's ruling, as noted by Rav Yosef Corcus in his gloss to the following halachah, for sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity may only be eaten in the Temple Courtyard and ordinary food may not be brought into the Temple Courtyard. Rashi (<i>Temurah</i> 23a) explains that the intent is that one may partake of ordinary food outside the Temple Courtyard and then enter the Temple Courtyard to partake of the sacrificial food. <i>Tosafot</i> explains that the prohibition against bringing ordinary food into the Temple Courtyard applies only when it is not being brought there for a purpose connected with the Temple service. If, however, it is being brought there to be eaten together with the sacrifices, there is no prohibition.</i> And they may change the manner [in which it is prepared] to be eaten, eating them roasted, lightly cooked, or thoroughly cooked<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Paschal sacrifice may only be eaten while roasted, but that is a specific obligation that does not apply to other sacrifices.</i> and to spice them with spices that are not consecrated. They may not, however, spice them with spices that are <i>terumah</i>, lest this cause the <i>terumah</i> to be disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices may be eaten only for a limited amount of time as mentioned above (Halachot 6-7), while there is no limit on the time <i>terumah</i> may be eaten. Thus if the sacrifices were cooked with <i>terumah</i>, the <i>terumah</i> could be disqualified with the sacrifices and the time in which it could be eaten would be reduced.</i><br>The bones that remain are permitted [to be used for any purpose].<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this is not considered as a desecration of consecrated articles (Radbaz). This applies provided there are not any portions of the bones that are fit to be eaten (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> A person may make any utensil he desires from them.
If there was only a small amount [of sacrificial meat], ordinary food and <i>terumah</i> should be eaten with it so that it will be eaten in a satisfying manner.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not befitting for one to leave his master's table when he is hungry (<i>Tosafot, Pesachim</i> 120a).</i> If there is a large amount [of sacrificial meat], ordinary food and <i>terumah</i> should not be eaten with it so that one will not have overeaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">For that is also not becoming.</i> Similar concepts apply with regard to the remainder of the meal offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that other foods may be eaten with it if there is only a small amount (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Indeed, the verse from which the above concepts are derived (Leviticus 6:9  concerns the meal offerings.</i>
A sin-offering and a guilt-offering should not be cooked together with the elevated portion of the thanksgiving-offering or the elevated portion of the nazirite's ram because doing so restricts the type of people able to partake of them<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A sin-offering and a guilt-offering may only be eaten by male priests, while the elevated portions of the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram may also be eaten by their wives, their daughters, and their servants (Radbaz).</i> and the place where they can be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A sin-offering and a guilt-offering may only be eaten in the Temple Courtyard, while the elevated portions of the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram may also be eaten throughout the city of Jerusalem (<i>ibid.</i>).<br>Needless to say, one may not cook a sin-offering or a guilt-offering with the portion of the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram to be eaten by the owner, for doing so certainly places restrictions on that meat, preventing even the owner from partaking of it.</i> Nor may one [cook] the elevated portion of the thanksgiving-offering or the elevated portion of the nazirite's ram with the breast and thigh of the peace-offering because doing so restricts the time in which they may be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The elevated portions of the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram may be eaten only for a day and a night, while the breast and thigh of the peace-offering may be eaten on the following day as well (<i>ibid.</i>).<br>The same concepts apply with regard to cooking the meat of the thanksgiving offering or the nazirite's ram together with the meat of a peace-offering. It is only that since the Rambam had been speaking about the portions eaten by the priests, he continued to do so.</i> Nor may the elevated portion of yesterday's peace-offering [be cooked] with a sin-offering or a guilt-offering brought today because doing so restricts the type of people able to partake of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For sin-offering and a guilt-offering may only be eaten by male priests, while the peace-offerings may be eaten by Israelites and women (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> the place where they can be eaten,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A sin-offering and a guilt-offering may only be eaten in the Temple Courtyard, while the peace-offering may also be eaten throughout the city of Jerusalem (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> and the time in which they may be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Here the restrictions are reversed for the peace offering of the previous day may only be eaten until sunset, while the sin-offering and the guilt-offering may also be eaten until midnight of the following night (<i>ibid.</i>).</i><br>One may, however, cook sin-offerings and guilt-offerings together, the thanksgiving offering and the nazirite's ram together, and the firstborn offering and the breast and the thigh [of the thanksgiving offering] together.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In all of these instances, the pairs of offerings mentioned may be eaten by the same type of individual, in the same places, and for the same duration of time.</i><br>If one cooked a piece [of meat] from the sacrifices of the most sacred order,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Which may only be eaten by priests.</i> one that was <i>piggul</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Meat from a sacrifice that was slaughtered with the intent to sprinkle its blood, offer the portions required to be offered on the altar's pyre, or partake of its meat at an improper time (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 13:1).</i> or one that was <i>notar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Meat that remained after the required time for partaking of this type of sacrificial meat passed (<i>ibid.</i> 18:10).</i> with other pieces of sacrificial meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">There are others who include sacrificial meat that became impure in the list of prohibitions mentioned here and indeed this is the standard text of the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 2:16) which serves as the source for this halachah. The Rambam does not, however, include that particular in his version of the mishnah. The rationale for this distinction is discussed by the commentaries. See the notes to the following halachah.</i> those other pieces are forbidden to non-priests and permitted to priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>), the Rambam explains that this is speaking about an instance when there is sixty times more permitted meat than the piece of meat that is <i>piggul</i> or <i>notar</i> and that forbidden piece of meat was removed. See <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:5-6 with regard to the latter points. This addition satisfies the objections raised by the Ra'avad.<br>According to this interpretation, the fundamental point of this ruling is that the meat is permitted only to priests and not to non-priests. Although the prohibitions against <i>piggul</i> and <i>notar</i> have been nullified since the sacrificial meat was originally to be eaten only by priests, that restriction still remains. There are, however, others who interpret the mishnah differently.</i>
When meat from the sacrifices of the most sacred order or sacrifices of lesser sanctity is cooked together with ordinary meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The term the Rambam uses literally translates as "the meat of desire," i.e., meat that there is no mitzvah to partake of.</i> the ordinary meat is forbidden to those who are ritually impure<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Because it has absorbed the flavor of the sacrificial meat. The Radbaz states that even if there is enough ordinary meat to nullify the presence of the sacrificial meat, the prohibition against eating the sacrificial meat in a state of ritual impurity is not nullified.</i> and permitted to those who are ritually pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam states in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 2:17), the intent is that if the sacrificial meat was from sacrifices of the most sacred order, the ordinary meat may be eaten by priests. If it was of sacrifices of lesser sanctity, it may be eaten by ordinary individuals, provided they are ritually pure.<br>In one of his responsa, the Radbaz mentions that only the ordinary meat is permitted to be eaten, the sacrificial meat is forbidden, because it is considered to have become ritually impure because of its contact with the ordinary meat. See <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 11:5. In his Commentary to the Mishnah, however, the Rambam states that "everything may be eaten by pure priests" or "pure non-priests."</i>
With regard to a burnt-offering, [Leviticus 7:8] states: "The hide of the burnt-offering which the priest sacrificed will belong to him." With regard to a sin-offering, [<i>ibid.</i> 6:19] states: "The priest who performs the sin-offering shall partake of it." With regard to a guilt-offering, [<i>ibid.</i> 7:7] states: "It will belong to the priest who brings about atonement because of it." With regard to a peace-offering, [<i>ibid.</i>:14] states: "It will belong to the priest who pours the blood of the peace-offering." And with regard to the meal-offering, it is written: "The priest who offers it shall partake of it."<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The verse cited in the text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> does not exist. The commentaries have suggested that the intent is Leviticus 7:9 as stated in the following halachah.<br>See the gloss of the Radbaz who explains why all the different verses are necessary, because the concepts cannot be derived from each other.</i><br>In all the above instances, the verses are not referring [to the priest who actually brings the offering, but] to [any priest] who is fit to have done so. Any priest who is fit to perform the service has a right to share in the division [of the sacrifice] to partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by Halachah 17 [see also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 12:1)], priests with a disqualifying physical blemish are not excluded from receiving a portion of the division of the sacrifices even though they are unfit to offer them, because there is a special verse that teaches that they should receive a portion.</i> [Conversely,] one who is not fit [to serve] at the time it is offered, e.g., he was ritually impure, does not have a right to share in the division [of the sacrifice] to partake of it even if he will regain ritual purity in the evening.<br>With regard to the division, the entire [matter] is given to the men of the clan who offer sacrifices that day.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 4:11 with regard to what is meant by a clan.</i> They all share in the division of all of the sacraments of the Temple, each man [receiving the same portion] as his brother, whether he was the one who offered the sacrifice or he was together with him in the Temple, but did not offer the sacrifice.
Why did the verse make a distinction between the meal-offerings that are baked and the meal-offerings of flour? For with regard to [the meal-offerings] that are baked, [Leviticus 7:9] states: "Any meal-offering that is baked in an oven... it will belong to the priest who offers it" and with regard to the meal-offerings of flour [<i>ibid.</i>:10] states: "Any meal-offering that is mixed with oil or that is parched shall belong to all the sons of Aaron, every man like his brother."<br>[The following explanation can be given:]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that the explanation appears to be the Rambam's own, rather than to stem from a prior source.</i> When [the meal-offerings] that are baked are divided among the members of the clan, whenever a person receives a portion, even if it is [merely] an olive-sized portion of bread, it is fit for him to partake of it, because it may be eaten immediately. When, however, we are speaking of flour, if [such an offering] will be divided among them, there will be those who will receive only a handful of flour or less. This is not fit either to be kneaded into dough or to be baked. Hence, one might think that one meal offering should be apportioned in consideration of another meal-offering instead of dividing each meal-offering individually among all the members of the clan.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if there were 20 meal-offerings and 60 priests, one might think that each of the meal-offerings would be divided among three priests rather than have all 20 divided among the 60. Hence, it is necessary to explicitly state that every priest receives a portion of a meal-offering of flour.</i> Therefore it is necessary for the Torah to state [that it] "shall belong to all the sons of Aaron, every man like his brother," i.e., that it is divided individually.<br>On this basis, our Sages said:<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra</i> to the verse, <i>Kiddushin</i> 53a; <i>Menachot</i> 73a.</i> that a meal offering is not divided in consideration of another meal-offering, not even one offering brought in a flat frying pan in consideration of another such offering or one flour-offering in consideration of another such offering. Instead, what remains<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">After the portions offered on the altar are taken.</i> from every [offering] is divided individually.
Similarly, an offering of fowl is not apportioned in consideration of another such offering, nor a sin-offering in consideration of another such offering, nor a breast and a thigh in consideration of another such portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In all these instances, one might think that it would be preferable to divide each of the sacrifices among a smaller number of priests.</i> Instead, every [sacrificial portion] is divided among all [the members of the clan] equally.
Even though it is permitted to feed a minor [meat even] from sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity, he is not given a portion, even from sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, he may only partake of what the other priests give him from their portions.</i> Similarly, a woman and an <i>androgynus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the halachic status of the gender of an <i>androgynus</i> is not defined, he is not given a portion as a male would be.</i> are not given a portion of the sacrifices from the Temple at all, as [the above verse] states: "Every man like his brother."<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The same phrase excludes both women and minors, because the term "man" implies that the recipient must be male and of age. Since they are not fit to perform service, they are not allocated a portion of the sacrifices.</i><br>[A priest] who has a disqualifying physical blemish - whether permanent or temporary, whether he was born with it or born unblemished and later disqualified - receives a portion of the sacrifices and may partake of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he is not fit to perform service, he is considered an ordinary member of the priestly clan and given a portion of the sacrifices. There is an explicit verse requiring this [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 12:1)].</i> as [Leviticus 21:22] states: "The food of his God, of the most sacred order... he may eat." [The above applies] provided he is fit to partake [of the sacrifice at the time it is offered]. If, however, he is impure, he is not given a portion to eat in the evening.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he would regain his ritual purity by the evening.</i>
The High Priest may partake [of any of the sacrifices] without a division having been made. Rather, he may take whatever he desires.
All [priests] who are fit to partake of sacrificial foods at the time the sacrifice is offered receive a share [of the sacrifice] to partake of. All [priests] who are not fit to partake of sacrificial foods at the time the sacrifice is offered, even though they are fit to perform sacrificial service and will be fit to partake [of the sacrifice in the evening] do not receive a share [of the sacrifice] to set aside until the evening.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">For everything depends on the priest's state at the time the sacrifice is offered (<i>ibid.</i>).</i><br>What is implied?<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., which priests could be fit to perform service during the day and fit to partake of sacrifices after nightfall, but are unfit to partake of the sacrifices at the time they are offered.</i> A priest who has immersed that day,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">But will not regain purity until nightfall.</i> one who is lacking in atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A priest who had been a <i>zav</i> or who had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> must immerse himself in a stream or a <i>mikveh</i> to regain ritual purity.
