In addition, he must bring a specific offering. Until he brings that offering, he is considered as lacking atonement, as explained in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i>.<br>These two categories of priests are considered as fit to serve, because they would be fit to offer a communal sacrifice if it were to be brought in a state of ritual impurity. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:10 and Halachah 23 and notes.</i> or one who is <i>onein</i> on the day of burial,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>onein</i> refers to a person in the acute state of mourning experienced at the time of the death of any of seven close relatives. On the day of such a relative's death, one is an <i>onein</i> according to Scriptural Law. If the relative is not buried on the day of his death, the priest is considered an <i>onein</i> according to Rabbinic Law until after the day of burial. Once the relative is buried, however, the priest may perform service and partake of sacrifices in the evening. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 2:7, 9-10.</i> whether a High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Who is permitted to serve even though he is in the state of <i>onein</i> mourning. See the following halachah.</i> or an ordinary priest, should not receive a share of the sacrifices in order to partake of them in the evening.
All [priests] who are not fit to partake of sacrificial foods are not fit to perform sacrificial service with the exception of a High Priest who is an <i>onein</i> who may offer sacrifices, but may not partake [of sacrificial foods], as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 2:6-8.</i> All [priests] who are not fit to perform sacrificial service are not fit to partake of sacrificial foods<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Or Sameach</i> suggests that the text should read "are not fit to receive a portion of sacrificial foods," because women and children are fit to partake of the sacrifices, but are not fit to perform sacrificial service.</i> except one disqualified because of a physical blemish [whose license to partake of sacrificial foods] is explicitly stated in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 17.</i>
All [priests] who do not receive a portion of the sacrificial meat do not receive a portion of the hides of the sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 12:2 derives this concept from Leviticus 7:12: "The hide of the burnt-offering which he offered will belong to the priest," i.e., the priest must be fit to offer the sacrifice. If he is fit to offer it, he is fit to partake of it.<br>The Radbaz explains that it is necessary for the Rambam to mention the allocation of the hides explicitly, because one might not necessarily derive this point from the allocation of the meat.</i> Even if a priest was impure at the time when the blood [of a sacrifice] was sprinkled on the altar, but pure at the time its fats were offered on the altar's pyre,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about an instance where a priest was impure at the time a sacrifice was slaughtered and its blood was sprinkled on the altar. Nevertheless, the offering of the fats of the sacrifice was delayed and not performed until after nightfall. In the interim, the priest immersed himself and thus was pure at the time the fats were offered.</i> he does not receive a portion of the sacrificial meat, as [Leviticus 7:33] states: "One who offers the blood of the peace-offering and the fat from the sons of Aaron, to him the right thigh will be [given] as a portion." [Implied is that] the priest must be pure and fit to perform service from the time [the blood] is sprinkled until after the fats are offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For the prooftext mentions both these services.</i>
If [a priest] became impure after [the blood of the sacrifice] was sprinkled [on the altar] and immersed himself and thus was also pure when the fats were offered on the altar's pyre, there is an unresolved question whether or not he receives a portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">For although he was pure when both these services were performed, he was impure in the interim.</i> Therefore if he grabs a portion, it is not expropriated from him.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is not given a portion, because he cannot prove that he is worthy of one, but it is not taken from him, since the other priests also cannot prove that he is not worthy. In both instances, the basis for the decision is the principle: When one seeks to expropriate money from a colleague, the burden of proof is on the plaintiff.</i>
When a communal sacrifice is offered in a state of impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as stated in <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:10,12, when the majority of priests are impure, the communal offerings may be offered by the impure priests.</i> even though impure priests [could have] offered it,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed additions are made on the basis of the glosses of the Radbaz and Rav Yosef Corcus who note that if the sacrifice was offered by impure priests, its meat is not eaten (<i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:11). Hence we are forced to say that we are speaking about a situation where the sacrifice could have been offered by impure priests, but in fact it was offered by pure priests. Thus the sacrifice may be eaten. One might think that the impure priests would receive a portion since they were fit to offer the sacrifice. Hence, the Rambam explains that they do not, because they were not fit to partake of it at the time it was offered.</i> they do not receive a portion together with the priests who are pure to partake of it in the evening,<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if they would be fit to partake of it in the evening.</i> because they are not fit to partake of it [at the time it is offered].

Chapter 11

Anyone<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This prohibition applies equally to priests and Israelites (Radbaz).</i> who eats an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle with regard to all prohibitions involving eating. Lashes are given only for partaking of an olive-sized portion.</i> of the meat of a burnt-offering, whether before its blood is sprinkled [on the altar] or afterwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Compare to Halachah 4.</i> is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 146) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 447) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. As explained in <i>Hilchot Meilah</i> 1:3, this negative commandment is also the source for the prohibition against deriving benefit from consecrated articles (<i>meilah</i>).</i> as [implied by Deuteronomy 12:17]: "You may not eat in your gates the tithe of your grain...<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second tithe which must be brought to Jerusalem.</i> and [the sacrifices brought because of] the vows that you take." According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifri</i> to the above verse, <i>Makkot</i> 17a.</i> it was taught that this is a warning against partaking of the meat of a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For the latter term is understood as a reference to the burnt offering (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
Its fat, its meat, and the flour, oil, and wine of its accompanying offering - all five [of these components] can be combined to comprise this olive-sized portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since they are all considered as components of the burnt offering, they are all forbidden. The Radbaz mentions that the omission of the blood of the sacrificial animal indicates that it is not included.</i>
Similarly, one who partakes of an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that the Rambam explicitly mentions that one must partake of an olive-sized portion to be liable - instead, of allowing a reader to reach that conclusion on his own - because one might think that since the verse states that "It shall be totally consumed," as long as a person prevents it from being totally consumed, he is liable.</i> of any of the portions of the sacrifices to be offered on the altar's pyre, whether before the blood [of the sacrifice] is sprinkled [on the altar] or afterwards, whether he is a priest or an Israelite, is liable for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Besides the prohibitions mentioned here, a person who partakes of these sacrificial components also violates the prohibition against <i>meilah</i>.</i> For the portions to be offered on the altar's pyre must be totally consumed like the totality of the burnt offering. Now [Leviticus 6:16] states with regard to a meal-offering of a priest: "It shall be totally consumed; it should not be eaten." [From this, we derive that] there is a negative commandment [prohibiting] partaking of anything that should be consumed by [the fire of] the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 138) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 137) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. Although the commandment specifically refers to the meal offering brought by a priest, the <i>Sifra</i> understands it as applying to a greater scope of sacrificial foods, as the Rambam explains.</i> One is liable for lashes for eating an olive-sized portion [of such substances].<br>Similarly, anyone who eats an olive-sized portion of any of the sin-offerings that must be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachot 2-4.</i> is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 139) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 139) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. A separate commandment is necessary, for these sin-offerings are not burnt on the altar of the Temple.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i>:23] states: "Any sin-offering whose blood is brought into the Tent of Meeting... should not be eaten. It should be burnt with fire."
Anyone who partakes of an olive-sized portion of the meat of any of the sacrifices - even sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity - before their blood is sprinkled [on the altar]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Meilah</i> 2:1, after the blood is sprinkled on the altar, even one who is not permitted to partake of these sacrifices is not liable for <i>meilah</i>, misappropriating the sacred articles for one's personal use.</i> is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 147) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 448) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. Significantly, in his listing of the mitzvot at the beginning of this set of halachot, the Rambam mentions this prohibition only with regard to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity.</i> as it is written: "You may not eat in your gates the tithe of your grain, your wine, and your oil... and [the sacrifices brought because of] your pledges." Implied is that you may not eat [the sacrifices brought because of] your pledges in your gates before their blood is sprinkled [on the altar] in God's gates.<br>According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For this is not the simple meaning of the verse (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i> it was taught that this is a warning against partaking of [the meat of] a thanksgiving-offering or a peace-offering before the sprinkling of its blood. This also applies to [the meat of] other sacrifices, whether sacrifices of the highest order of sanctity or those of a lesser degree.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Makkot</i> 17a explains the process of exegesis through which the prohibitions against partaking of the other sacrifices are derived.</i> The six components of the thanksgiving offering, the fat, the meat, the flour, the oil, the wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the flour, the oil, the wine that comprise the accompanying offering that is brought together with the thanksgiving-offering.</i> and the bread, may all be combined to comprise the olive-sized portion [for which one is liable].
Anyone who partakes of an olive-sized portion of the meat of any of the [sacrifices of] the most sacred order that was taken outside of the walls of the Temple Courtyard, and meat from [sacrifices of] a lesser degree of sanctity that was taken outside Jerusalem, is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 145) and the <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 446) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. Significantly, in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> and in his listing of the mitzvot at the beginning of this set of halachot, the Rambam mentions this prohibition only with regard to sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity although he does state that one who partakes of other sacrifices outside of Jerusalem is liable for lashes. When listing the prohibitions for which lashes are given in <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 19:4, the Rambam lists these two activities separately.</i> as [implied by] the verse: "You may not eat in your gates the tithe of your grain, your wine, your oil, and the firstborn of your cattle and your sheep...." According to the Oral Tradition, it was taught that this is a warning against partaking of [the meat of] a sin-offering or a guilt-offering outside the Temple Courtyard. Similarly, one who partakes of [the meat of] the sacrifices of lesser sanctity outside Jerusalem is liable for lashes. For with regard to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, the walls of Jerusalem are comparable to the walls of the Temple Courtyard with regard to sacrifices of the most sacred order. [Included equally are] the meat of the sin-offerings and the guilt-offerings and what remains of the meal-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">After the handful was taken to be offered on the altar. All of these are considered as sacrifices of the most sacred order.</i>
Meat from [sacrifices of] the most sacred order that was taken outside of the walls of the Temple Courtyard, and meat from [sacrifices of] a lesser degree of sanctity that was taken outside Jerusalem, is disqualified and is forbidden forever. Even if it is taken back to its place, it is forbidden to partake of it. One who eats an olive-sized portion of it<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Whether it was returned to its place or not.</i> is liable for lashes, as [implied by Exodus 22:30]: "Meat in a field [from an animal that is] <i>treifah</i>, you shall not eat." Once meat has left its designated place, it is considered as <i>treifah</i>, as we explained in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 5:9. As the commentaries explain there, the term "field" is referring to a place which is not the natural place for the meat to be found. Being there causes it to be considered <i>treifah</i>, unfit to be eaten. Similarly, the fact that this meat is taken outside its natural place causes it to become forbidden.</i>
If meat from [sacrifices of] a lesser degree of sanctity was taken into the Temple Building, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since, as stated in Chapter 10, Halachah 3, after the fact, sacrifices of the most sacred order may be eaten in the Temple Building, taking sacrificial meat there is not considered as taking it outside its designated place. The Radbaz questions whether it is acceptable to eat meat from sacrifices of lesser sanctity in the Temple Building.</i>
A non-priest who ate an olive-sized portion of meat from sacrifices of the most sacred order<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that this applies only to meat from a sin-offering or a guilt-offering. If, however, a non-priest partakes of meat from a burnt-offering, he is liable only for the prohibition against partaking of the meat of a burnt-offering (see Halachah 1) and not because of the prohibition against a non-priest partaking of sacrificial meat.</i> in the Temple Courtyard after their blood was sprinkled [on the altar] is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 148) includes this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. The <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i>, however, does not include it.</i> as [Exodus 29:33] states: "And they shall partake of [the sacrifices] which bring them atonement... and a non-priest shall not partake of them." [The association teaches<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as <i>Makkot</i> 18b states, the prohibition against a non-priest partaking of this sacrificial meat applies only in an instance where atonement would be granted were a priest to partake of the meat there.</i> that when sacrificial meat] was eaten by a non-priest in a place where a priest partakes of it and at a time when it is fit to be eaten, he is liable for lashes. If, however, a non-priest ate an olive-sized portion of meat from sacrifices of the most sacred order outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable for lashes for partaking of the sacrificial meat outside [the Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 5.</i> and not because [of the prohibition against] a non-priest partaking of sacrificial foods,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that there is a practical as well as a theoretical difference resulting from this concept, for if the non-priest was given a warning for partaking of the meat because he was a non-priest and not because he was partaking of it outside the Temple Courtyard, he is not liable.</i> for they are not fit to be eaten by priests there. Similarly, if [a non-priest] partook of [such meat] in the Temple Courtyard before [the blood] was sprinkled [on the altar], he is liable for lashes only for partaking [of the meat] before the sprinkling of the blood, not because of [partaking of it] as a non-priest.
A non-priest who ate an olive-sized portion of meat from a sin-offering from fowl is liable for two sets of lashes: one because he is non-priest partaking of sacred meat<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This law also applies only when the meat of the fowl was eaten in a time and a place when the fowl would have been permitted to be eaten by the priests (Radbaz).</i> and one because he is partaking of a <i>neveilah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">An animal that died without proper ritual slaughter.</i> For every [fowl killed by] <i>melikah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Snipping off its head, as stated in Chapter 6, Halachah 21.</i> is a <i>neveilah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For this is not an acceptable process of ritual slaughter.</i> When license was granted to partake of it, it was granted to priests, but not to non-priests. These two prohibitions are considered as two prohibitions that take effect at the same time,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">At the time the fowl's head was snipped off, it becomes both forbidden to non-priests and a <i>neveilah</i>.</i> as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:8 states:<br>There is a major general principle that applies with regard to all of the Torah's prohibitions. One prohibition does not take effect when another prohibition is in effect unless:<br>a) both of the prohibitions take effect at the same time;<br>b) the latter prohibition forbids additional entities besides [the entity that was originally] prohibited;<br>c) the scope of the [latter] prohibition encompasses other entities together with [the entity that was originally] prohibited.<br></i>

Chapter 12

Meal-offerings are a type of sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 67) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 116) include bringing the meal-offerings as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> The meal-offerings that are offered independently and not as part of the accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1.</i> include both communal meal-offerings and individual meal-offerings.
All of the meal-offerings are brought from fine wheat flour with the exception of the meal offering of a <i>sotah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A woman suspected of adultery. See <i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 3:12.</i> and the <i>omer</i> with which <i>tenufah</i> is performed,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the <i>omer</i> offering which is brought on the second day of the Pesach holiday. See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:3-12.</i> which are brought from barley.
There are three types of communal meal-offerings:<br>a) the <i>omer</i> with which <i>tenufah</i> is performed.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the <i>omer</i> offering which is brought on the second day of the Pesach holiday. See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:3-12.</i> [A portion of] it is offered on the altar, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:12.</i><br>b) the two breads offered on Shavuot.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 8:1-16.</i> They are called a meal-offering, but are not offered on the altar and are <i>chametz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Leavened bread in contrast to almost all the other meal-offerings that are unleavened.</i> Concerning them, [Leviticus 2:12] states: "You shall offer them as a sacrifice of first fruits to God, but they shall not be offered on the altar."<br>c) The third meal-offering is the showbread that is brought every Sabbath. They are not offered on the altar, but instead are eaten entirely by the priests, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 4:10-12; 5:5.</i>
There are nine types of individual meal-offerings. They are all offered on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are brought to the southwestern corner of the altar as described in Halachah 6 and a handful from them (or with regard to offerings brought by priests, the entire offering) is offered on the altar's pyre, as described in Halachah 9.</i> They include:<br>a) the meal-offering of a sinner when he is obligated to bring a sin-offering, but is financially incapable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 5:11; <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 1:4;10:4.</i><br>b) the meal offering of a <i>sotah</i>; it is the meal-offering of jealously. The manner in which it was offered has already been described.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A woman suspected of adultery. See <i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 3:12.</i><br>c) the meal-offering that every priest brings at the outset when he enters the Temple service the first time. He brings it himself. It is called the meal-offering of initiation.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 5:16. See also Chapter 13, Halachah 4.</i><br>d) the meal-offering that the High Priest would offer every day. It is called the <i>chavitin</i> [offering];<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 6:13-15; Chapter 13, Halachot 2-4; <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 3:18. The above four offerings are obligatory. The five that follow are voluntary.</i><br>e) a meal-offering of fine flour.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 2:1; Chapter 13, Halachah 5.</i> It is brought as either a vow or a pledge;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 1:2 with regard to the distinction between these two types of commitments.</i><br>f) a flat-pan flour-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 2:5; Chapter 13, Halachah 6.</i> It is brought as either a vow or a pledge;<br>g) a deep-pan flour-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 2:7; Chapter 13, Halachot 6-7.</i> It is brought as either a vow or a pledge;<br>h) a flour-offering baked in an oven.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 2:4; Chapter 13, Halachah 8.</i> It is brought as either a vow or a pledge;<br>i) an offering of wafers.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This offering is also baked in an oven, as stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 8.</i> It is brought as either a vow or a pledge.
With regard to all of the meal-offerings that are brought to the altar, none may be less than an <i>isaron</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">An <i>isaron</i> is 2500 cc in contemporary measure according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 4320 cc according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> this an absolute requirement for the offering to be acceptable.<br>A person may pledge and vow as large a quantity as he desires, even 1000 <i>isaronim<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>The offerings must, however, be of complete isaronim.</i></i></i> for any of the five meal-offerings that are brought as a pledge or a vow. By contrast, the meal-offering of the <i>omer</i>, the meal-offering of a sinner, the meal-offering of jealously, the meal-offering of initiation, and the <i>chavitin</i> offering must each be one <i>isaron</i>, neither more or less.
All of the meal-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that this includes the meal offerings brought by priests even though a handful of flour is not removed from them. It does not, however, include the meal-offerings brought as part of the accompanying offerings. The rationale for the distinction is that this rite is required only for the meal-offerings that are brought as independent offerings, not for those that merely accompany other offerings. The showbread and the two loaves offered on Shavuos are not brought close to the altar, because they are not offered on the altar at all.</i> that are brought to the altar must be brought close to the altar on its western side, facing the tip of its southwest corner.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 6:7 states: "The sons of Aaron will bring it close, before God, towards the face of the altar." <i>Sotah</i> 14b interprets this as referring to the southwest corner of the altar."</i> <i>Tenufah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 7, where this rite is described.</i> is not required for them with the exception of the meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 16.</i> and the <i>omer</i> [offering].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:12.</i> Both of them require both <i>tenufah</i> and being brought close to the altar.
All of the meal-offerings that are brought to the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This term excludes the showbread and the two loaves offered on Shavuos, as mentioned above.</i> require that oil and frankincense be placed upon them,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">With the exception of the meal-offering of a sinner and a <i>sotah</i>, it is explicitly stated that oil must be brought with every meal-offering. Including frankincense is mentioned only with regard to the offering of fine flour. Nevertheless, <i>Menachot</i> 59a uses techniques of Biblical exegesis to derive that it should be included with every meal-offering.</i> a <i>log</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>log</i> is 344 cc in contemporary measure according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>, and 600 cc according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>.</i> of oil for every <i>isaron</i> [of flour]<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This represents the minimum. More oil is placed on certain offerings as mentioned in Halachah 8.</i> and a handful of frankincense for every meal-offering whether it comprised one <i>isaron</i> or 60 <i>isaronim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the oil which is added proportionately to the flour, a uniform measure of frankincense is included for every meal offering.</i> - [the latter measure is mentioned because] more than 60 <i>isaronim</i> are never brought in one container, as will be explained<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 17, Halachah 6.</i> - with the exception of the meal-offering of jealously and the meal-offering of a sinner, as [Leviticus 5:11] states:<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This verse applies to the meal-offering of a sinner. Similar statements are made with regard to the meal-offering brought by a <i>sotah</i> in Numbers 5:15. In both instances, the commentaries explain that the reason for the prohibition is so that the sinner's (or the suspected adulteress') sacrifice should not appear attractive.</i> "He shall not place oil upon it,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 104) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 366) include the prohibition against placing oil on the meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i> as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. See also <i>Hilchot Sotah</i> 3:13.</i> nor shall he place frankincense upon it."<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 105) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 367) include the prohibition against placing frankincense on the meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i> as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i>
If one placed [oil and/or frankincense on these offerings] and offered them,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's understanding is that one is not liable unless he offers these meal-offerings on the altar after placing the oil and frankincense on them (Radbaz).</i> he is liable for the oil individually and the frankincense individually.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For they are considered as independent prohibitions.</i> If one placed a container holding oil or frankincense on the offering, he does not transgress,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he offers the meal-offering on the altar in this manner (Radbaz).</i> nor does he disqualify [the offerings].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he placed oil and/or frankincense directly on the meal-offering, he does disqualify it. Should one remove the frankincense after placing it upon the offering, the offering becomes acceptable again (Radbaz).</i> Oil must be added to each of the initiation and <i>chavitin</i> meal-offerings, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 13, Halachah 2.</i>
A handful is taken<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 13, for a description of how this handful is taken.</i> from all of the meal-offerings that are brought to the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this does not include the meal-offerings that are brought as part of the accompanying offerings, for they are offered on the altar in their entirety.</i> That handful is offered on the altar in its entirety and the remainder [of the offering] is eaten by the priests with the exception of a meal-offering brought by a male priest. A handful is not taken from such an offering. Instead, the entire offering is offered on the altar's pyre, as [Leviticus 6:16] states: "Every meal-offering from a priest [is offered on the pyre in its entirety]."<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The passage from which the prooftext is taken speaks about the initiation offering and the High Priest's <i>chavitin</i> offering. Nevertheless, since it includes the word "every," the concept it states is applied to other offerings brought by priests (Radbaz).</i> From this we learn that all [of the following offerings]: an initiation and <i>chavitin</i> meal-offering or a meal-offering of a sinner or a free-will meal-offering brought by a priest are all offered on the altar's pyre and a handful is not taken from them.
A handful is taken from a meal-offering brought by a woman of the priestly family<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one unmarried, alternatively, one married to an Israelite as evident from Halachah 12 (Radbaz; <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> as it is taken from the meal-offerings brought by Israelites and the remainder of it is eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sotah</i> 23b derives this concept from the fact that the prooftext cited above uses a male term for priest.</i>
When sons [from a priestly family and one of Israelites] become intermingled and each of their identities are doubtful, a handful is taken from a meal-offering [brought by either], as is done with regard to a meal-offering brought by an Israelite, but [the remainder] is not eaten, as is done with a meal-offering brought by a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since there is a doubt involved, the offering is treated with both the stringencies applying to one brought by priests and those applying to one brought by Israelites (<i>Yevamot</i> 99b).</i><br>What is done? The handful alone is offered on the altar and the remainder is scattered over the ash heap.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>ibid.</i> 100a) interprets this as referring to the place outside the Temple Courtyard where the ashes from the altar are deposited. <i>Tosafot</i> interprets it as the place inside the Temple Courtyard where sacrifices of the most sacred order that are disqualified are burnt. This difference of opinion is possible, because the term ash-pile is used for several different places.<br>The offering may not be eaten, for perhaps the person bringing it is a priest and meal-offerings a priest brings may not be eaten. Nor may it be burnt entirely on the altar, for perhaps the person bringing it is an Israelite and an Israelite's offerings may not be burnt entirely.</i>
The meal-offerings brought by all women married to a priest - whether from the priestly family or Israelites - are not eaten, because of the portion of the husband [present within them],<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For a woman will bring her offering from flour that belongs to her husband (Rashi, <i>Sotah</i> 23a).</i> nor is it offered on the pyre in its entirety, because of the portion of the woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For she is bringing it for her atonement.</i> Instead, a handful alone is offered on the altar and the remainder is scattered over the ash heap.<br>The handful may be taken in any place within the Temple Courtyard. If it was taken in the Temple Building, it is acceptable.
A meal-offering may be consecrated by [placing the flour] in a container [even] while it is placed on the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it need not be held by a priest.</i> [Similarly,] the handful may be taken from a container [placed] on the ground, [but] the handful may not be consecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The consecration of the handful is discussed in Chapter 13, Halachah 12.</i> in a container [placed] on the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 7b derives this concept from a parallel to the receiving of the blood. Just as that is only acceptable when the priest holds the container in his hands, so too, the consecration of the meal-offering is acceptable only when the priest holds the container.</i><br>When is the remainder of a meal-offering permitted to be eaten? When the fire [of the altar] has consumed most of the handful.
All of the meal-offerings that are brought to the altar are unleavened.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The only meal-offerings that are leavened are the two loaves brought on Shavuos and ten of the loaves brought for the thanksgiving offering and these are not brought to the altar.</i> Similarly, although the remaining portions of the meal-offerings that may be eaten by the priests may be eaten with all foods and with a sweetener,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Although a sweetener may not be offered on the altar (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:1), it may be eaten with sacrificial foods.</i> they may not be eaten while leavened, as [Leviticus 6:10] states: "It shall not be baked leavened; their portion...." [Implied is that] even their portion may not be leavened. If they cause the remaining portion to become leavened, they are liable for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 124) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 135) include this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> One who performs an act that causes [the remaining portion of the meal-offering] to be leavened after it was leavened,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., one allowed it to leaven while kneading it and then another baked it.</i> is liable. One is liable for each act [that causes the remnants of the meal-offering to leaven].<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the following halachah.</i>
What is implied? If one mixed [the remnants of a meal-offering] with water in a manner that causes them to leaven, one kneaded them in a manner that causes them to leaven, <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 5:2).</i> one shaped the loaves in such a manner, or baked them in such a manner, he is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Each of these acts carries liability individually, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> as [Leviticus 2:11] states: "It shall not be prepared as leavened." [Now it is also written:] "It shall not be baked leavened." [Why are the two verses necessary?] To make one liable for every individual act performed [in its preparation]. If one prepared it as leavened from the beginning to the end, one is liable for lashes for every individual act performed.
If one left yeast on a dough and then departed and sat elsewhere and [let] it leaven on its own accord, he is liable for lashes, for placing yeast [on the dough] is a deed.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the person caused the dough to leaven.</i>
If one dipped the remainder [of a meal-offering] in caraway or sesame seeds or any type of spice or oil, it is acceptable. It is <i>matzah</i>; it is merely called spiced <i>matzah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 5:20 where the Rambam rules that the addition of such substances does not cause <i>matzah</i> to leaven.<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam, stating that it is permissible to dip baked <i>matzah</i> in the substances mentioned in this halachah, but not to mix them into the dough used to prepare <i>matzah</i>. The Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, and others support the Rambam's position.</i>
A person who causes a meal-offering that was disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i>, ch. 11, describes many different factors that can cause a meal offering to become disqualified.</i> to become leavened is exempt, as [implied by Leviticus 2:11]: "which will be offered to God; it should not be prepared as leaven." [We can infer that the prohibition applies when the offering is] acceptable to God, not when it is disqualified.<br>If one caused [a meal-offering] to become leaven while it was acceptable and then it was taken outside the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Which disqualifies it, as evident from Chapter 11, Halachah 6.</i> and he then caused it to leaven again after it was disqualified, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For the second leavening. He is, however, liable for the first leavening (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i> for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Although generally one who causes a meal-offering to leaven a second time is liable (Halachah 14), in this instance, since it was disqualified in the interim, he is exempt.</i> If one caused [a meal-offering] to become leavened at the top of the altar, he is not liable for lashes, for it is written "which will be offered," and this [offering] was already offered and it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">It should not, however, be offered on the altar's pyre (but should be taken from the altar and discarded), because no leaven should be offered on the altar, as stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:1 (Radbaz).</i>
One who causes the showbread to become leaven is liable for lashes, for [the verse cited] states: "Any meal-offering."<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">And this also includes the showbread. The commentaries note that <i>Menachot</i> 57a derives this concept from a different prooftext.</i> [Causing] the meal-offering of the accompanying offering [to leaven] does not incur liability for lashes. For if [the flour] was mixed with water, it was disqualified before it became leavened.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">For the flour of the accompanying offerings should be mixed with oil, not water (Chapter 2, Halachah 4).</i> And if it was mixed with the oil<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text states "wine" and hence, has been questioned by many.</i> of the accompanying offerings, it is considered as fruit-juice and it does not cause [dough] to leaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 5:2.</i>
We do not soak the wheat kernels<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the Talmudic era, when preparing fine flour, the wheat kernels would be soaked and then ground in a mill so that the shell of coarse bran would be removed. See <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 5:7 which states that in order that the kernels not become leaven, they should be ground immediately.</i> for the meal-offerings, lest they leaven. For they would be soaked outside [the Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">By private individuals.</i> and not everyone is ardent [enough] to watch them [so that they do not leaven]. With regard to the meal-offering of the <i>omer</i>, since it is a communal offering, [the kernels] are soaked, for [those acting on behalf of] the Jewish people as a whole are ardent<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Pesachim</i> 36a) states that this sacrifice would be prepared by the agents of the court and those individuals would certainly act with the proper care and energy.</i> and watch it.
All of the meal-offerings that are baked should be mixed with lukewarm water<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Even though lukewarm water will serve as a catalyst to cause the dough to leaven faster (<i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 5:11), since the priests inside the Temple Courtyard are performing the service, we are not concerned that they will allow it to leaven.</i> and watched so that they do not leaven. [This is permitted,] because they are mixed and baked within the Temple Courtyard and [the priests] inside [the Temple Courtyard] are ardent.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">And will not allow the flour to leaven.</i>
In the place where the sacrifices of the most sacred order are cooked, the meal-offerings are baked,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">For their status is the same.</i> as [Ezekiel 46:20] states: "This is the place where the priests will cook the guilt-offering and the sin-offering, where they will bake the meal-offering...."
The grinding and the sifting [of the flour] for the meal-offerings is performed outside [the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 12; <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 3:19.</i> while the mixing of the dough, the kneading, and the baking are performed inside.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Exceptions to this general principle were the showbread and the two loaves offered on Shavuos which were also kneaded outside the Temple Courtyard, as stated in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 5:7; 8:7).</i><br>All of the acts [necessary to prepare it] are acceptable [when performed] by a non-priest until it reaches the stage where the handful [of flour] is separated.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 11:7.</i> There were a flat frying pan and a deep frying pan in the Temple Courtyard. They were both considered as sacred utensils and caused [the substances placed in them] to be sanctified.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">For anything placed in a sacred utensil becomes sanctified, as stated in <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 1:19.</i> The oven in the Temple Courtyard was made of metal.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 96a explains that it could not be made of earthenware, because it had the status of a sacred utensil since the showbread and the two loaves offered on Shavuos were sanctified because they were baked inside of it and it is not befitting to make a sacred utensil from earthenware.</i>

Chapter 13

It is a positive commandment to prepare all of the meal-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 67) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 116) include bringing the meal-offerings as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> according to the commandments prescribed by the Torah.
How was the <i>chavitin</i> offering of the High Priest prepared? He would bring an entire <i>isaron</i> and sanctify it<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">By placing it in the <i>isaron</i> measure found in the Temple Courtyard (see Chapter 2, Halachah 8). Once it was sanctified, it could not be taken out of the Temple Courtyard. Hence all the stages of its preparation had to be completed within the Temple Courtyard (Radbaz). Indeed, one of the chambers in the Temple Courtyard was the Chamber of the Makers of the <i>Chavitin</i> (<i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 5:17).</i> and then divide it in half<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Because half was sacrificed in the morning and half in the afternoon.</i> with the half <i>isaron</i> measure in the Temple. [This was necessary,] because even though it was offered in half [portions],<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4.</i> it was not sanctified in half portions. Three <i>lugim</i> of oil are brought with it, as [Leviticus 6:13] states: "You shall prepare it with oil," i.e., [an amount of] oil equivalent to the accompanying offering for a lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachot 4, 7.</i> should be added to it.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 51a explains that since the <i>chavitin</i> is cooked on a flat frying pan, it is obvious that oil should be added to it like the other meal offerings cooked on such a frying pan (see Halachah 7). Hence by saying that the <i>chavitin</i> is prepared in oil, the verse implies that additional oil is added. The need for three <i>lugin</i> is then derived from a textual association of the terms used.</i> The flour should be mixed with the oil and then scalded<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Challah</i> 1:6) the Rambam defines the term <i>cholait</i>, the verb translated as "scalded" here as: "pouring boiling water over the flour."</i> with boiling [liquid].<br>Each half <i>isaron</i> should be kneaded into six loaves. Thus there are a total of twelve loaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 15a derives this point from a comparison to the showbread which also comprised twelve loaves.</i>
[These loaves] were prepared one by one.<br>How were they prepared? The three <i>lugim</i> of oil would be divided [into twelve] using the measure of a <i>revi'it</i> [of a <i>log</i>] that existed in the Temple,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when the quantity of three <i>lugim</i> is divided in twelve (an equal portion for each loaf), we arrive at the figure of a <i>revi'it</i> (a fourth of a <i>log</i>). There was a measure of this size in the Temple. Hence the oil would be poured into this measure for each loaf to provide it with the desired amount of oil.</i> a <i>revi'it</i> for each loaf. The loaf would be baked some and then fried on the flat frying-pan with the remainder of its oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Thus there were three phases in the cooking process of these loaves. First, the flour was scalded as mentioned in the previous halachah. Then the loaf was made and then baked. Afterwards [see also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 9:3)], the oil was boiled and the loaf fried in it. See also Chapter 9, Halachah 19, which describes all these different stages of the cooking process. The same term, <i>murbechet</i>, is use to describe both that offering and the <i>chavitin</i> offering.</i> It should not be cooked very much,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he should only bake it slightly (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). This interpretation is also borne out by Chapter 9, Halachah 19.</i> for [Leviticus 6:14] uses the term <i>tufinei</i> which implies something between cooked and lightly cooked.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Afterwards, it is fried to complete the cooking process (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
Afterwards, each loaf should be divided into two<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that the cakes should not be divided. Thus instead of offering twelve half-cakes as the Rambam maintains, he maintains that six full cakes should be offered in the morning and six in the afternoon.</i> by approximation,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although half should be offered in the morning and half in the afternoon, an exact division is not required. The division is made by hand and not with a utensil. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> supports the Rambam's position, noting that the concept of dividing the cakes by hand and not with a utensil is stated in <i>Menachot</i> 87b.</i> so that half can be offered in the morning and half in the evening.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in the late afternoon.</i> He should take the halves and fold each one of them in half and then divide [the loaves at] the folds so that each flat cake will be folded in half. He then offers the halves with half of a handful of frankincense in the morning and the remaining half with half a handful of frankincense in the evening.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Further details concerning the bringing of this offering are mentioned in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 3:18-22.</i><br>If it is an initiation offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Both an ordinary priest and a High Priest bring a meal-offering prepared in the same way to mark their initiation into office (Chapter 12, Halachah 4).</i> it is not divided in half. Instead, it is offered entirely as a single entity together with the handful of frankincense. Both of them are consumed entirely by the altar's pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For the meal-offering of a priest is never eaten (Chapter 12, Halachah 9).</i>
How is a meal-offering of fine flour prepared? One brings an <i>isaron</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">But no less than an <i>isaron</i> (<i>ibid.</i>:5).</i> - or several <i>isaronot</i> or [many,] according to his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">One may bring as many <i>isaronot</i> as he desires, even 1000 (or more).</i> - of fine flour and the oil appropriate for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>log</i> of oil for every <i>isaron</i> of flour, as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 7.</i> The flour should be measured in the <i>isaron</i> measure<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the offering contained many <i>isaronot</i>, it is measured out, <i>isaron</i> by <i>isaron</i> (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> of the Temple. Oil should be placed in<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Menachot</i> 6:3, oil is added to all of the meal-offerings offered other than those which are baked in the oven three times: it is placed in the container before the flour, it is mixed into the flour and then it is poured over the mixture of flour and oil. This sequence is not explicitly stated with regard to the offering of fine flour. In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam explains the process through which it is derived that the above concepts apply to this meal-offering as well.</i> a container<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a trough where the oil and flour are mixed. Both the Radbaz and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> state that, from the Rambam's wording, it appears that this trough was not a sacred utensil. They question that conclusion, for once the flour had been put in a sacred utensil, it does not appear appropriate to place it in an ordinary utensil again. Indeed, they explain that perhaps the second utensil was also sacred.</i> and the flour poured upon it, and then other oil should be poured over the flour and the flour mixed in it. Afterwards, it is placed in a sacred vessel and oil is poured into it. The total of the oil placed first, that mixed with the flour, and that poured upon it is a <i>log</i> for an <i>issaron</i>. Frankincense is placed upon it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A handful of frankincense for every offering regardless of its size (Chapter 12, Halachah 7).</i>
How are a meal-offering [cooked in] a flat frying-pan and one [cooked in] a deep frying pan prepared? Oil should be placed in a container and the flour poured upon it and then other oil should be poured over the flour and the flour mixed in it. Afterwards, it should be kneaded with warm water<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Care was taken so that they do not leaven (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and baked in a flat frying-pan or a deep frying-pan as he vowed. It is broken into pieces<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Leviticus 2:6.</i> and placed in a sacred vessel and the remainder of the oil is poured upon it. [Then] its frankincense is placed [upon it].
What is the difference between a flat frying-pan and a deep frying pan? A deep frying-pan has an edge<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 63b notes that Leviticus 7:9 states of such an offering being cooked "in a deep frying-pan," while the others are described as being cooked "on a flat frying-pan." Implied is that the deep frying-pan had an edge large enough to contain liquids.</i> and the dough cooked in it is soft,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">And thin like the dough of pancakes [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 5:8)].</i> because since it has an edge, [the dough] does not drip off. The flat frying-pan does not have an edge. The dough cooked in it is firm so that it will not drip off to either side.
How is a meal-offering baked in an oven prepared? If it is of loaves, one should mix the flour with oil,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Oil is not, however, placed in the container before the flour is (<i>Menachot</i> 6:3).</i> knead it with warm water, and bake it. [Afterwards,] it is broken into pieces, placed in a sacred utensil, and its frankincense is placed upon it. Oil is not poured over it, as [indicated by Leviticus 2:4]: "loaves of <i>matzah</i> mixed with oil."<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., only "mixed with oil." No other oil is used in preparing them.</i><br>If it is of wafers, one should knead the flour with warm water and smear the wafers with oil, as [<i>ibid.</i>] states "wafers of <i>matzah</i> smeared with oil." It appears to me that they should be smeared after they are baked.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz supports this supposition, explaining that if the wafers were smeared before they were baked, they would not absorb the oil.</i>
How are they smeared? One brings a <i>log</i> of oil for every <i>isaron</i> and smears them and then smears them again until all of the oil in the <i>log</i> is finished.
All of these four types of baked<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., those fried in a deep frying pan and a flat frying-pan or the two types baked in an oven. Excluded is only the flour offering which is presented without being baked or cooked.</i> meal-offerings, when they are baked, they are baked when dividing [each] <i>isaron</i> into ten loaves.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 76a derives this from a comparison to the thanksgiving offering. See Chapter 9, Halachot 17-18.</i> If, however, one added or subtracted loaves, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For even a thanksgiving offering is acceptable if its loaves are less than the required number (<i>ibid.</i>:22).</i><br>How are they<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">These four types of meal-offerings. Again, a flour offering is excluded.</i> broken into pieces? Each loaf should be folded into two and then the double fold into four<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>, explains the rationale, stating that Leviticus 2:6 mentions "breaking them (i.e., into two) into pieces" (i.e., that the two are divided again).</i> and then [the folds] should be separated. If the meal-offering was brought by males of the priestly family,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For their meal-offerings are consumed entirely by the altar's pyre. Meal-offerings brought by females of the priestly family are broken into pieces.</i> they should not be separated and broken off. All of the pieces should be the size of an olive.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For making them any smaller would make them appear insignificant.</i> If, however, one made them larger or smaller, [the offering] is acceptable.
If one did not mix [the oil into the meal],<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Note, however, Chapter 17, Halachah 6, which states that the offering must not be so large that it could not be mixed with its oil.</i> fold [the loaves], bring [the meal-offering to the corner of the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Chapter 12, Halachah 6.</i> or smear the wafers [with oil],<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 7:8 rules that if the required amount of oil is not added to a meal offering, it is not acceptable. Nevertheless, after the fact, the oil does not have to be applied to the offering in the manner detailed above.</i> [the offerings] are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 3:2, the source for this halachah, mentions the above concepts and adds that the offerings are acceptable if salt and oil were not placed upon them. In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that the intent is not that the offering is acceptable without salt and oil, but that they need not be added to the offering by a priest.</i> All of these matters were mentioned only as a mitzvah,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as the optimum manner to perform the mitzvah.</i> for it is a mitzvah [to bring the meal-offerings] in this manner.
What is the order in which the meal-offering is brought? A person should bring flour from his home in a container of silver, gold, or another type of metal, a container that is fit [to be sanctified] as a sacred vessel.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 1:18, a sacred utensil may not be made of wood, bone, stone, or glass, only of metal.</i><br>If it is a meal-offering of flour, he should place it in a sacred utensil and consecrate it<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Placing it in the sacred vessel with the intent to consecrate it brings about its consecration. See Chapter 2, Halachah 9, <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:20.</i> in the sacred utensil.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As described in Halachah 5.</i> If it is one of the meal-offerings that are baked<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Or fried, i.e., any of the other types of meal offerings.</i> it should be baked there in the Temple and broken into pieces as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 6-10.</i> The pieces should be placed into a sacred utensil and its oil and frankincense placed upon it. It should then be brought to a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., up until this point, the services could have been performed by a non-priest. From this stage onward, they must be performed by a priest. See Chapter 12, Halachah 23.</i> who brings it to the altar. He approaches the southwest corner of the altar with it. That is sufficient.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he touches the altar's corner with the utensil. He does not have to have the flour itself touch the corner (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>He then moves all of its frankincense to one side<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">So that it will not be mixed with the flour taken in the handful (<i>Sotah</i> 14b).</i> and gathers a handful from the place where the majority of its oil has collected, as [Leviticus 2:2] states: "[He shall take a full handful] from its flour and from its oil." He places the handful in a sacred vessel and consecrates it in the sacred vessel.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Although the handful of flour was consecrated together with the other flour, it should be consecrated again now that it has become a separate entity. <i>Sotah, loc. cit.</i>, draws a comparison to the blood from a sacrificial animal which is consecrated together with the entire animal at the time of its slaughter and is consecrated again when collected in a sacred utensil.</i> If a handful [from a meal-offering] was divided into two vessels, it is not consecrated. [Instead,] he should go back and consecrate it [in a single vessel].<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Although placing the handful of flour in two utensils is unacceptable, it does not disqualify it.</i><br>He should gather all of its frankincense and place it on the handful of flour in the vessel and take it up to the altar. He should salt it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As required by <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:23.</i> and place it on the pyre with a sacred utensil.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he should empty the flour from the sacred vessel over the pyre.</i> If the meal-offering is brought by [male] priests, he does not take a handful. Instead, he salts the entire offering and tosses everything on the pyre.
How is a handful [of flour]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">We have mentioned flour, because taking a full handful - but only a handful, not more and not less - of flour can be easily pictured. In truth, however, an exact handful must also be taken from those meal offerings that were already baked and that is much harder to picture.</i> taken from those meal-offerings from which it is taken? As any person would take a handful. He extends his fingers<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">On the basis of <i>Menachot</i> 11a and <i>Yoma</i> 47b, many commentaries maintain that the handful is taken only with the priest's three middle fingers. The pinky and the thumb are not included. Therefore it is considered as one of the difficult services in the Temple. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 1:2; see also <i>Yoma</i> 1:5), however, the Rambam rejects this understanding and maintains that all of the priest's fingers should be used in taking the handful.</i> over the palm of his hand and closes them.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad (in his gloss to the <i>Sifra</i>) states that he should cup his hand and then insert his fingers into the flour until his palm is covered. He then closes his fingers over his palm.</i><br>If he gathered the flour only with his fingertips<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., closing his fingers over themselves and not over the palm of his hand and thus collected only a small amount of flour (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i> or from the side [of the container],<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of from the middle (Radbaz). There are also other interpretations to these concepts.</i> he should not offer it on the altar's pyre. If he does so, nevertheless, it is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 11a and <i>Yoma</i> 47b question whether these two ways to take a handful are acceptable and does not reach a conclusion. Therefore the Rambam rules that as an initial preference, one should not take a handful in this manner, but if one does so, it is acceptable after the fact.</i> If he added to the handful, i.e., he spread out his fingers and closed them,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the handful was overflowing.</i> it is unacceptable.
A handful should not be less than two olive-sized portions.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In modern measure, approximately 28 cc according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>.</i> [Bringing every] portion of the handful is an absolute requirement in [bringing] the entire handful.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For Leviticus 2:2 states that "a full handful" must be offered. Thus if one cannot bring a portion of the handful, it is disqualified and should not be brought at all (<i>Menachot</i> 27a).</i> [Bringing both] the handful [of flour] and the frankincense are absolute requirements for [bringing] either of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the offering should not be brought unless both are included.</i> [Bringing both] the flour and the oil are absolute requirements for [bringing] either of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Both are necessary because Leviticus 2:2 mentions the "full handful of flour" and "all of its frankincense." This emphasis is repeated in Leviticus 6:8, indicating that it is an absolute requirement.</i> [Bringing] even a portion of the oil is an absolute requirement in [bringing] the entire quantity.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if one does not bring the entire amount, one should not bring the offering at all.</i> Less than a <i>log</i> [of oil] should not [be brought] for every <i>isaron</i> of flour, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 5; Chapter 12, Halachah 7.</i>

Chapter 14

A person may vow or pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 4 for the distinction between the two terms.</i> to bring a burnt-offering, a peace-offering, or any of the five types of meal-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 4.</i> that may be brought as vows or as pledges.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to sin-offerings and guilt-offerings, see Halachah 8.</i> And he may vow or pledge [to bring] a meal offering from one of the three types of meal-offerings that serve as the accompanying offerings, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1, 4.</i> One may pledge or vow wine as an independent offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 14; Chapter 17, Halachah 12.</i> frankincense as an independent offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 13.</i> oil as an independent offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 16, Halachah 14.</i> or wood for the arrangement [of wood on the altar], for that is like a sacrifice,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 6:9-10.</i> as [Nechemiah 13:31] states: "for the sacrifice of wood."
Two people may pledge or vow one sacrifice, a burnt-offering or a peace-offering, even one turtle-dove and large ordinary dove in partnership. A meal-offering, by contrast, may not be brought in partnership. These matters were conveyed by the Oral Tradition.
When a person set aside a meal-offering for [the merit of] his two sons and died,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Before bringing it.</i> they may both bring it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This is not considered as bringing an offering in partnership.</i>
What is meant by a vow and what is meant by a pledge? When one says: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering," "I promise to bring a peace-offering," "I promise to bring a meal-offering," or "I promise to bring the value of this animal<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he mentions a specific animal, since his promise focuses on the animal's value and not the animal itself, it is considered as a vow and not a pledge. Moreover, he made a promise incumbent on himself - that he bring the value of the animal as a sacrifice - and did not designate the animal's worth itself. See also Halachah 6.</i> as a burnt-offering" or "...as a peace-offering," this is considered a vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation is on him; he has made a commitment to bring the sacrifice.</i> If, however, he said: "This animal..." or "The value of this animal is [designated as] a burnt-offering,"<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., pledging that the animal would be sold and the money received be designated for the purchase of a sacrifice.</i> or "...a peace-offering," or "This <i>isaron</i> [of flour] as a meal-offering," this is a pledge.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the animal or its worth is designated as a sacrifice.</i>
What is the difference between vows and pledges? If a person took a vow and separated a sacrifice and then it was lost or stolen, he is obligated to replace it<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For the obligation is incumbent on him and it was not fulfilled.</i> until he offers the sacrifice he vowed.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Moreover, the sacrifice must be offered in an acceptable manner. If it was disqualified, he is still obligated to fulfill his vow.</i> If a person made a pledge and said: "This [animal] is a sacrifice," he is not obligated to replace it if it died or was stolen.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Because he personally is under no further responsibility. He fulfilled his obligation by designating the animal.</i>
When one says: "I promise to bring the value of this ox as a burnt-offering" or "I promise to bring the value of this house as a sacrifice," if the ox dies and the house falls, he is obligated to pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 4, this is considered a vow. The rationale is that he said: <i>elai</i>, "I promise to bring." That expression indicated his willingness to accept responsibility.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Arachin</i> 5:5), the Rambam states that he is obligated to pay only the value of the dead ox, not its value when it was alive.</i> If one says: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering on the condition that I am not obligated to replace it," he is not obligated to replace it.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since he made an explicit stipulation freeing himself of responsibility, he is not considered as liable.</i>
We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:14 and the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 13:10) which refers to the temple constructed in Alexandria by Chonio, the son of Shimon the Just.</i> that one who constructs a temple to offer sacrifices within, outside the Temple is not considered as [having built] a temple to a false divinity. If one says: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering to sacrifice in the Temple [in Jerusalem]," and he brings it in [such] a temple, does not fulfill his vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 13:10) states merely "a burnt offering," omitting the words "to sacrifice in the Temple." The commentaries question why the Rambam felt it necessary to add them.</i> If he vowed to offer it in such a temple and he offers it in the Temple [in Jerusalem], he fulfills his vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since he promised to bring a burnt-offering, it is necessary that it be brought to Jerusalem. The fact that he added that he would bring it elsewhere is not significant.</i> If he offers in such a temple, he fulfills his vow. He is like someone who vowed to bring a burnt-offering on the condition that he is not obligated to replace it.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to the Rambam, the animal set aside is considered as designated as a burnt-offering and the person is liable for offering a sacrifice outside the Temple. Nevertheless, he is considered to have fulfilled his vow for the reason stated by the Rambam.</i> He is liable for <i>karet</i> for offering [the sacrifice] outside [the Temple Courtyard].<br>Similarly, if one takes a vow saying that he is a nazirite on the condition that he shave<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As is done at the conclusion of one's nazirite vow.</i> in such a temple, he fulfills his obligation if he shaves there. He is considered as one who vowed to abstain from wine and he did so abstain. This is not considered as a nazirite vow.
A sin-offering and a guilt-offering may be brought only for a sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Thus when Leviticus 5:1 introduces the obligation to bring a sin-offering, it states: "If a person will sin...."</i> They may not be brought because of a pledge or a vow. [Hence,] if one<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one who is not obligated to bring such a sacrifice.</i> says: "I promise to bring a sin-offering" or "...a guilt-offering," his statements are of no consequence. [Similarly,] if he said: "This animal is [designated as] a sin-offering" or "...a guilt-offering," his statements are of no consequence.<br>If he was obligated to bring a sin-offering or a guilt-offering and he said: "This [animal] is for my sin-offering" or "...for my guilt-offering" or "This money is for my sin-offering" or "...my guilt-offering," his statements are binding.
When a person says: "I promise to bring the sacrifices of this person afflicted by <i>tzara'at</i>" or "...this women who gave birth," if the afflicted person or the women are poor, the one who took the vow should bring the sacrifices of a poor person.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The types of sacrifices these individuals are obligated to bring vary dependent on the person's financial standing, as explained in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:3. Accordingly, one might think that regardless of the financial status of the person who pledged to bring the sacrifice, the type of sacrifice to be brought is determined by the financial status of the person who is obligated to offer it. Nevertheless, as stated in <i>Arachin</i> 13a (and quoted by the Rambam in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 5:11), this is not the case. If a wealthy person vows to bring the sacrifices of a poor person afflicted by <i>tzara'at,</i> he must bring the sacrifices of a wealthy man. Hence, we are forced to say that here the Rambam is speaking of a poor person who took such a vow.</i> If they were wealthy, the person who took the vow must bring the sacrifice of a wealthy person even though he is poor.
[The following laws apply when] one says: "I promise to bring the sin-offering, burnt-offering, guilt-offering, and peace-offering of so-and-so." If that person agrees, he may allow him to bring those sacrifices for him and he receives atonement thereby. If he agreed at the time [the sacrificial animals] were set aside, but reneged and did not agree at the time they were offered,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Arachin</i> 21a) interprets the passage as referring to an instance where the person obligated to bring the sacrifice did not know it was being offered on his behalf.
