If he did know, however, he must consent. The Rambam differs and maintains that since he gave his consent originally and the atonement associated with these sacrifices is achieved immediately, it is not necessary that he consent at the time the sacrifice was offered (Rav Yosef Corcus; see Halachah 17).</i> with regard to a burnt-offering and a peace-offering, they should be sacrificed and he receives atonement through them even if he does not consent at this time, because he agreed at the time [the sacrificial animals] were set aside. With regard to a sin-offering and a guilt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the atonement associated with these sacrifices is more encompassing, it requires not only his initial consent, but also continuous willful activity (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> by contrast, he does not receive atonement unless he consented from the beginning until the end.
One who says: "I promise to bring vows like the vows of the wicked who take upon themselves nazirite vows, sacrifices, and oaths," he is obligated in all [of his statements].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to the wording of the Mishnah (<i>Nedarim</i> 1:1). The intent of the Mishnah is that the wicked make vows hastily and moreover, obligate themselves for vows which constitute a commitment incumbent on their person that they will not necessarily be able to fulfill. As the Rambam writes in <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 13:25 and in his commentary to the above mishnah, it is preferable not to take vows, for it is possible one will not be able to fulfill them. See also <i>ibid.</i> 1:25.</i> If he says: "...as the vows of the upright," he is not obligated in anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For the upright do not take vows for the reason stated in the previous note (<i>ibid.</i>:26).</i> If he says: "as the pledges of the upright," his vow is binding<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For the upright do make pledges, for a pledge involves the sanctification of an article at hand and if the upright wish to take such action, there is nothing preventing them from fulfilling their vow. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam explains that the upright would bring their sacrificial animals to the Temple Mount and only then, pledge to bring them. In this way, there would be no question of them keeping their word.</i> with regard to nazirite vows and sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not mention oaths, because the upright try to avoid taking oaths entirely.</i> All terms used to refer to sacrifices are considered as sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 1:16 which states:<br>There are places where people are inarticulate and mispronounce words, calling subjects by different names. [In those places,] we follow the meaning of the local term.<br>What is meant by the statement that all the terms used for the word <i>korban</i>, "sacrifice," are equivalent to the term <i>korban</i>? When one says: "[This produce] is considered for me like a <i>konam</i>," "...a <i>konach</i>," or "...a <i>konaz,</i>" they are all terms referring to a <i>korban</i>.<br></i>
Neither one who takes a vow or one who makes a pledge is liable unless his statements match his intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle applying with regard to oaths (<i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 2:10-12) and vows (<i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 2:2).<br>In the continuation of the halachah, the Rambam describes a situation in which one is liable for a sacrifice he intended to give even though he does not say anything. That, however, is not a contradiction to the statements here, because here, the person's statements contradict his intent.</i><br>What is implied? If one had the intent of saying: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering," but he said "...a peace-offering" or he had the intent of saying: "I promise to bring a peace-offering," but he said "...a burnt-offering," his words are of no substance. If, [however,] he intended to take a vow to bring a burnt-offering and said: "a sacrifice," or he intended to say devotion offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Which is consecrated to the Temple treasury.</i> and he said: "consecrated property," his statements are binding, for a burnt-offering is a sacrifice, and something designated as devotion offerings is consecrated. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.<br>With regard to vows and pledges, it is not necessary for him to make any verbal statements. He is obligated even if he made a firm resolve in his heart without saying anything. What is implied? If one made a resolve in his heart that an animal should be designated as a burnt-offering or that he should bring a burnt-offering, he is obligated. [This is derived from Exodus 35:5]: "All those generous of heart shall bring it." Generosity in the heart [alone is sufficient to] establish an obligation to bring. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations involving vows and pledges for the consecration [of sacrifices].
With regard to vows and pledges and other commitments that a person makes that obligated him, endowment obligations, pledges for worth,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these terms refer to commitments to make donations to the Temple treasury. See <i>Hilchot Arachin</i>, ch. 1, for a more complete description.</i> tithes.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e. the tithes (including the second tithe and the tithe for the poor) separated from one's crops (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aser</i> and <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>). Also, implied are the tithe offerings. See <i>Hilchot Bechorot</i>, ch. 4.</i> and the presents to the poor,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Leket, pe'ah, ollelot</i>, etc., as described in <i>Hilchot Matanot Aniyim</i>.</i> it is a positive commandment of Scriptural origin to bring everything on the festival of immediate proximity,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 83) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 438) include this commandment among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Deuteronomy 12:5-6] states: "And you shall come there and you shall bring your burnt-offerings, your sacrifices, your tithes,... your vows, your pledges..."], as if to say: When you come [to Jerusalem] to celebrate a festival bring there everything that you are obligated and satisfy every obligation upon you.<br>If the festival arrived and he did not bring [the gifts in which he is obligated], he has negated the observance of a positive commandment. If three festivals pass without him bringing the sacrifices he vowed or pledged to bring or without giving the endowment obligations, devotion offerings, and pledges for worth, he violates a negative commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 155) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 574) count this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i> 23:22] states: "Do not delay in paying it." He does not violate the negative commandment until all of the three pilgrimage festivals of the year pass. Lashes are not given for the violation of this negative commandment, because it does not involve a deed.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 18:2, lashes are only given for the violation of a prohibition that involves a deed.</i>
If one consecrated an animal [to be offered] on the altar and [after] two festivals passed [without it being offered], it became blemished and it was redeemed for another animal, the person does not violate the negative commandment until three festivals pass without the second [animal being sacrificed].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For the calculation begins anew with the consecration of the second animal.</i><br>Both a man and a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 6b explains that since a woman is not obligated to bring a burnt-offering (<i>olat re'i'ah</i>) for the pilgrimage festival, one might think that the entire concept of a pilgrimage festival - and hence, this commandment - does not apply to her. This supposition is, however, negated, for she is obligated in bringing the peace-offering of rejoicing (<i>shalmei simchah</i>) that are sacrificed on festivals.</i> are liable for the transgression [the prohibition against] delaying [the offering of a sacrifice]. An heir, however, does not violate this prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition is not to delay paying one's vow. Since the heir did not make the vow himself, he does not violate this prohibition if he does not carry out the vow at the appropriate time. He is, however, to bring the sacrifice. See <i>Rosh HaShanah</i> 6b.</i>
Despite the fact that three festivals passed, no [animal consecrated as] a sacrifice is disqualified. Instead, [such animals should be] sacrificed and they are acceptable. Each and every day that passes after the three pilgrimage festivals involves a violation of [the above] prohibition. The [Jewish] court is commanded to apply physical coercion to the person<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Ketubot</i> 86a-b explains, the court is obligated to compel every individual to observe every positive commandment incumbent upon him, even if it requires beating him to the point of death.</i> immediately until he offers his sacrifices on the first festival that presents itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording appears to imply that if the court sees the person acting indolently with regard to the sacrifice, it is obligated to subject him to compulsion even if the first festival has not passed. This point is not, however, accepted by all authorities (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i>
Even though [Leviticus 1:3] states that [a burnt-offering must be sacrificed] "willfully," he may be compelled until he says: "I desire."<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Gerushin</i> 2:20, the Rambam explains why acting under compulsion in such circumstances can be considered willful activity. [For there also, a bill of divorce must be written with the husband's consent and yet he can be compelled to consent.]<br>The concept of being compelled against one's will applies only when speaking about a person who is being compelled and forced to do something that the Torah does not obligate him to do - e.g., a person who was beaten until he consented to a sale, or to give a present. If, however, a person's evil inclination presses him to negate [the observance of] a mitzvah or to commit a transgression, and he was beaten until he performed the action he was obligated to perform, or he dissociated himself from the forbidden action, he is not considered to have been forced against his will. On the contrary, it is he himself who is forcing [his own conduct to become debased].<br>With regard to this person who [outwardly] refuses to divorce [his wife] - he wants to be part of the Jewish people, and he wants to perform all the mitzvot and eschew all the transgressions; it is only his evil inclination that presses him [to act otherwise]. Therefore, when he is beaten, until his [evil] inclination has been weakened, and he consents [to the divorce], he is considered to have performed the divorce willfully.<br>The Rambam's statements have implications far beyond their immediate halachic context. The Rambam is saying that the fundamental desire of every Jew is to affirm his Jewishness and observe the Torah and its mitzvot. Even when a person's conscious mind does not necessarily consent to this inner motivation, it is at work, molding his character without his knowledge. And at times, either because of undesirable circumstances - being compelled against his will as above - or because of desirable ones - an expression of Divine favor - this inner drive will surface.</i> Whether he vowed [to bring a sacrifice] and did not set it aside or set it aside, but did not offer it, he may be compelled until he sacrifices it.
[The court] seizes collateral from all of those obligated to bring burnt-offerings or peace-offerings [to compel them to offer them]. Even though [the sacrifice] will not bring him atonement unless he desires to offer it, as [implied by] the term "willfully," he is compelled until he says: "I desire."<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> 3:14:<br>They take... [from the persons obligated] against their will. They are not required to return the collateral by day or by night. They sell all the landed property and movable property in [those person's] possession including their clothing, household articles, servants, and livestock, taking their payment from everything.<br></i> Collateral is not seized from those obligated to bring sin-offerings and guilt-offerings, by contrast. [The rationale is that] since [the failure to bring these sacrifices] prevents [the people] from receiving atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Burnt-offerings also feature in bringing atonement for the failure to fulfill positive commandments. Nevertheless, in that instance the atonement is achieved through the person's repentance and the offering is considered merely as a present. In contrast, a sin-offering is required to bring about the atonement itself.</i> we are not concerned that they will be sinful and delay [bringing] their sacrifices. [The only] exception is the sin-offering brought by a nazirite. Since [the failure to bring] it does not prevent him from drinking wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nazirut</i> 8:10.</i> he is compelled [to bring the offering], lest he delay it.

Chapter 15

When a person says: "The offspring of this [animal]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about an animal that is not consecrated. If an animal that is consecrated becomes pregnant, its status is automatically transferred to its offspring [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 5:1)].</i> is [consecrated] as a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the offspring should be sacrificed as a burnt-offering.</i> and it is [consecrated] as a peace-offering,"<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In the discussion to follow, it is important to note that burnt-offerings are only male. Peace-offerings can be both male and female. Sin-offerings may also be female (Chapter 1, Halachot 8-11). Nevertheless, as stated in <i>Hilchot Temurah</i> 4:2, the offspring of a sin-offering is not sacrificed, but instead, set aside to die. Hence the Rambam speaks only of the offspring of peace-offerings.</i> his words are binding. [Should he say:] "It is [consecrated] as a peace-offering and its offspring as a burnt-offering," if that was his intent, his words are binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between the first clause and the second clause is that the first clause speaks about the offspring first. Consecrating the offspring certainly does not determine the mother's status. In the second clause, by contrast, the person consecrated the mother first. Now consecrating the mother determines the status of the offspring, for the offspring is considered as "the thigh of its mother." Hence ordinarily if an animal is consecrated as a peace-offering, its offspring also has that status and should be offered as such a sacrifice (<i>Hilchot Temurah</i> 4:1). Nevertheless, in this instance, if the person originally had the intent of consecrating the offspring as a burnt-offering, the offspring is given that status, because it was never meant to be a peace-offering. The person could only make one statement at a time and the fact that he chose to speak about the mother first does not affect the offspring's status.</i><br>If, after he made a resolve in his heart and expressed verbally that [the offspring] should be a peace-offering, he retracted and said: "Its offspring is a burnt-offering," even though he retracted immediately,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The phrase translated as "immediately," <i>toch kedai dibbur</i>, has a very specific halachic meaning: "Within the time it takes to say <i>Shalom Elecha, Rebbi</i>" ("Greetings my master"; <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 2:17).</i> the offspring are [designated] as peace-offerings. For one cannot retract [pledges of] consecration even when one does so immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 5:3), the Rambam explains that generally if one seeks to retract his statements immediately, the retraction is effective. There are, however, several instances marriage and divorce, the acceptance of a false divinity, blasphemy, the consecration of sacrifices, and the transfer of holiness from one animal to another where retraction is not possible.<br>Nevertheless, the Rambam's perspective is not accepted by all authorities. <i>Siftei Cohen</i> (<i>Choshen Mishpat</i> 255:5) takes issue with him and argues that one may retract the consecration of an animal. Other sages, however, support the Rambam's position.</i>
When one says: "The right forefoot of this animal is a burnt-offering," or "The leg of this animal is a burnt offering," it should be sold to those obligated to bring burnt-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">So that it will be sacrificed for that purpose and hence, the portion of the animal consecrated as a burnt-offering will have been offered as such.</i> The proceeds of the sale are considered as ordinary funds with the exception of the worth of that limb.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For that limb was consecrated.</i> [The above applies] provided the person obligated to bring a burnt-offering who purchased [the animal] vowed [to bring] a burnt-offering for a specific price.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difficulty because the person bringing the burnt-offering will be bringing a sacrifice that does not belong to him entirely. The Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Temurah</i> 1:5) resolves this difficulty, explaining that we are speaking of an instance where the person vowed to spend a specific amount to bring a burnt-offering. Since the animal is worth that amount, he has fulfilled his vow.</i><br>If one says: "The heart [of this animal]..." or "The head of this [animal] is a burnt-offering," the entire animal is a burnt-offering, for he consecrated an organ upon which [the animal's] life depends. If one consecrated a limb from a fowl, there is an unresolved doubt whether or not the entire fowl becomes consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This concept is debated without resolution by our Sages in <i>Temurah</i> 11b. The argument centers on the fact that the concept that one can consecrate a limb from an animal as a sacrifice was derived from the exegesis of a verse (Leviticus 27:9 . Now that verse is referring to an animal sacrifice and hence, our Sages question whether the concept applies to all sacrifices or only those involving animals.</i>
When one says: "Half of this animal is a burnt-offering and half is a peace-offering," it is consecrated, but it should not be offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Because there is no way that one animal can be offered for two purposes.</i> Instead, it should be allowed to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying physical blemish and then be sold. Half of the proceeds should be used to bring a burnt offering and half, a peace-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For in this way, his vow will be kept to the fullest extent of his capacity.</i><br>If he was obligated to bring a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Here he must be obligated, because as stated above, a sin-offering may not be brought on one's own initiative.</i> and he said: "Half of this animal is a sin-offering and half is a burnt-offering," or "...a peace-offering," or he said: "Half of this animal is a burnt-offering..." or "...a peace-offering and half is a sin-offering," [the animal] should be left to die, as will be explained with regard to the sin-offerings that are left to die.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 4:1 regarding what is done with the animal. There the Rambam speaks about an animal separated as a sin-offering that was lost and discovered after its owner had brought another sin-offering in its stead. Since this animal was consecrated for a specific purpose and cannot be sacrificed for that purpose, it cannot be used for any other purpose and is hence caused to die. Similarly, in the instance at hand, since the animal cannot be sacrificed for the purpose for which it was consecrated, it is caused to die.</i>
When one of the partners who owned half an animal consecrated his portion and then purchased the other half and consecrated it, it is consecrated and offered on the altar. Even though originally, when he consecrated half of it, it was unacceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For half an animal cannot be sacrificed.</i> the fact that it was originally unacceptable does not cause it to be unacceptable forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This is a concept that applies in many different contexts of the laws concerning the consecration of animals. See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:24.</i> Even though originally only its worth was consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since only its worth was consecrated (for originally, it could not be sacrificed), there is more reason to think that it would not become acceptable afterwards.</i> since it is a live animal, [we follow the principle:]<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 59a. This is also a concept that applies in many different contexts of the laws concerning the consecration of animals. See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:23; <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 3:8, <i>et al</i>.</i> "Living animals are never permanently disqualified." Since it is [ultimately] fit to be sacrificed, it should be sacrificed. [Moreover,] if one sought to transfer its holiness to another animal,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Violating the prohibition of <i>temurah</i>.</i> the transfer is binding.
[Whether] one says: "The worth of this animal is a burnt-offering,"<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">And thus from his statements, there is room to say that it could be sold and the proceeds used to purchase an offering rather than it having to be sacrificed itself.</i> or "This animal is a burnt-offering," if it is fit to be sacrificed as a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It is male and unblemished.</i> its body itself is consecrated and it should be sacrificed as a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For any animal that is consecrated and fit to be offered as a sacrifice should be offered (Radbaz).</i> If it is not fit [to be sacrificed], it should be sold<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> elaborates, proving that here also, the Rambam's intent is that it should be left to pasture until it becomes blemished and then sold.</i> and a burnt-offering brought with the proceeds of the sale.
If a person said with regard to an impure animal<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an animal from a non-kosher species.</i> or another similar animal that is forbidden [to be offered] as a sacrifice that can never be consecrated: "Behold this is consecrated as a burnt-offering,"<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Or any other type of sacrifice.</i> his words are of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The animal is not consecrated at all. Not only is it considered as ordinary property, the person who took the vow is under no obligation and need not sell the animal and purchase a sacrifice with the proceeds.<br>We have punctuated the Rambam's words in this manner so that there is a direct correlation between the law stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:10 and the one stated here. Others maintain that the source refers to the first clause. In <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> (see below), the Rambam speaks of consecrating an animal "for the altar." If he states: "This animal is a sacrifice," no holiness is attached to it at all (see the gloss of the Ra'avad there). Similarly, in this instance, since he wished to designate the animals as a sacrifice, his words are of no consequence at all.</i> As we explained in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries explain that this reference is to <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:10 where the Rambam states:<br>One who consecrates an animal which is a <i>tumtum, androgynus, tereifah</i>... to the altar is like one who consecrated stones or wood.... The holiness does not take effect with regard to its physical substance. It is considered as ordinary property in all contexts. It should be sold and the proceeds of the sale used to purchase any sacrifice one desires. It is not considered like a blemished animal, for a sacrifice may be brought from the species of a blemished animal.<br>Similarly, in the case at hand, since a sacrifice cannot be brought from such animals, they should be sold and the proceeds used to purchase the appropriate sacrifice.</i> if he says: "These are [consecrated] for the sake of a burnt-offering," they should be sold and a burnt-offering brought with the proceeds of the sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that he made his promise in a manner that it would be of consequence (Radbaz).</i>
If a person said:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is referring to a difference of opinion among our Sages, <i>Nedarim</i> 29a, whether or not the consecration of the value (but not the physical substance) of an object for a limited time expires if nothing is done within that time. As will be stated, his ruling is that it does expire.</i> "The worth of this cow<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is speaking of a situation where the cow's worth was consecrated, because all authorities agree that if the physical substance of the cow was consecrated, it would have to be redeemed for the first consecration to be nullified (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> is [consecrated] for the sake of a burnt-offering for an entire 30 day period. After 30 days, [it is consecrated for the sake of a peace-offering," or he said: "The worth of this cow is [consecrated] for the sake of a peace-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This point is added, because it possesses an added dimension of severity beyond the first instance. For a cow may not be offered as a burnt-offering, but it may be offered as a peace-offering. Thus it is fit to be sacrificed for the sake of the offering.</i> for an entire 30 day period. After 30 days, [it is consecrated] for the sake of a burnt-offering," his words are binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling and maintains that the animal must be redeemed for the initial consecration to be nullified. The issue is debated by the subsequent commentaries.</i> [Thus] if he [comes to] offer [a sacrifice from] the proceeds of its sale within 30 days, he should bring the type of sacrifice that he vowed. If he offers it after 30 days, he should bring the type of sacrifice that he vowed.
If a person possessed a pregnant animal<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In his gloss to <i>Hilchot Nedarim</i> 12:10, the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> states that this applies only when the animal is pregnant and thus the fetus exists. If, however, the animal is not pregnant, the person's words are not binding.</i> and said: "Should it give birth to a male, it is a burnt-offering. Should it give birth to a female, it is a peace-offering," if the animal gives birth to a male, it should be sacrificed as a burnt-offering. If it gives birth to a female, it should be sacrificed as a peace-offering. If it gives birth to a male and a female, the male should be sacrificed as a burnt-offering and the female, as a peace-offering.<br>If it gives birth to two males, one should be sacrificed as a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that the animal of higher quality should be offered as the sacrifice.</i> and the other sold for the sake of a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For the person's initial vow encompassed both of them.</i> but the proceeds of the sale are ordinary money.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is only obligated to bring one sacrifice, he is allowed to sell the second animal and use the proceeds for whatever purpose he desires.</i> If it gives birth to two females, one should be sacrificed as a peace-offering and the other sold for the sake of a peace-offering, but the proceeds of the sale are ordinary money. If it gave birth to a <i>tumtum</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">An animal whose gender cannot be determined, because it is masked by a piece of flesh.</i> or an <i>androgynus</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An animal that has both male and female organs.</i> they are not consecrated and they are like ordinary animals,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are not consecrated at all and they may be used for ordinary purposes. Since the person specified that he was consecrating the offspring as a sacrifice and these animals are unfit for sacrifice, his words are of no consequence whatsoever.</i> as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:10 (cited above). See the second interpretation given in the notes to Halachah 6.</i> When a person consecrates a fetus, whose mother has a disqualifying blemish or the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., animals that are of a type able to be sacrificed, but disqualified for a particular reason. See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach, op. cit.</i></i> while it is in its mother's womb, it is consecrated.

Chapter 16

When a person vows to bring a large animal, but instead brings a small one, he does not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For he has not fulfilled his vow.</i> [If he vows to bring] a small one and brings a large one, he fulfills his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For it is as if the promise to bring the smaller animal included the possibility of bringing the larger one.</i><br>What is implied? He said: "I promise [to bring] a lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">An animal in the first year of its life (Chapter 1, Halachah 14).</i> as a burnt-offering" or "...as a peace-offering," and he brings a ram,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">One that is more than one year and one month old (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> or he vowed a calf<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">An animal in the first year of its life (Chapter 1, Halachah 14).</i> and brought an ox,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">An animal in the second year of its life (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> or a kid<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">An animal in the first year of its life (Chapter 1, Halachah 14).</i> and brought a goat,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">An animal in the second year of its life (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> he fulfills his obligation.
If he vows to bring a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The laws to follow also apply with regard to a peace-offering. They do not apply with regard to a guilt-offering or a sin-offering, for those sacrifices may not be brought voluntarily.</i> either from lambs or from rams and brings a <i>pilgas</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">A sheep that is between the age of one year and one year and one month. Thus it is no longer a lamb, but it is not yet considered as a ram (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> there is an unresolved doubt whether or not he fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">There is an unresolved doubt regarding the status of a <i>pilgas</i>. Hence, the person cannot be considered as having fulfilled his obligation. Nevertheless, since its status is unresolved, we are not definitely certain that he did not fulfill his obligation.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions why is he not considered to have fulfilled his obligation if he vowed to bring a lamb. As stated in the previous halachah, if one vows to bring a small animal and instead, brings a large animal, he is considered to have fulfilled his obligation. Thus in the case at hand, if a <i>pilgas</i> is considered a lamb, he will have fulfilled his obligation and if it is considered as a ram, he would have fulfilled his obligation, based on the principle stated in Halachah 1. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> answers that the animal's status is considered one of doubt, an intermediate state between the two. Hence it is not considered as acceptable as either type.</i> Similarly, if one vowed to bring a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies only with regard to burnt-offerings, for peace-offerings may not be brought from fowl.</i> of fowl, from either turtle-doves, or ordinary doves<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Turtle doves may only be brought as sacrifices when they are small and under-developed. Ordinary doves, by contrast, may be brought as sacrifices only when they have developed and reached a mature state (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:2).</i> and brought a fowl that began to sprout yellow feathers<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">An intermediate stage in which the fowl is unacceptable, because it is too mature for a turtle-dove and not mature enough for an ordinary dove (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> of both species, there is an unresolved doubt [whether or not he fulfilled his obligation].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is an unresolved doubt concerning a fowl that reached this state of development, a person who brings one such dove would certainly not fulfill his obligation. In this instance, however, since he brought one for each species, one might think that it is considered as if he fulfilled his vow. For if this is considered as a stage of development, he will have fulfilled the vow for he brought an acceptable ordinary dove. And if it is not considered acceptable, he would have fulfilled the vow for he brought an acceptable turtle dove. Nevertheless, since a priest is not permitted to offer such a fowl as an initial preference, he is not considered to have fulfilled his vow.</i><br>If one vowed to bring a black[-colored animal] and brought one that was white, [vowed to bring] a white one and brought a black one, [vowed] a male and brought a female or vowed a female and brought a male, he did not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For he did not bring the animal that he vowed to bring as a sacrifice.</i>
When one takes a vow without specifying [the type of animal he is bringing], he should bring from the developed animals in the species he vowed to bring. If in his place [of residence], people commonly identify one of [the type of sacrifices] with a specific species [of animals], he should bring [the type of animal brought by] the people of that locale.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This follows a general principle stated by the Rambam, that with regard to the interpretation of the wording used in vows, everything is determined by local custom.</i><br>What is implied? If a person vowed to bring a burnt-offering from cattle," he should bring an ox.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., and not a calf.</i> Should he say: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering," if the practice of the people of that locale is to use the term "burnt-offering" without any description to refer even to a fowl offered as a burnt-offering, he may bring even one fowl, a turtle dove or an ordinary dove. If their practice is to use that term only when referring to a burnt-offering of cattle, he should bring an ox. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
A person who vowed to bring an ox, a ram, a lamb, a calf, or the like should not bring the frailest specimen of that species, because their value is minimal.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As Malachi 1:14 asks rhetorically: "You bring the stolen and the lame and the sick and offer it as a sacrifice. Shall I accept it from you." See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 7:1.</i> Nor is he obligated to bring the nicest, stockiest specimen of which there is no better.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>ibid.</i>:11 states: "One who desires to gain merit for himself, subjugate his evil inclination, and amplify his generosity, should bring his sacrifice from the most desirable and superior type of the item he is bringing," this is a desirable ethical standard, but not a halachic imperative.</i> Instead, he should bring an average animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 13:8 mentions prices to be paid for animals offered as sacrifices. In the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, he explains that the prices mentioned were the average prices for animals at that time.</i> If he brought a frail animal, he fulfilled his vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the animal is unblemished.</i>
When a person says: "I promise to bring an ox worth a <i>maneh</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">100 silver pieces.</i> he should bring an ox worth a <i>maneh</i> in that place aside from its accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The wine, meal, and oil brought together with the offering, as stated in Chapter 2.</i> If he brought two [oxen] for a <i>maneh</i>, he did not fulfill his obligation.
[The following rules apply when] a person says: "This ox is a burnt-offering" and it becomes blemished. If he desires, he may bring two for the price he receives for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, since the ox was consecrated as a burnt-offering and was no longer fit to be offered as such, the proceeds of its sale must be used for that purpose. It does not matter, however, which animal(s) is purchased for that purpose.<br>In the previous halachah, it was not acceptable for him to bring two oxen that have a combined worth of 100, for he made an explicit vow to bring one ox worth 100 and until he does so, he does not fulfill his obligation. In this halachah, once the consecrated animal is disqualified, there are no qualifications regarding which animals may be brought as burnt-offerings. See <i>Menachot</i> 108a.</i> If he brings even one ram<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the sacrifice he brings need not even be of the same species as the animal he originally designated for sacrifice.</i> for the price he receives, he fulfills his vow.<br>If he says: "These two oxen are burnt-offerings," and they become blemished, should he desire, he may bring one with the money he receives for their sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The new point this clause is teaching is that even if two animals were originally consecrated, one may be brought with the proceeds of their sale.</i> "This ram is a burnt-offering" and it becomes blemished, if he desires he may bring a lamb with the money he receives for its sale. Similarly, if he vows a lamb and it becomes disqualified, he may bring a ram with the money he receives for its sale.
If he says: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering" and he set aside an ox and it was stolen, he may fulfill his obligation with a sheep.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For when taking the vow, he did not specify what animal he would bring. This applies provided people of this locale also refer to a sheep as a burnt-offering (see Halachah 3). Moreover, if he had pledged to bring that particular ox as a burnt-offering, he is not obligated to bring any sacrifice at all (Radbaz).</i> If he says: "I promise to bring this ox or its value as a burnt-offering," [his commitment] is established as binding.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the animal is disqualified, he is obligated to bring another one like it as a sacrifice.</i> If [the ox] is disqualified, he may bring only an ox with the money he receives for its sale.
If he says: "One of my sheep is consecrated and one of my oxen is consecrated," but he had two, the larger one is consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that anyone who consecrates his property does so generously (<i>Menachot</i> 108b).</i> If he has three, the larger one is consecrated, but concern should also be shown for the middle one.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For it is also possible that this was his intent, since consecrating the middle one is still acting generously, because there is a smaller one.</i><br>What should be done? He should wait until the middle one becomes blemished so that the holiness will fall on the larger one alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interprets the Rambam's words as implying that once the second one becomes blemished, the holiness automatically falls on the third. This runs contrary to Rashi's approach (<i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>) which requires the person to make a stipulation conditionally transferring the holiness that might have fallen on the middle ox.</i> If he says: "An ox from my oxen is consecrated," the larger one is consecrated and concern need not be shown for the middle one.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For by saying "An ox," we assume he meant the choicest one (<i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>). The commentaries note that the Rambam's ruling in <i>Hilchot Meilah</i> 7:7 appears to run contrary to this explanation.</i>
If he designated one of [three animals as a sacrifice] and forgot [which one he designated] or his father told him "One of these animals was consecrated,"<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">And then his father died without informing him which one was consecrated (Rashi, <i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>).</i> he should designate the largest one as consecrated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that he intended to offer the most choice one as a sacrifice. The Radbaz explains that this case is different from the one in the previous halachah where concern is also given to the middle one, because since he already designated the animal, we assume that he chose the best one.</i> and, afterwards, he fulfills his obligation [with its sacrifice]. Similarly, if one vowed to bring a burnt-offering from cattle and designated [an animal as a sacrifice to fulfill] his vow and [later] forgot whether he had designated an ox or a calf, he should bring an ox.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">For even if he originally designated a small animal as a sacrifice, we follow the principle stated in Halachah 1 that a person who vows to bring a small animal, but instead brings a large one fulfills his obligation [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 13:6); see also Ra'avad].</i> Similarly, if he designated a sheep and forgot what he designated,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Whether a ram or a lamb.</i> he should bring a ram. If he designated a goat and forgot what he designated,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Whether a grown goat or a kid.</i> he should bring a grown goat. If he forgot the species from which he designated the burnt-offering, he should bring an ox, a ram, and a grown goat.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he must allow for the possibility that he designated any of the three types of animals that could be offered as burnt-offerings and he must offer a large animal from each species for the reason mentioned above.</i> If he was in doubt that perhaps he designated the burnt-offering from fowl, he should also add a turtle-dove and an ordinary dove.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">He must bring both, because they are considered as two separate species and not as developed and underdeveloped specimens of the same species.</i>
When a person vowed to bring a thanksgiving-offering or a peace offering, specifying that it would be brought from cattle, but forgot what he designated to bring, he should bring an ox and a cow.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As stated above with regard to burnt-offerings, he is obligated to bring a developed specimen. Unlike a burnt-offering, these sacrifices may be brought from females as well as males. Hence, although bringing a large animal fulfills his obligation even if he originally vowed to bring a small one, he is still required to bring both a male and a female, for he is unsure of the gender of the animal he originally designated.</i> Similarly, if he is unsure with regard to sheep, he should bring a ram and a ewe. If he is unsure with regard to goats, he must bring a he-goat and a she-goat.<br>If he forgot the species from which he designated the sacrifice, he should bring an ox, a cow, a ram, a ewe, a he-goat, and a she-goat.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Peace-offerings may not be brought from fowl, so there is no need to bring doves.</i> If he said: "I promise to bring a burnt-offering of fowl," he should bring a turtle-dove or an ordinary dove.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Bringing either will enable him to fulfill his vow.</i> If he designated one particular species in his vow and forgot which one, he should bring both a turtle-dove and an ordinary dove.
When one says: "I promise to bring [an offering] for the altar," he should bring a handful of frankincense,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 13, when one brings frankincense, he must bring at least a handful.</i> for there is nothing that is offered entirely on the altar in its immediate state except for frankincense.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since he said: "For the altar," we assume he meant something that is only for the altar. As mentioned below, there are other entities which are also offered on the altar, but unlike frankincense, they are not offered on the altar in their entirety. (The hide of an animal offered as a burnt-offering and the feathers of a fowl offered as a burnt-offering are withheld. With regard to the wine, it is not consumed by the altar's pyre, but poured upon it. And with regard to an offering of flour, since there are types of meal-offerings which are given to the priests to eat, we assume that his intent was not to bring such an offering.)</i><br>If he specified his vow, but forgot what he specified to bring, he should bring every type of entity that is offered on the altar in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since the primary elements of the burnt-offering are burnt on the altar and the wine is poured over the altar, it is possible that this was his intent.</i> Therefore he should bring a burnt-offering of an animal, a burnt-offering of fowl, a meal-offering of flour, frankincense, and wine alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., aside from the wine brought as part of the accompanying offering of flour (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a person says: "I promise to bring [an offering] worth a <i>sela</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A large silver coin used in the Talmudic era.</i> to the altar," he should bring a sheep,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As a burnt-offering. The Radbaz states that this word should be included in the statement of the person's promise.</i> for there is nothing offered on the altar worth [exactly] a <i>sela</i> except a sheep. If he specified [a particular entity], but forgot what he specified, he should bring a <i>sela's</i> worth of everything that is offered on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the burnt-offering, this could include wine, frankincense, and flour.</i>
When a person says: "I promise to bring frankincense," he should not bring less than a handful.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 106b derives this concept from a comparison to the flour offered from a meal offering, just as there a handful is offered, so too, a handful of frankincense should be offered.</i> If one says: "I promise to bring wood," he should not bring less than two<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew term is written in the plural. Hence at least two logs are required.</i> logs as thick as a leveling rod that are a cubit long.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 7:3.</i> "I promise to bring a piece of wood," he should bring one log a cubit long. If he desires to bring the worth of the logs, he may.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">He would place the money in a container designated for that purpose and the priests would take the money and purchase the wood (<i>Tosefta, Shekalim</i> 3:3).</i>
What should a person who vowed or pledged to bring oil do? He should take a handful of it,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> questions how one takes a handful of oil.</i> put salt on it,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As required of all the sacrifices (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:11).</i> and toss it on the fire. The remainder is eaten by the priests like the remainder of the meal offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 9.</i><br>What is done with wine that is brought independently? It is salted<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach, loc. cit.</i>, states that salt is not required for wine that is offered on the altar, that refers to wine offered as a libation accompanying other sacrifices. When, however, wine is offered alone, salt is required (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and then poured entirely<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">None is given to the priests to partake of.</i> down the <i>shittin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:11 which states: "There were two cavities in the southwest corner [of the Altar], resembling two thin nostrils.... The blood [which was poured onto the Altar] would run off through them and... [ultimately,] go out to the Kidron River."</i> like the other libations.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">It is not poured over the fire, because it would - at least partially - quench the fire and that is forbidden (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Frankincense which is brought independently should be salted and then offered on the pyre in its entirety.
When a person vowed [to bring a sacrifice], he should not bring it from money [for which] the second tithe [was redeemed].<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ma'asaer Sheni</i>, ch. 4, when a person lives far from Jerusalem and cannot easily take produce from the second tithe to the holy city, he should redeem it for money. That money must be used to purchase food which must be eaten in Jerusalem according to the strictures that apply to the second tithe.</i> [The rationale is that] he became obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Because of his vow.</i> to bring this sacrifice and anyone who is obligated to bring a sacrifice may bring it only from ordinary property.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">And the second tithe is considered as holy, "consecrated unto God."<br>This is a general principle. The rationale is that since the person is obligated to bring the offering, it must be brought from resources that belong to him entirely (see the conclusion of Halachah 17).</i>
If a person states: "I promise to bring a thanksgiving-offering from ordinary funds and its bread from the [second] tithe," he should bring its bread only from ordinary funds. [The rationale is that] he vowed to bring a thanksgiving-offering and that offering comes only when accompanied by bread which is from ordinary property.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the bread is considered as an integral part of that offering and not an additional element. Thus when he vows to bring a thanks-giving offering, that vow also requires him to bring bread. Hence the bread must come from ordinary property (Radbaz).</i>
If he explicitly said: "I promise to bring a thanksgiving offering from money from the second tithes and its bread from ordinary property," he may bring [the sacrifice] as he vowed.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">As stated below, if he specifies, he is allowed to bring a thanksgiving-offering using the money from the second tithes, for he will partake of the meat of that sacrifice in Jerusalem. What is unique here is the fact that the sacrifice will be from the second tithes and the bread from ordinary funds. Even so, the sacrifice is acceptable.</i> If he brought it all from ordinary property, he fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 7:5; based on <i>Menachot</i> 81b), the Rambam writes that it is indeed more desirable for him to bring the sacrifice from ordinary funds.</i> Similarly, if he explicitly said: "I promise to bring a thanksgiving-offering and its bread from the second tithe," he may bring it [as vowed].<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, in his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam states that it is desirable for him to bring everything from ordinary property.</i> He should not bring the bread from wheat that is the second tithe, but from [flour purchased with] money [for which] the second tithe was redeemed like the animal which was [purchased with] such money.<br>Even though he explicitly stated that he would bring the bread from [the money of] the second] tithe, he should not bring its libations from the money of the second tithe, for at all times, libations must be brought solely from ordinary funds, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3, Halachah 13.</i> [The rationale is] that concerning [the libations],<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This refers not only to the wine libations, but to all vows for sacrifices.</i> [Numbers 15:4] states: "The one who offers them shall offer his sacrifice." Implied is that the sacrifice must be his without having any dimension that belongs to the Most High.

Chapter 17

When a person vows to bring a meal-offering baked in an oven, he should not bring one baked in a <i>kopach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A range with an opening for one pot [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 5:9)].</i> one baked on rafters,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one of the common ways to bake in the Talmudic period was to heat rafters and stones until they were glowing hot and place dough upon them. Afterwards, the stones and rafters were covered and thus the dough would bake (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>or one baked in Arab pits.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A pit covered with mud into which wood was placed and kindled. The dough was placed within and it was covered so that it would bake like an oven (<i>ibid.</i>). These are not acceptable, because the person took a vow that he would bring a meal offering cooked in an oven and these devices do not fit that description.</i>
When one says: "I promise to bring a meal offering [baked] on a flat frying-pan," and he brings one [baked] in a deep frying-pan<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 6, with regard to the difference between the two.</i> or he promised to bring one [baked] in a deep frying-pan and brought one [baked] on a flat frying-pan, [the sacrifice] he brought is acceptable, but he did not fulfill his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Because he did not bring the sacrifice he vowed to bring.</i><br>If he said: "I will bring these<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Pointing to cakes that he prepared to bake.</i> [baked] in a deep frying-pan and brings them [baked] on a flat frying-pan or promised to bring it [baked] on a flat frying-pan and brought it [baked] in a deep frying-pan," [the sacrifice] is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For he specified that the cakes be prepared in one way and they were not.</i> Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.<br>Similarly, if a person says: "I promise to bring two <i>esronim</i> in one vessel and he brings them in two vessels" or "...in two vessels" and he brings them in one vessel, [the sacrifice] he brought is acceptable, but he did not fulfill his obligation. [The rationale<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For all the above.</i>is that Deuteronomy 23:24] states: "As you vowed to G‑d."<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Implying that the vow must be fulfilled in all its particulars.</i>
If he said: "I will bring these cakes in one vessel" and he brought them in two or [promised] to bring them in two and brought them in one, they are unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If he can take a handful from each one separately, they are acceptable as indicated by the conclusion of Halachah 4.</i>If he did not specify what he would bring when he took the vow, but [merely] said: "I promise to bring two <i>esronim</i> and when he designated [the meal for the offering], he set [the flour] aside in two vessels and afterwards, brought it in one vessel, it is acceptable. For the verse states "as you vowed" and not "as you set aside."<br>When he said: "I promise to bring two <i>esronim</i> in one vessel," but he brought them in two, if despite being told that he vowed to bring them in one vessel, he offered them in two, they are not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Because he did not fulfill his vow. It is, however, necessary to ask him and have him respond as the Rambam states. Otherwise, we assume that he is not bringing this offering in fulfillment of his vow, but rather as a separate sacrifice. In that instance, although he would not have fulfilled his vow, the sacrifice would be acceptable.</i> If he brought them in one vessel, they are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since he offered them as he vowed, the fact that he originally brought them in two vessels is not significant.</i>
When he said: "I promise to bring two <i>esronim</i> in two vessels," but he brought them in one, if after being told that he vowed to bring them in two vessel, he offered them in two, they are acceptable. If he brought them in one vessel, they are like two meal-offerings that became mixed together.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In that instance, as stated in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 11:29, the law is that if one can take handfuls of each individually, they are acceptable. If not, they are not.</i>
If one says: "I promise to bring a meal-offering," he should bring one of the five types of meal-offerings that can be either vowed or pledged.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 4, for a description of these meal-offerings.</i> If he says: "I promise to bring meal-offerings," he should bring two<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since he used the plural, at least two offerings are required.</i> of the five types of meal-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that the word "types" is a printing error, because even if he brought two of the same type of meal-offering, he fulfilled his obligation. The Radbaz, however, initially explains that the Rambam's wording could be interpreted as being precise. Since the person said two meal-offerings, we can assume that he meant of two different types. Otherwise, he would have just vowed to bring one large meal-offering. Nevertheless, ultimately, the Radbaz rejects this interpretation and states that the Rambam's intent is "even of two types," i.e., he may bring two offerings either of one type or of two types.</i> If he says: "I promise to bring a type of meal-offerings," he should bring two<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, since he spoke of "meal-offerings," using the plural, he is required to bring two.</i> meal-offerings of one type.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For he said "a type," limiting him to only one type.</i> If he says: "I promise to bring types of meal-offerings," he should bring two meal-offerings [coming] from two types.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since he used the plural for both offerings and types, he should bring two offerings and they should be of two different types.</i> Similarly if he says: "...types of a meal-offering," he should bring two types of meal-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">One offering from each type, as in the previous clause. Hebrew grammar occasionally allows for a singular term to be used in a plural sense. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that this matter is debated by <i>Menachot</i> 105a and a ruling is not reached. Hence he questions how the Rambam can require him to bring a second offering: If it is not required, he will be bringing ordinary flour into the Temple Courtyard (which is forbidden). Hence he maintains that the person must make a stipulation when bringing this offering: "If I am obligated to bring it, this is to fulfill my vow. And if I am not obligated, it is a freewill offering."</i> If he specified that he would bring one type [of meal-offering] and forgot [which type he specified], he should bring all five types.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For in this way, he will certainly fulfill his vow.</i>
A private individual<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in contrast to the community at large. For there is no concept of a voluntary communal meal-offering and all the required communal meal-offerings have specific measures.</i> should not bring more than sixty <i>esronim</i> [of flour] in one vessel as a meal offering. If he vowed more than sixty, he should bring sixty in one vessel and the remainder in a second vessel.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by the following halachah, it appears to be preferable that he bring sixty in one vessel and the remainder in the other, rather than dividing the sum evenly between the two.</i><br>[The rationale is that] no more than sixty [<i>esronim</i> of flour] can be mixed together [with oil as one].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even though oil is always mixed with the flour at a ratio of one <i>log</i> to every <i>isaron</i> (Chapter 12, Halachah 7), nevertheless, if there is a very large quantity of flour, it will be difficult to get a proper mixture.</i> It is not an absolute requirement for [the flour and the oil] to be mixed together as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 13, Halachah 11.</i> Nevertheless, our Sages said:<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 18b; 103b.</i> "Whenever a [meal-offering] is fit to be mixed [with oil], it is not an absolute requirement for it to be mixed. Whenever it is not fit to be mixed [with oil],<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Because there is too large a quantity of flour.</i> mixing it is an absolute requirement."<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the meal and the oil could be mixed together, the fact that they were not mixed together is not significant, because there is nothing inherently lacking in the mixture. If, however, they could not be mixed together, there is an inherent difficulty with the mixture, therefore it is disqualified. Note the parallels to the declaration made with regard to the firsts fruits mentioned in <i>Bava Batra</i> 82a.</i>
If one says: "I promise to bring 121 <i>esronim</i> [as a meal offering]." He should bring 120 [<i>esronim</i>] in two vessels - 60 in each vessel - and one <i>isaron</i> in a third vessel.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he does not divide them into three equal portions.</i><br>If he said: "I promise to bring an <i>isaron</i>," he should bring one <i>isaron</i>. "I promise to bring <i>isaronim</i>," he should bring two. If he specified [the number of <i>esronim</i>] he vowed and then forgot how many he specified, he should bring 60 <i>esronim</i> in one vessel.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For an individual meal-offering is never more than 60 <i>esronim</i> and if he had promised a lesser amount, bringing more will not disqualify his offering (Radbaz).</i> If he forgot how many <i>esronim</i> he specified and which type [of meal-offering] he specified, he should bring 60 <i>esronim</i> of each of the five types [of meal-offerings].
If he specified his vow and forgot both how many <i>esronim</i> he vowed and the number of vessels in which he vowed to bring them, he should bring [the full range of] one to sixty <i>esronim</i> in sixty different vessels.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This is necessary, because as stated in Halachah 3, if a person vowed to bring two <i>esronim</i> in two vessels and he brought them in one, the offering is unacceptable. By bringing the full range of vessels from one to sixty, the person will certainly have included the entire number he vowed to bring. Any extra are considered as voluntary offerings.<br>The Radbaz notes that there is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in <i>Menachot</i> 13:2 and the opinion the Rambam quotes here is that of Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi. The Sages, however, differ and maintain that it is sufficient to bring one meal offering of 60 <i>esronim</i>. The Radbaz questions why the Rambam chooses to follow Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi's view, for it is a minority opinion. Moreover, he notes that in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, he explicitly states that the halachah does not follow this view. The Radbaz explains that since the Talmud (<i>Menachot</i> 106a) tries to justify other teachings according to Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi's view, we can assume that it is accepted as halachah.</i><br>What is implied? He should bring one <i>isaron</i> in the first vessel, two <i>esronim</i> in the second vessel, three in the third, until he brings 60 <i>esronim</i> in the last vessel. If he also forgot what type [of meal-offering] he designated, he should also brings [60 offerings] according to this pattern in 60 vessels of each type. Thus he will be bringing 1830 <i>esronim</i> from each type.
When a person says: "I promise to bring a meal-offering of barley,"<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Which is unacceptable, because as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 2, all the meal-offerings are brought from wheat except the meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i> and the <i>omer</i> offering. Those are obligatory offerings and cannot be vowed by a person.</i>"...a meal-offering of a half an <i>isaron</i>,"<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Which is also unacceptable, because a meal-offering may not be less than an <i>isaron</i> (Chapter 12, Halachah 5).</i> or "...a meal-offering without oil or frankincense,"<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Such an offering is also unacceptable, for oil and frankincense are absolute requirements (Chapter 12, Halachah 7).</i> he is exempt, because he did not vow an entity that is sacrificed.<br>If he said: "I promise to bring a meal-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between this and the previous clause depends on the precise Hebrew term used. If he said <i>minchat</i> ("meal-offering of"), as in the first clause he is not obligated at all, for the grammatical structure of the term is that of an adjective and the emphasis is on the words that follow. If, however, he used the term <i>minchah</i> ("meal-offering"), we assume that the fundamental intent of his vow was to bring a meal-offering. Since the specifics he mentioned were unacceptable, we ask him to clarify his intent. The Ra'avad does not accept this distinction, but the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain the Rambam's position.</i> of barley," "...of lentils,"<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Which is also unacceptable. Although <i>Menachot</i>103a debates whether a person could possibly err and think that a meal-offering from lentils is acceptable, from the resolution of that passage, it appears that such an error is plausible.</i> "...a meal-offering without oil or frankincense," or "...a meal-offering of a half an <i>isaron</i>," we ask him [what his intent was]. If he says: "I only took the vow because I thought it was permissible to offer such [sacrifices]. Had I known that one could only offer a complete <i>isaron</i> of fine [wheat] flour together with oil and frankincense, I would not have taken a vow," he is exempt. If, [however,] he said: "Had I known [that such offerings were unacceptable], I would have taken a vow to bring [an offering] like those that are offered," he is obligated to bring an offering like those that are offered.
If he vowed to bring an <i>isaron</i> and a half<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the conclusion of the clause and the explanations in the previous halachah, the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintain that this law applies only when the person said: "a meal-offering (<i>minchah</i>) of an <i>isaron</i> and a half."</i> and he said: "Had I known [that such offerings were unacceptable], I would have taken a vow to bring [an offering] like those that are offered," he must bring two [<i>esronim</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For he obviously desired to bring more than one <i>isaron</i>.</i> If he said: "I promise to bring coarse flour"<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 2, all of the meal-offerings are brought from <i>solet</i>, "fine flour," and not <i>kemach</i>, "coarse flour." This he is vowing to bring an entity that is never offered.</i> or "I promise to bring a half an <i>isaron</i>" without mentioning the term "meal-offering," he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs concerning this point and states that in this instance as well, he should be asked to clarify his intent, as mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> It as if he never took a vow at all.<br>Similarly, if one said: "I promise to bring a thanksgiving-offering without bread,"<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachah 5, which includes the bread as an integral part of the thanksgiving-offering.</i>or "...a sacrifice without its accompanying offerings,"<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2 which explains that every sacrifice is offered together with wine, meal, and oil.</i> he is exempt. If he said:<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as in the previous halachah, he is asked about his intent (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> "Were I to have known that such offerings are not sacrificed, I would have taken a vow to bring [an offering] like those that are offered," he is obligated to bring [an offering] like those that are offered.
When a person says: "I promise to bring the bread of a thanksgiving-offering," He must bring a thanksgiving offering and its bread. [The rationale is that] it is known that the bread is never offered without the thanksgiving-offering and he mentioned merely the conclusion of the sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that this was intent when making the vow. The Radbaz explains that it is not even necessary to ask him to clarify his intent, since he mentioned the thanksgiving-offering when making his vow, we take for granted that this was what he meant to say.</i> If he said: 'I promise to bring the bread to fulfill the obligation for so-and-so's thanksgiving offering," he should bring the bread for a thanksgiving offering together with the offering of his friend.
A person may vow or pledge to bring wine independently.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 14, Halachah 1; Chapter 16, Halachah 14.</i> One should not vow to bring a <i>log</i> of wine or two <i>lugim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 14.</i> for there are no libations that are [only] a <i>log</i> or two <i>lugim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 2, Halachah 4, the wine libations are 3, 4, or 6 <i>log</i>, depending on the animal offered.</i> Nor should one vow five <i>lugim</i>, for five <i>lugim</i> are not fit for the libations of one animal or for those of two animals.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For no two offerings will reach a total of five. See also Halachah 14.</i> One may, however, vow three, four, six, or more <i>lugim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For any number over six will be able to be broken up into multiples of 3,4, or 5, as stated in the following halachah [see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 12:4)].</i> because they are fit for the wine libations for sacrificial animals.
What is implied? If a person vows seven [<i>lugim</i>], they are considered as the libations for a sheep and for a ram.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The libations for a sheep are three <i>lugim</i> and those for a ram are four. We assume that the person desired to bring them both.</i> If he vowed eight, they are the libations for two rams; nine are the libations for an ox<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Six <i>lugim</i>.</i> and a sheep or those of three sheep. If he vowed ten, they are the libations for an ox and a ram or two sheep and a ram. Similarly, all numbers [can be seen as such combinations].
If he vowed to bring five <i>lugim</i>, we tell him: "Make it a complete six."<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Which are the libations for an ox.</i> [The rationale is that] he already established it as fit for a sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since libations of three and four <i>lugim</i> are brought, we assume that he did not want to make an empty statement. Hence, we ask him to increase the amount so that he will also be able to bring a valid offering. We do not reduce the amount, because there is an unresolved discussion in <i>Menachot</i> 104a if that is acceptable.</i>If, by contrast, he vowed one <i>log</i> or two, he is exempt, for this amount or their components are not fit to be used as a libation.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Based on the ruling in Halachah 9 with regard to a meal-offering of a half an <i>isaron</i>, the Radbaz states that if one says: "a wine libation of one <i>log</i>," he should be asked to clarify his intent.</i><br>Less than a <i>log</i> of oil should not be vowed or pledged, for there is no meal-offering smaller than an <i>isaron</i> and it requires a <i>log</i> of oil.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachot 5 and 7.</i>
When a person says: "I promise to bring wine," he should not bring less than three <i>lugim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 12.</i> "I promise to bring oil," he should not bring less than a <i>log</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See the previous halachah.</i> If he specified a given amount in his vow and forgot how many <i>lugim</i> of wine or oil he specified, he should bring 140 <i>lugim</i>. For there is no day on which there are more communal offerings sacrificed than on the first day of Sukkot that falls on the Sabbath. On that day, the accompanying offerings included 140 <i>lugim</i> of oil and an equal amount of wine as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachot 3, 14, for the details of the number and types of animals sacrificed on that day. The wine and oil brought as accompanying offerings for these sacrifices totaled 140 <i>lugim</i>. We assume that a person would not vow or pledge a larger amount. The Radbaz clarifies that this ruling applies to a very wealthy person who can be assumed to have made a generous vow. A person of ordinary means, by contrast, should be required to pay the largest amount he could conceive of having pledged (see <i>Hilchot Arachin</i> 2:8-10).</i>

Chapter 18

There is a positive commandment to offer all of the sacrifices - whether sacrifices of animals or fowl or meal-offerings - in [God's] chosen house,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 84) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 440) include this commandment as one of the Torah's 613 mitzvot.</i> as [Deuteronomy 12:14] states: "There you will perform everything that I command you."
