Similarly, it is a positive commandment for a person to take the effort to bring animal sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that this excludes sacrifices from fowl, but this understanding is not accepted by all authorities.</i> that he is obligated to bring<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Temurah</i> 21a states that the firstborn offering is not included in this commandment.</i> [and transport them] from the Diaspora to [God's] chosen house,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 85) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 453) include this commandment as one of the Torah's 613 mitzvot. The Ramban differs and maintains that this should not be considered as an independent mitzvah.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i>:26] states: "[Only] your sacraments that you possess and your vows shall you bear... [to the place that God will choose]." According to the Oral Tradition, we have learned that [the verse] is speaking only about sacrificial animals from the Diaspora which he takes the effort to deal with until he brings them to [God's] chosen house.
One who offers a sacrifice outside the Temple Courtyard negates a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The one stated in the previous halachah.</i> and violates a negative commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 90) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 186) include this prohibition as one of the Torah's 613 mitzvot. There are several historical dimensions to this prohibition. First of all, in the era between the destruction of the Sanctuary of Shiloh and the construction of the Temple, it was permitted to offer sacrifices on <i>bemot</i> (literally, "high-places"), i.e., individual altars. See the notes to <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 1:3 which explain the transition between these periods. It is also relevant with regard to the sanctuary constructed by Chonio, the son of Shimon the Just, described in the notes to <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:14.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i> :13] states: "Take heed lest you offer your burnt-offerings in any place that you see." If he offered a sacrifice [in such a place] willfully, he is liable for <i>karet</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, the soul's being cut off. This involves premature death in this world (before the age of 50, <i>Mo'ed Kattan</i> 28a) and the soul not meriting a portion in the world to come (<i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 8:1).</i> as [Leviticus 17:8-9] states: "[Any man]...who will offer a burnt-offering or a sacrifice, but did not bring it to the Tent of Meeting... he will be cut off from his people." [If he transgressed] unknowingly, he must bring a fixed<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This term is used to distinguish this sacrifice from the adjustable guilt offering. See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 1:4.</i> sin-offering.
Similarly, one who slaughters sacrificial animals outside [the Temple Courtyard], even though he does not offer them as a sacrifice, [is liable].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 89) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 439) include this prohibition as one of the Torah's 613 mitzvot.</i> If he acted willfully, he is liable for <i>karet</i>, as [<i>ibid</i>:3-4]: "[Any man]...who will slaughter an ox, a sheep, or a goat... it will be considered as [the shedding of] blood for that person. He has shed blood... He will be cut off." [If he transgressed] unknowingly, he must bring a fixed sin-offering.
Which source serves as a warning not to sacrifice outside [the Temple Courtyard]? [It is derived through] an association of verses. [Deuteronomy 12:14] states: "There will you offer your burnt-offerings" and it continues: "There you will perform everything that I command you." [We can conclude:] Just as [the Torah] warns explicitly against offering a sacrifice outside the Temple, and one incurs punishment for this, as it is written: "Take heed lest you offer your burnt-offerings...," so too, it has warned with regard to the "performance" that is involved in slaughtering, for which it is explicitly stated that one receives punishment. For the Torah does not prescribe punishment unless it has issued a warning.
A person who slaughters sacrificial animals outside [the Temple Courtyard] and offers them [in such a place] is liable twice: once for slaughtering and once for offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if he performed both transgressions without realizing the prohibitions involved in the interim, for they are two separate transgressions that are not dependent on each other (Radbaz).</i><br>If he slaughtered [a sacrificial animal] in the Temple Courtyard and offered it outside, he is liable for offering it. If he slaughtered [such an animal] outside, but offered it inside, he is liable for slaughtering it.
He is not liable unless he slaughtered sacrificial animals that are fit to be offered on the altar. If, however, he slaughtered an animal that was forbidden [to be offered on] the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the animals mentioned in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i>.</i> or one of the sin-offerings that was consigned to death<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 4:1 for a definition of this term.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, forbidden to do so.</i> [The rationale is that Leviticus 17:4<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The source for this prohibition, as stated in Halachah 3.</i>] mentions "before the Sanctuary of God." Whenever [an animal] is not fit to be come to the Sanctuary of God, one is not liable [for its slaughter].
If one slaughters an animal that is unacceptable because of a time factor involving its body<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> or the status of its owners<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 9.</i> outside the Temple Courtyard], he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, forbidden to do so.</i> [The rationale is that] in its present state, it is not fit to be brought into the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition involves slaughtering sacrificial animals and since these animals are not fit to be sacrificed or their owners are not fit to sacrifice them, they are not considered sacrificial animals in the full sense.</i>
What is meant by a disqualifying time factor involving its body? An animal in the seven days following its birth,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:8 which states that it is a positive commandment to offer sacrificial animals after eight days of life, but not before.</i> turtle-doves which have not reached the stage of development at which time [they are fit to be sacrificed],<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:2 which states that "Turtledoves are acceptable when [their feathers all] are of a golden hue." Before this stage, they are not acceptable (<i>ibid.</i>:9).</i> and an animal and its offspring; for if one slaughters one on a particular day, the other is not fit to be slaughtered until the morrow.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 12:1-2 which states that it is forbidden to sacrifice an animal and its offspring on the same day. No matter which is slaughtered first, one must wait until the following day to sacrifice the other.</i>
What is meant by a disqualifying time factor involving the status of its owners? A sacrifice whose owners have not reached the appropriate stage of time to offer it.<br>What is implied? If a <i>zav</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A person with a physical affliction somewhat similar to gonorrhea that renders one ritually impure and obligates him to bring a set of sacrifices (see <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:1, 3-4; 2:1).</i> a <i>zavah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A woman who bleeds for three consecutive days after the seven days associated with her menstrual period. This renders her ritually impure and obligates her to bring a set of sacrifices (see <i>ibid.</i> 1:1, 3-4,6)</i> and a woman who gave birth<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Who is ritually impure after giving birth and must bring a set of sacrifices (see <i>ibid.</i> 1:1, 3, 5).</i> slaughtered [the animal designated for] their sin-offerings outside [the Temple Courtyard] during the days of their counting,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the above sources, a <i>zav</i> and a <i>zavah</i> must wait seven "spotless days" after their condition ceases before bringing their sacrifices. A woman must wait 40 days after giving birth to a male and 80 days after giving birth to a female before bringing her sacrifices.</i> they are exempt. Similarly, if a person afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A skin affliction similar to, but not identical with leprosy that is a spiritual manifestation of the impurity resulting from improper speech (the conclusion of <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Tzara'at</i>). Such a person must bring a set of sacrifices when emerging from ritual impurity (see <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:1, 3-4).</i> slaughtered his sin-offering and[/or] his guilt-offering outside [the Temple Courtyard] during the days of his counting,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Such a person must also wait seven days after his condition ceases before bringing his sacrifices.</i> he is exempt, for the owners of these sacrifices are not yet fit for their atonement. Nevertheless, if these individuals slaughtered their burnt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices of all of these individuals include a burnt-offering and a sin-offering and the sacrifice of a person emerging from <i>tzara'at</i> also includes a guilt-offring.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard] during the days of their counting, they are liable. [The rationale is that] a burnt-offering is a present<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">To appease God and restore His favor.</i> and it is the sin-offering and the guilt-offering which are the fundamental [factors leading to] atonement.<br>Similarly, when a nazirite slaughters his sin-offering outside [the Temple Courtyard] during the days of his nazirite vow, he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nezirut</i>, ch. 8, for the details regarding the sacrifices a nazirite must bring upon completion of his nazirite vow. He may not bring these sacrifices beforehand.</i> If he offered his burnt-offering or his peace-offering outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable. For the sin-offering is what prevents him [from completing his nazirite vow] and it is the fundamental dimension [of the conclusion of] his nazirite vow.
When a person offers a conditional guilt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i>, Chapter 8, for a description of the situations which warrant bringing these sacrifices.</i> or sin-offering of fowl that is brought because of a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:7 which describes the situations under which such sacrifices should be brought.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is exempt. [The rationale is that] it was not definitely established that a prohibition [was violated].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to these sacrifices, it is possible that it will be discovered that the person definitely did sin. In such an instance, the sacrifice is not offered. If the sacrificial animal has not been slaughtered, it is left to pasture until it contracts a blemish. If it was slaughtered, its blood is poured down the drainage channel. Since there is a possibility of this taking place, the animal is not deemed a sacrificial animal in the full sense and one is not liable for slaughtering it or offering it.<br>The Ra'avad accepts the Rambam's ruling with regard to a sin-offering of fowl brought because of a doubt since only its blood is offered on the altar, but not its body. Hence, there is room to free one from the obligations involved with a sacrificial animal. Nevertheless, he argues, a conditional guilt-offering is offered on the altar. Hence one should be liable for slaughtering and offering it outside the Temple. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> question the Ra'avad's objection, noting that the Rambam's ruling has its basis in <i>Keritot</i> 18a.</i> When a guilt-offering for one afflicted by <i>tzara'at</i> was slaughtered outside [the Temple Courtyard], but not for the desired intent, [the one who slaughters it] is liable. [The rationale is that] since [when such a sacrifice is] not [slaughtered] for the desired intent in [the Temple Courtyard], it is deemed appropriate and acceptable, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 15:1, with the exception of a sin-offering or a Paschal sacrifice, whenever a sacrificial animal was slaughtered for the sake of an offering other the one for which it was intended, it is acceptable, but it does not fulfill the obligation of the owner (see also <i>ibid.</i>:20). Since the sacrifice would be acceptable if it was offered in the Temple, the person is liable for slaughtering it outside.</i><br>Whenever one is exempt for slaughtering a sacrificial [animal] outside the [Temple Courtyard], one is also exempt for offering it there.
[The following rules apply if] one slaughters the two goats offered on Yom Kippur<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The goat sent to Azazel and its pair whose blood is taken into the Holy of Holies. See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:1.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard]. Before [the High Priest] recites the confessional over them,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, the confessional is recited only over the one sent to Azazel (<i>ibid.</i> 4:2).<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that the matter is dependent not only the confessional, but on the lottery in which the goats are designated for their respective purposes. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain that the Rambam's ruling is based on <i>Zevachim</i> 113a. Although there are other interpretations of that passage, the Rambam has a foundation for his decision.</i> he is liable for both of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Or for either of them, were he to slaughter only one.</i> since [they are both] fit to come before God for the confessional. After [the High Priest] recited the confessional, one is exempt for slaughtering [the goat] that is sent [to Azazel], because it is no longer fit to come before God [as a sacrifice].
When one slaughters [animals designated as] peace-offerings outside [the Temple Courtyard], before the gates of the Temple building are opened, he is exempt, for a [necessary] deed is lacking. [Only] afterwards are they fit to be offered before God, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 5, which states that the gates to the Temple building must be open for the slaughter of peace offerings to be acceptable.</i><br>One who slaughters a Paschal sacrifice outside [the Temple Courtyard] - even during the other days of the year, whether for the sake of the Paschal sacrifice or for another purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes that this applies only during the remainder of the year. The rationale is that as explained below, during the remainder of the year, an animal designated as a Paschal sacrifice is considered as a peace-offering and a peace-offering is not disqualified if it is not offered for the desired intent. On the day preceding Pesach, when the Paschal sacrifice is offered, it is unacceptable if it is not offered for the desired intent. Hence on that date, were someone to slaughter an animal designated for this purpose for the sake of another sacrifice outside the Temple Courtyard, he would not be liable.</i> - is liable. [The rationale is that] during the remainder of the year, a Paschal sacrifice [that is offered] is considered as a peace-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 4:7. Since it is considered as a peace-offering, just as one is liable for slaughtering an animal designated as a peace-offering outside the Temple Courtyard, so too, he is liable for slaughtering such an animal.</i>
When the fetus [being carried by] an ordinary animal was consecrated [as a sacrifice for] the altar, it is forbidden to slaughter [the mother] outside [the Temple Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fetus it is carrying will later be offered as a sacrifice, it is forbidden to cause that sacrifice to be disqualified.</i> If he slaughtered it, he is not liable for lashes, because it<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Neither the mother nor the fetus.</i> is not fit to come before God [as a sacrifice].
When one stole [an animal] and consecrated it, and afterwards, slaughtered it outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the animal did not belong to him and hence, he had no right to consecrate it, our Sages considered it as his own so that he would be liable for <i>karet</i>. See <i>Gittin</i> 55b. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> raises questions on the Rambam's ruling based on that passage, but Rav Yosef Corcus resolves the Rambam's decision.</i> From which time was it considered in his property with regard to his being liable for <i>karet</i> for it? From the time he consecrated it.<br>[The above applies] provided he slaughtered it after [the owner] despaired of its return. [If he slaughtered it] before then, by contrast, the consecration is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For then it is still considered as belonging to the owner. Hence, the thief's consecration is not effective. See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:7 which speaks of the disqualification of a thief's offering. From <i>Hilchot Geneivah</i> 2:6, it appears that the thief must have also consecrated the animal after the owner's despair and not before. Otherwise, the consecration would not be effective.</i>
If the entire body of the animal was outside [the Temple Courtyard] and its neck was inside and one slaughtered it, he is liable,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Karet</i> or lashes for slaughtering the animal outside the Temple Courtyard.</i> as [Leviticus 17:3] states: "[Any man]...who will slaughter an ox, a sheep, or a goat in the camp or who will slaughter outside the camp... [and he has not brought it as an offering]." This applies whether the one who slaughters is standing in the Temple [Courtyard] and the animal's neck was inside, but the remainder of its body was outside, or its body was inside and its neck was outside. He is liable unless the animal was entirely within the Temple [Courtyard], as [implied by <i>ibid.</i>:9]: "And he will not bring it to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting."<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that <i>Zevachim</i> 107b uses this prooftext to teach the following concept: that one who slaughters on the roof of the Temple Building is exempt. Hence he suggests that a printing error crept into the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and that the text should be amended to fit the Talmud's teaching. This conception is not, however, shared by all authorities.</i> If, however, one slaughters on the roof of the Temple Building, even though it is not fit for sacrifice at all,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 4.</i> he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For he did not slaughter the animal outside the Temple Courtyard.</i>
Two people who held a knife and slaughtered [a sacrificial animal] outside [the Temple Courtyard] are exempt,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Note the contrast to Chapter 19, Halachah 12, with regard to offering an animal as a sacrifice.</i> for [the prooftexts] say "who will slaughter" "or who will slaughter"<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The verses use a singular form.</i> [implying] one and not two.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that although the Rambam's ruling has a source in <i>Zevachim</i> 108a and in the <i>Sifri</i>, his process of exegesis is different than that used in those sources.</i><br>When one slaughters a sacrificial animal outside [the Temple Courtyard] even though he had no intention of sacrificing this animal to God,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">But instead was slaughtering it for mundane purposes.</i> he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In this as well, there is a contrast to offering an animal as a sacrifice, as stated in Chapter 19, Halachah 1.</i> This is [implied by the prooftext]: "It will be considered as [the shedding of] blood for that person. He has shed blood."<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, the Rambam's method of exegesis is different from that of his apparent source, <i>Zevachim</i> 108b. Significantly, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 13:3), he cites the method of exegesis used by the Talmud.</i> [One can infer that] even if [the person slaughtering] thinks of the blood [from the sacrificial animal] as blood that was shed<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, "poured out."</i> and not as a sacrifice, he is liable.
A person who slaughters [a sacrificial animal] outside [the Temple Courtyard] at night is liable, since slaughtering an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an ordinary animal, not one designated as a sacrifice.</i> is acceptable at night. Similarly, if, at night, one offered as a sacrifice [an animal] he slaughtered at night outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable for offering it as a sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad maintains that the person is exempt in such a situation. The Radbaz justifies the Rambam's ruling, explaining that there is a fundamental difference between slaughtering an animal inside the Temple Courtyard and slaughtering it outside. When it is slaughtered inside the Temple Courtyard at night, it is disqualified as a sacrifice, because of it having been slaughtered at night and hence, it is not acceptable wherever it was offered. If, by contrast, it was slaughtered outside the Temple Courtyard, it becomes placed in the category of animals slaughtered in such a place. Hence the time when it is offered is not significant. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> also justifies the Rambam's approach.</i><br>If, however, one slaughtered [a sacrificial animal] inside [the Temple Courtyard] at night and offered it as a sacrifice outside,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Even during the following day (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> he is exempt. [The rationale is that] he offered merely an unacceptable article,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:6.</i> for there is no conception of acceptable slaughter in the Temple at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 1.</i> Similarly, if one received [the blood of a sacrificial animal] with an ordinary vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This disqualifies the sacrifice, for as stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 1, the blood of a sacrificial animal must be received in a sacred vessel.</i> inside [the Temple Courtyard], but poured it [on an altar] outside, he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For the animal was no longer acceptable as a sacrifice.</i>
Similarly, when a person performs <i>melikah</i> on a fowl outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">For the prooftext defining the prohibition (Leviticus 17:3  mentions slaughter, but not <i>melikah</i> (<i>Zevachim</i> 107a).</i> If he offered it [as a sacrifice there], he is exempt. If he performs <i>melikah</i> on a fowl inside [the Temple Courtyard], but offered it outside, he is liable for offering it.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is equivalent to slaughtering an animal inside the Temple Courtyard and offering it outside.</i><br>If one slaughtered [a fowl] in [the Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Thus disqualifying it, for in the Temple Courtyard, a fowl should be killed through <i>melikah</i> not ritual slaughter.</i> and offered it outside, he is exempt, for he offered something that is not fit to be offered. If he slaughtered the fowl outside [the Temple Courtyard] and offered it outside, he is liable twice,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Both for slaughtering and for offering.</i> because slaughtering a fowl outside [the Temple Courtyard] is acceptable. It is comparable to performing <i>melikah</i> inside.

Chapter 19

A person who offers a sacrifice outside [the Temple Courtyard] is not liable unless he brings [the sacrificial animal] to the top of the altar that he constructed outside [the Temple]. If, however, he offered it on a stone or a rock, he is exempt, for the term sacrifice applies only when [an animal is offered] on an altar, even if it is outside [the Temple], as [indicated by Genesis 8:20]: "And Noah built an altar." He is not liable unless he offers the sacrifice to God, as [Leviticus 17:9] states: "...to offer it to God," i.e., unless his intent is for God.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if he offers it for the sake of a person without designating him as a deity, but merely as a token of appreciation, he is not liable.</i>
One is liable only for offering an entity that is fit for the fire<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to an entity which is eaten, as stated in Halachah 4.</i> and for the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Excluding sacrifices that are not offered on the altar, as mentioned in Halachah 7.</i> for example, a burnt offering, as [<i>ibid.</i>:8] states: "who will offer a burnt-offering or a sacrifice." [One may infer:] Just as a burnt-offering is fit to be offered on the fires, so too, everything that is fit to be offered on the fire is what one is liable for offering outside [the Temple Courtyard].
On this basis, [our Sages] said that individuals [who perform the following services] outside [the Temple Courtyard] are liable: One who throws the blood, offers on the pyre the limbs of a burnt-offering, the parts of an animal offered on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This applies with regard to all sacrifices, even sacrifices of a lesser order of sanctity. Since these parts are offered on the altar in the Temple, one is liable for offering them outside the Temple (Radbaz).</i> a handful [of meal], or frankincense,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Both the handful of meal taken from the meal-offering and the frankincense from that offering are offered on the altar in their entirety (Chapter 13, Halachah 12).</i> or incense,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is offered on the inner altar twice daily (<i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 2:11).</i> the meal-offering of a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Which is offered on the altar in its entirety (Chapter 12, Halachah 9).</i> or the meal-offering within the accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1.</i> or one who pours a libation of three <i>lugim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For a wine libation is never less than three <i>lugim</i>. See the following halachah.</i> of wine or of water.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintain that one is liable for pouring water on an altar only during the holiday of Sukkos, for only then is water offered on the altar in the Temple. The Radbaz does clarify that this is not necessarily apparent from the Rambam's wording. Indeed, on the contrary, from the following halachah, one could infer the opposite.</i> [This is derived from <i>ibid.</i>:9:] "He did not bring it [to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting] to offer it." [Implied is that] any [sacrifice] that would be accepted within [the Temple Courtyard] causes one to be liable for [offering] it outside.
If, however, one throws the remainder of the blood [of a sacrificial animal] - even the remainder of the blood [from a sin-offering whose blood was offered] inside [the Sanctuary],<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the sin-offerings that were burnt. See Chapter 5, Halachah 11.</i> he is exempt. [The rationale is that] throwing the blood on the altar is the remaining aspect of the mitzvah and is not an absolute necessity.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fundamental aspect of pouring the blood is the sprinkling of the blood on the altar - or in the Temple Building - each sacrifice according to its laws. Pouring out the remainder of the blood on the base of the altar is not of fundamental importance and the sacrifices are acceptable even if it is not performed. Hence, it is not considered as an act of significance for which one is liable.</i><br>Similarly, one who pours a libation of less than three <i>lugim</i> of wine or water outside [the Temple Courtyard] is exempt, whether during Sukkot<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">When the water libation is offered. See the notes to the previous halachah.</i> or throughout the year. Since the required measure is lacking,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For three <i>lugim</i> is the smallest wine libation offered (see Chapter 17, Halachot 12, 15). Similarly, the water libation offered on Sukkot is three <i>lugim</i> (<i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 10:7).</i> they are not fit to be accepted within [the Temple]. Similarly, one who offers from the meat of a sin-offering, that of a guilt-offering, or that of a peace-offering whether of an individual or of the community or from the remainder of the meal-offerings, the two breads [offered on Shavuot], or the showbread outside [the Temple Courtyard] is exempt. [The rationale is that] all of these are fit to be eaten, not for the fires [of the altar].
One who offers an entire animal outside [the Temple Courtyard] is liable, because of the portions offered on the altar. Even though they have not been separated, the meat of the sacrifice is not considered as an intervening substance<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 13:5), the Rambam explains that the rationale is that we follow the principles that two entities that are the same substance are never considered as intervening substances.</i> and it is as if he offered those portions on the pyre alone. In contrast, if one offers a meal-offering from which a handful [of meal] has not been separated, he is exempt. [Even though he would have been liable for the handful], the handful is not a distinct and discrete entity. If he separated [the handful] and then it was mixed back into it and he then offered the entire [measure] outside the Temple Courtyard, he is liable.
One who pours oil [over a meal-offering], mixes the meal and oil, breaks up the wafers, salts them, waves them, approaches an altar with them, arranges a table for showbread, cleans the lamps of a candelabra, separates a handful [of meal], or receives the blood [of a sacrificial animal] outside [the Temple Courtyard] is exempt. [The rationale is that] all of these are not activities that complete the offering [of the sacrifice] and [the prooftext] says: "Who will offer a burnt-offering or a sacrifice." [One may infer:] Just as offering [these sacrifices] is the final stage of the service [involved with them], so too, one is liable only for activities that are the final stage of sacrificial service.
When one burns a red heifer outside the place where it is required to be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The red heifer should be slaughtered on the Mount of Olives, as stated in <i>Hilchot Parah Adumah</i> 3:1-2.</i> or if one offers, outside [the Temple Courtyard], the goat that is sent [to Azazel]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 3:7, the goat sent to Azazel was pushed off a mountain cliff in the desert outside of Jerusalem.</i> after the confession was recited over it,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 18, Halachah 11.</i> he is exempt. [The rationale is that the prooftext] says: "He did not bring it to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting." [One may infer:] One is not liable for any sacrifice which is not fit to be brought to the entrance to the Tent of Meeting.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the Temple. This excludes offerings like the above which, though they are consecrated, are not offered in the Temple.</i><br>In contrast, one is liable for offering [outside the Temple Courtyard] sacrificial animals that were disqualified, if they were disqualified in the Temple. What is implied? [Sacrificial meat or blood] that remained overnight [without being offered], they were taken out [of the Temple Courtyard], they became impure, or they were disqualified because of the intent of the person sacrificing them, all are required to be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Radbaz notes that <i>Zevachim</i> 109a mentions sacrifices disqualified for other reasons. He explains that the Rambam does not mention them here, because here he is speaking in general terms. They are detailed in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> where he discusses the particulars pertaining to these laws.</i> as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukkdashim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i>, ch. 19, which mentions the obligation to burn sacrifices that were disqualified for all these reasons.</i> If a person transgressed and offered [such entities] as sacrifices outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable. [This is derived from the prooftext]: "...to offer it to God." One is liable for any [entity] that is are fit to be offered to God and these are fit to be offered to God.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i>, ch. 3, if sacrificial meat or blood was brought to the top of the altar after being disqualified for these reasons, it should be offered on the altar's pyre.</i>
Whenever there is a substance for which one is liable for offering it outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is liable for offering an olive-sized portion of it outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Any lesser amount is not considered significant. See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 14:10.</i> [This applies] whether he offered [a portion of the entity] inside [the Temple Courtyard] first, left over an olive-sized portion and then offered it outside or left the entire entity inside and took an olive-sized portion and offered it outside. If, however, [the size of] the sacrificial entity was decreased in the slightest way<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a portion of it was lost or burnt.</i> inside the Temple Courtyard and then the remainder was offered outside, he is exempt.
What is implied? If a portion of the handful [of meal offered on the altar], the frankincense, the portions of a sacrifice offered on the altar, a burnt-offering, a meal offering that is burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a meal offering brought by a male priest, the meal offering of the High Priest, and the meal component of the accompanying offerings (<i>Zevachim</i> 13:4).</i> and the wine libations was decreased within [the Temple Courtyard] and the remainder was offered outside [the Temple Courtyard], he is exempt. [This is derived from the prooftext which states:] "to offer it." [Implied is that] he is liable for a complete entity, but he is not liable if it is lacking.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the prooftext states "it," and this implies that the entity must be complete.</i> If one removed [the sacrificial entity] from the Temple Courtyard while it was complete, its [size] was decreased outside [the Temple Courtyard] and then he offered it [there], there is an unresolved question [whether he is liable]. Therefore [a transgressor] is not given lashes.
If, [outside the Temple Courtyard,] one offered a limb that did not have an olive-sized portion of meat on it, but the bone itself caused it to reach the olive-sized measure, he is liable, because the meat is connected to the bone. If salt caused [the sacrificial entity] to reach the olive-sized measure, there is an unresolved question [whether he is liable].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Note a similar ruling in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 3:10.</i> Therefore [a transgressor] is not given lashes. A burnt-offering and the portions of the innards of a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">We have translated the text according to its straightforward meaning. Nevertheless, the Radbaz states that this ruling applies, not only to a burnt-offering and its own innards, but even one that is combined with the innards of another sacrifice. Thus he maintains that one is liable for combining the meat of a burnt-offering, not only with the innards of a burnt-offering, but also with the innards of a peace-offering.</i> that are offered on the altar, can be combined to complete an olive-sized portion [to cause one to be liable].<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For they are part of the same type of offering and are both offered on the altar in their entirety.</i>
If one offered [a portion of a sacrifice outside the Temple Courtyard] and then offered another portion of it, he is liable for every individual limb.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Provided he was notified of the transgression between the offering of each particular limb (Radbaz, based on <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 6:2).</i> If he sprinkled its blood [outside the Temple Courtyard] and then offered its limbs, he is liable twice. For the Torah made a distinction between [offering blood and offering limbs as indicated by the two prooftexts] "Who will offer a burnt-offering" and "to offer it."<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that the Torah uses two prooftexts implies that two different prohibitions are involved. The prooftext "to offer it" refers to both the prohibitions against slaughter and against sprinkling the blood and the prooftext "who will offer it" refers to the prohibition against offering the limbs on the altar (Radbaz and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, thus resolving the questions raised by the Ra'avad).</i><br>If one offered a limb that was lacking [in substance], he is exempt,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to this ruling, noting that the previous halachah stated that a person is liable if the combination of a portion of a limb and the portions of the innards offered on the altar equal an olive-sized portion. This indicates that a limb need not be whole. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others, however, justify the Rambam's ruling.</i> as [one can infer from the prooftext] "to offer it." [This indicates] that one is liable [only] for a complete [limb].
When two people slaughter [a sacrificial animal outside the Temple Courtyard], they are exempt.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This law has already been stated in Chapter 18, Halachah 16. Indeed, there are some who considered its inclusion here as a scribal error.</i> If two people hold a limb [from a sacrificial animal] and offer it outside the Temple Courtyard], they are liable. [The rationale is that the prooftext states] "Every man<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The original Hebrew repeats the word <i>ish</i> meaning man, implying that even two men can be held liable for the same activity.</i> who will offer a burnt-offering." Implied is that even two people who offer [a sacrifice] are liable.
If a person makes several of the required sprinklings [of blood] outside [their appropriate place], he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he did not complete the required service associated with the sacrifice, as long as he sprinkled the blood on an altar once, he is liable.</i> A person who receives the blood of a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is quoting the wording of the mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i>13:6). Nevertheless, according to his understanding, this law applies to the blood of other sacrifices as well.</i> in one cup and applies it to an altar outside [the Temple Courtyard] and then applies it to the altar inside [the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">He is liable even if he does not apply the blood to the altar inside. The Rambam mentions the application of the blood inside only to emphasize that offering the blood properly does not remove the liability that was already established.</i> he is liable for the portion applied outside [the Temple Courtyard]. [The rationale is that] the entire amount was fit to be offered inside.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Hence if he offers any of it outside first, he is liable. The fact that he does not offer the entire amount outside is not significant. As long as a portion is offered outside, he is liable.</i><br>If he applied it to [the altar] inside and then applied it outside, he is exempt, for [the blood he used] was merely remnants.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the offering was completed through the application of the blood to the altar inside. Although the remainder of the blood should also have been dashed on the altar, that is not an absolute requirement. Hence one is not liable for offering such blood outside the Temple Courtyard.</i> If, however, he received the blood in two cups, he is liable whether he applied both of them outside [the Temple Courtyard], [the first] outside and the other inside, or [the first] inside and the other outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad takes issue with the Rambam regarding the latter point, explaining that <i>Zevachim</i> 112a states that one is exempt in the latter instance. Since the blood was first offered inside, the sacrifice is acceptable and the fact that later blood was also offered outside is not of consequence. The Radbaz explains that, according to the Rambam, that rationale applies when the blood was offered inside according to all of its specifications. In this instance, however, the Rambam is speaking about a situation where the applications of the blood to the Temple altar were not completed. Hence, the blood in the second cup is still significant.</i>
When one offered a handful [of meal] or the frankincense from the meal offering<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachot 12-13, which describe the offering of the meal-offering.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard], or offered one inside and the other outside, he is liable. Similarly, with regard to the two bowls of frankincense from the showbread,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 5:2 for a description of the offering of the showbread.</i> if one offered [the first] outside the [Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the offering is not complete until both bowls of frankincense are offered (<i>ibid.</i>:3), one is liable for offering even one of them outside.</i> or [the first] inside and the second outside, he is liable.
If a person slaughtered consecrated animals in the present era and offered them outside the Temple Courtyard, he is liable, because [the sacrifices] are fit to be offered inside. For it is permitted to offer sacrifices even though the Temple is not built, because [when the Temple was] consecrated originally, it was consecrated for the immediate time and for all future time.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 6:15-16 for an explanation of these concepts. See also <i>ibid.</i> 2:4 which states that as long as the altar is built in its appropriate place sacrifices may be offered even though the Temple is destroyed. Based on <i>Zevachim</i> 59a, the Radbaz states that even if the altar is not built, sacrifices can be offered on its site. Indeed, he writes that it is only because the gentiles do not allow us that we do not offer communal sacrifices in the present age. (Communal sacrifices may be offered while ritually impure.)<br>Based on this rationale, after the conquest of Jerusalem in 5727 (1967), the Lubavitcher Rebbe advised his chassidim to leave the holy city on the day before Pesach. The rationale is that the Paschal sacrifice may also be brought while ritually impure. Now anyone who is close to Jerusalem on the day before Pesach and does not bring a Paschal sacrifice is liable for <i>karet</i>. Although many factors are involved and the Rebbe did not advise his followers to actually bring a sacrifice, he felt it necessary that precautions be taken so that they would not be held liable for not bringing the offering. This situation persisted for several years until the Rebbe felt that the Jewish control of the Temple Mount was weakened to the point that it would be impossible to bring an offering.</i>
[A Jew] who slaughters sacrificial animals belonging to a gentile outside [the Temple Courtyard] is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For slaughtering these animals outside the Temple Courtyard. As the Rambam proceeds to explain, this is speaking about a situation where the gentile desires to offer the sacrifice to God.</i> Similarly, one who offers them outside [the Temple Courtyard is liable].<br>Gentiles are permitted to offer burnt offerings to God in all places,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 116b notes that the passage prohibiting the slaughter of sacrificial animals outside the Temple Courtyard begins: "Speak to the children of Israel," implying that the prohibition applies only to them.</i> provided they sacrifice them on a raised structure that they build.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This license applies in the present era as well.</i> It is forbidden to help them [offer these sacrifices] or act as agents for them, for we are forbidden to sacrifice outside [the Temple Courtyard]. It is permitted to instruct them and teach them how to sacrifice to the Almighty, blessed be He.
